## MODULE W2.3: ULTRA-HIGH-NET-WORTH CLIENTS

Video covering this content is available online.

LOS W2.c: Describe the unique characteristics of ultra-high-net-worth individuals and how these characteristics distinguish them from other private wealth management clients.

## Ultra-High-Net-Worth Clients

Aside from the level of their wealth, ultra-high-net-worth (UHNW) clients have five common characteristics that often distinguish them from other wealthy clients:

1. UHNW clients have more in common with institutional clients than private clients.
2. They often have multi-jurisdictional holdings.
3. They have a longer time horizon, focusing on wealth transfer and succession planning.
4. They often have an entrepreneurial approach to risk.
5. They have a greater need to collaborate with a wide range of specialists and service providers.

Typical service providers include commercial bankers, investment bankers, tax accountants, attorneys, custodians, financial managers, philanthropic advisors, and wealth advisors.

## Family Offices

Coordinating a UHNW family's interests is not unlike managing an organization. It requires structure, defined roles, specialist resources, and planning. Family offices are often created to fulfill this integrated approach for UHNW clients.

Features of family offices include the following:

- Integration. A family office is a dedicated, integrated private wealth advisory firm looking after family and business interests for UHNW clients.
- Holistic view. A family office focuses on the family's interests and priorities in both financial and nonfinancial matters.
- Privacy. Family offices access multiple services under one organizational structure, making it easier to maintain confidentiality and privacy within the family.
- Family engagement. A family office provides a forum for family members to discuss family and business interests.
- Role of wealth managers. Wealth managers fill multiple roles in a family office. They are educators, mediators, negotiators, and trusted family advisors.

## Family Office Models

Family offices have evolved into two distinct models to support the size and scope of a family's wealth management interests: (1) the single-family office and (2) the multifamily office. With this, the single-family office has two variations.

The first variation is an embedded family office (EFO). As the name suggests, an EFO is embedded within a family business. A small team within the family business is responsible for managing the family's financial and legal matters.

There are advantages:

- Convenience. Owners of a family business can use existing employees to work on both business and family matters.
- Family and business interests are interconnected. Often, it makes sense for the same team to handle both. An example is taxation, where the owners' business interests affect their personal taxation.

There are also disadvantages:

- EFOs do not manage assets outside the family business. EFOs focus on business assets rather than the wider assets held by the family. As a result, EFOs are not a holistic approach to wealth planning.

The second variation is a virtual family office (VFO). With a VFO, this is a separate legal organization from a family business. The VFO has a small team of staff to support the family and outsources any needed specialist services.

There are advantages:

- Lower costs. A smaller team reduces operating costs significantly compared to an embedded family office.
- Holistic approach. A VPO encompasses all the family's interests, not just the family business.

There are also disadvantages:

- Outsourcing specialists. Relying on outsourcing specialist services makes it more challenging to integrate and personalize services to the family.
- Minimum net worth. A relatively high level of client assets is required to justify this approach, usually between USD 20 million and USD 100 million.

Single-family offices can also be categorized by the source of wealth and by the generation currently managing the wealth, as Figure W2.3 shows.

39
Figure W2.3: Single-Family Office Models

| | Still Own and Control the Business | No Longer Own and Control the Business |
| --- | --- | --- |
| First Generation | Optimizer single-family office Wealth is created by the current generation, often through a family business venture. The family has entrepreneurial objectives to build the business rather than focusing on transferring wealth to the next generation. | Founder single-family office The founder has sold all or part of the family business they created. Family wealth is now held in financial assets and is usually controlled by the founder who created the business. Often, the family's focus is less on asset accumulation and more on wealth transfer. |
| Second Generation | Preserver single-family office Wealth has been owned for at least two generations. The family's focus is to balance growing the business with planning for wealth transfer to the next generation. The family has likely already experienced a succession event, so they are aware of the need for effective planning. | Entrepreneurial single-family office The proceeds from a family business are inherited by a younger generation. Wealth is held in financial assets as well as in property and possessions. The family has experienced a succession event, and they have a greater focus on asset growth than asset preservation. |

# Multifamily Office

The second form of a family office is a multifamily office (MFO) that serves multiple wealthy families. MFOs offer economies of scale, a wider range of services, and centralized data management. The larger scale of operations provides access to a greater choice of investment managers and associated trading and research services. However, being one of many clients reduces the personalization of the services provided and the service levels achieved.

Other important features of MFOs include the following:

- For-profit entities. The goals of the MFO are unlikely to align perfectly with each individual family, considering a typical MFO has over 100 clients.
- Cost reduction. MFOs benefit from economies of scale and focus on cost reduction.
- Collective experience. Individual families benefit from the collective experience gained within the MFO. The MFO wealth managers are highly experienced from managing a diverse range of wealthy clients.

# Professional Family Office

A professional family office (PFO), also known as an institutional family office, is a type of multifamily office backed by an institution. The backing of the institution provides wealthy clients access to a wide range of services from the institution.

# Working With UHNW Clients

The UHNW Institute examined the range of services needed by wealthy families. Based on this analysis, the institute developed the Ten Domains of Family Wealth:
1. Financial and investment management. Investment management services are required across a range of asset classes including financial assets, business assets, real estate, and collectibles. UHNW families often have multi-jurisdictional assets requiring specialist tax planning services.

2. Estate planning and legal issues. Estate planning and wealth transfer is a primary concern for UHNW families. The scale of the family's assets and business interests requires specialist estate planning advice, including succession planning.

3. Social impact and philanthropy. In addition to objectives such as asset accumulation and wealth preservation, wealthy families may have charitable interests and objectives. Family foundations enable families to use their wealth to improve the lives of others.

4. Risk management. Risk management is far broader in scope for UHNW clients. In addition to financial assets, risk management must consider high-value residences, possessions, collections, and family security, as well as fraud, theft, and cyber risks.

5. Governance and decision-making. This describes how a family manages its business and family interests. Governance includes the family's decision-making structures, processes, and norms. Governance is covered in more detail later in this reading.

6. Leadership and transition planning. Every family has its leaders and leadership style. The decision as to when, and how, to hand over leadership to the next generation is challenging. The next generation will need support to ensure the continuity of leadership across time.

7. Learning, development, and the younger generation. Succession plans across family and business interests require educating the younger generation in family and business matters.

8. Family dynamics. Every family has its own culture, shared values, communication preferences, and approaches to decision-making. Individual family members may or may not be aligned in their views of family and business matters.

9. Health and well-being. UHNW families may also require the integration of a range of nonfinancial services such as child care, medical, travel, logistics, home maintenance, and security services. This is known as a **full-service wealth management** or an **integrated wealth management** approach.

10. Family advisory relationships. UHNW families often use single-family or multifamily offices to coordinate their family and business interests and provide access to the specialists needed.

## Selecting a Wealth Manager

Wealthy families often prefer to engage wealth managers who are known to them or recommended by their social circle or business network. UHNW clients look at the following factors when selecting a wealth manager:

- Empathy. This is the ability of a wealth manager to understand and align with the client's values, beliefs, and attitudes toward their goals and objectives. Empathy is an important factor in building trust with clients.
- Objectivity. This is the ability of a wealth manager to act in the best interests of the family. This can be challenging for wealth managers whose income may include commissions and bonuses linked to sales and services provided to the family.
- Expertise. Because UHNW families vary in their existing levels of knowledge and expertise, the wealth manager needs to be able to add value to the family in the specific services required. Wealth managers need to be able to access various specialist services to support the family.
- Privacy. The wealth manager must maintain the confidentiality and privacy of family information and family business interests.
- Creativity and collaboration. Every family has challenges to face and problems to solve. Wealth managers need to be creative in their approach to solving their client's problems, rather than being limited to standard solutions.
- Confidence. Wealth managers need confidence and sensitivity to address the challenges facing wealthy families. Navigating family dynamics is often challenging, and confidence grows with experience.

## EXAMPLE: Case Study Part 3'Vaino and Tarja Korhonen

After two meetings with Vaino and Tarja, Emppu Nieminen has a clear picture of the family's financial and business circumstances, goals and objectives, and family values. Nieminen explains that the family is classified as UHNW within his firm due to the size of their overall wealth and the wider range of services they require.

Nieminen explores the concept of single-family offices with the family. Vaino and Tarja agree that greater coordination of their business and family interests would be beneficial, as would access to specialists.

Based on this and the information from the earlier examples, recommend the most appropriate single-family office for the clients. Justify your recommendation with two reasons.

## Answer:

You should recommend a virtual family office (VFO). This will better coordinate the family's business and personal financial matters, which is even more important with the upcoming inheritance. The VFO will have the resources needed to cope with this increased scale of wealth management services.

Secondly, the VFO connects the family to the wealth services and specialists that they require across their business and family financial matters.

Arthur Allinson started work in his grandfather's family bakery business straight from school. Now in its fourth generation, Allinson's Bakery has grown into a national enterprise. Arthur's goal is to continue to build his family's legacy, but to leave the
business in the capable hands of his daughters.

Andrea runs the office side of the bakery business. The team that reports to her manages the financial affairs of the business as well as the family's financial matters. Andrea finds this beneficial due to the interconnected nature of family and business matters, especially regarding taxation.

Arthur, Andrea, and Alice meet with Jessica Page, an experienced wealth advisor who is known by the family from their social circle. Although Page has plenty of experience with wealthy clients, she has no experience with ultra-high-net-worth clients.

## MODULE W2.4: FAMILY GOVERNANCE

LOS W2.d: Recommend appropriate approaches to the development, implementation, adherence, and amendment of a common, long-term framework for joint family decision making.

Video covering this content is available online.

## Family Governance

Wealthy families are often compared to organizations and businesses in the structures they need to function effectively. Family governance, like running a business, requires a leadership team, clear communication, and effective decision-making.

Seven principles of good family governance are as follows:

1. Competent decision makers. Family members must be capable of performing the required roles in making family decisions.
2. Objective decisions. Family leaders aim to make the best decisions for the good of the family. Balancing the views of individual family members and of different generations is often challenging.
3. Working together. Governance structures facilitate collaboration and communication between family members, especially between different generations of the family.
4. Trust and integrity. Good governance requires that those making decisions act honestly and in the best interests of the family.

43
5. Transparency: Wealth managers may encounter difficulty in being accepted and trusted with certain family matters. Families may be reluctant to share certain sensitive information within and outside the family.

6. Independent oversight. Specialist service providers allow families access to independent, objective advice across legal, tax, and accounting matters. The use of trustees, custodians, and depositaries is important for the independent safekeeping of family assets.

7. Fairness. Treat all family members fairly including those with opposing views, those who are less able, and future generations.

## Family Governance Structures

Continuing the comparison with organizations and corporations, wealthy families use similar structures to coordinate and organize their wealth management activities. Commonly used structures and processes include the following:

- A family mission statement details the family's aspirations and shared values.
- A family constitution is a written agreement between family members detailing how a family makes decisions about their financial, family, and business matters. The document needs to be detailed enough to provide clarity in the day-to-day management of family wealth and the agreed transition plans to future generations. Provisions for resolutions of disputes, codes of conduct, and the family's charitable goals are also included.
- A family council represents the family in certain family matters (e.g., relating to the family business). With small families, all family members will participate.
- A family advisory board comprises both family and nonfamily members and is used to provide strategic guidance to the family. Including nonfamily members, such as members of other wealthy families and external advisors, adds valuable insights when making strategic decisions.
- A family foundation allocates roles and responsibilities to achieve the family's charitable goals and objectives.

## Creating a Family Constitution

A family constitution is a living document to represent the family's values, goals, and objectives. Aside from detailing what the family wants to achieve, it details how they intend to achieve this by specifying the chosen structures and the family code of conduct.

There are five steps to create a family constitution:

1. Input from each family member. All family members should have a say in describing the current family decision-making processes and sharing suggestions to change and improve them.
2. Engagement and support. The more engaged family members are in the process, the greater the benefits are to the family of the constitution. This requires a high level of transparency and fairness of family matters and business interests.

44
3. Strengths and weaknesses. A written summary captures the family's current informal decision-making processes. Feedback from each family member identifies strengths and weaknesses in the current approach.
4. Family meeting. The family discusses the feedback from each family member, and they agree on a way forward.
5. Commitment to the constitution. If a family chooses to create a constitution, all family members must have an active role in the writing process, to reflect their joint commitment.

## EXAMPLE: Case Study Part 4'Vaino and Tarja Korhonen

Three months later, the Korhonen family collaborates with Emppu Nieminen to create their own family constitution.

Emppu Nieminen calls a meeting with Vaino, Tarja, and all five children. The family discusses what they want from their family constitution, which results in heated discussions about the younger generation being more involved in family matters.

The family identifies that three of the children have plenty of financial experience and are confident in making financial decisions. The other two children have little interest in financial matters and do not want active involvement in governance structures.

The family also expresses the important criteria to keep family matters completely confidential.

Recommend an appropriate governance structure to help the family's decision-making while keeping full confidentiality within the family.

## Answer:

Family council. A family council acts as representatives of the wider family in specified decisions. Three of the children have plenty of financial experience and are confident in making financial decisions.

If the family members agree, a family council can be created to represent the interests of all family members. Representatives from all the generations of the family should be included in the family council.

## Attitudes to Money

The field of psychology has studied various attitudes to money and how this is shaped by childhood experiences and life events. The money scripts framework identifies four attitudes toward money (called money scripts). Each money script acts as an unconscious bias that influences an individual's relationship with money and the decisions they make.

45
Figure W2.4: Money Scripts

| Money Script | Description |
| --- | --- |
| Money worship | This is the belief that happiness is measured by an individual’s level of wealth. |
| Money status | This is the belief that wealth indicates an individual’s status in life. Status (money) indicates power, intelligence, and personal character. |
| Money avoidance | This is the belief that wealth brings bad outcomes. Individuals are ambivalent to money and may be fearful of being obsessed with it. |
| Money vigilant | This is the belief that one should be careful and discreet with money. Individuals prefer that others are unaware of their wealth. |

## Working With the Wealthy: Conclusions

Every wealthy family operates in its own unique way, often blending family and business interests. The family is at the heart of all discussions, often adding nonfinancial dimensions to important decisions.

Wealthy families require a greater level of structure and organization to connect them to the specialists they require. A private wealth manager must focus on building empathy and trust in their client relationships.

Kevin Jones, a private wealth manager, is planning for a second meeting with Edward and Jennifer Carrington, who are owners of Carrington Jewelry as well as parents of three children and five grandchildren. Jones identified the following details at the first meeting:

- The Carringtons are ultra-high-net-worth clients with total assets of $35 million.
- The clients are interested in formalizing the way family decisions are made and would like to include the wider family in decision-making moving forward.

## KEY CONCEPTS

### LOS W2.a

Family and human dynamics is the study of how families operate, including their norms, roles and responsibilities, forms of communication, rituals, and how they manage change.

Socioemotional wealth (SEW) suggests that family businessowners are often motivated by social and emotional goals connected to the family, rather than profit.

The FIBER scale identifies five SEW goals: (1) family control, (2) identification, (3) binding social ties, (4) emotional attachment, and (5) renewal of family bonds.

Hofstede's framework of six cultural dimensions includes the following: (1) power distance index, (2) individualism vs. collectivism, (3) masculinity vs. femininity, (4) uncertainty avoidance index, (5) long-term vs. short-term orientation, and (6) indulgence vs. restraint.

Framing, anchoring, and the disposition effect are examples of supposedly irrelevant factors (SIFs), or biases, that affect an individual's decision-making.

Humans use heuristics and shortcuts to solve complex problems, which may result in biases such as availability bias and hindsight bias.

Cognitive biases are errors made in the decision-making process such as the endowment effect, confirmation bias, and framing.

Emotional biases result from an individual's beliefs, emotions, and life experiences.

Wealth identity is an individual's relationship with wealth and how it integrates into their life. There are four stages: (1) honeymoon phase, (2) wealth acceptance, (3) identity consolidation, and (4) achieving balance.

### LOS W2.b

Wealthy clients are segmented by the level of assets, generational cohort, and means of acquisition.

The inheritor's dilemma suggests that inheriting wealth may have strings attached, such as parental control, distrust of others, anxiety, and impact on career.

Cognition-based trust ("head trust") is an evidence-based judgment of competence. Affect-based trust ("heart trust") is an emotional bond between individuals.

In relationships with clients, empathy is the factor most likely to lead to trust.

Values are beliefs that drive human behavior. Values cards are tools used to identify a client's most important values. Challenges of values analysis include (1) overload bias, (2) social desirability, and (3) polysemy.

47
Effective advisor-client communication strategies include (1) listening skills; (2) rating scales; (3) "what if" questions; (4) past, present, and future discussions; (5) clear goals; (6) involving others; (7) impact of change; (8) common ground; and (9) priorities.

## LOS W2.c

UHNW clients are defined by five common characteristics: (1) similar to institutions, (2) multi-jurisdictional holdings, (3) longer time horizon, (4) entrepreneurial approach to risk, and (5) need to collaborate with a range of specialists.

Two categories of family office are the single-family office (SFO) and multifamily office (MFO).

Features of family offices include the following: (1) integration, (2) holistic view, (3) privacy, (4) family engagement, and (5) dedicated wealth manager.

There are two main types of single-family offices: embedded family office (EFO) and virtual family office (VFO).

Defining SFOs by the generation of the family business are (1) optimizer, (2) preserver, (3) founder, and (4) entrepreneurial.

There are 10 domains of family wealth that summarize the needs of UHNW clients: (1) financial and investment management; (2) estate planning and legal issues; (3) social impact and philanthropy; (4) risk management; (5) governance and decision-making; (6) leadership and transition planning; (7) learning, development, and the younger generation; (8) family dynamics; (9) health and well-being; and (10) family advisory relationships.

## LOS W2.d

Wealthy families are often compared to organizations and businesses in the structures they need to function effectively.

Seven principles of good family governance include (1) competent decision makers, (2) objective decisions, (3) working together, (4) trust and integrity, (5) transparency, (6) independent oversight, and (7) fairness.

Commonly used governance structures include the following:

- A family mission statement for the family's aspirations and shared values
- A family constitution to document how the family manages their wealth matters
- A family council to represent the wider family in decision-making
- A family advisory board for strategic guidance, including family and nonfamily members
- A family foundation for the family's charitable goals and objectives

Five steps to create a family constitution include (1) input from each family member, (2) engagement and support, (3) strengths and weaknesses, (4) family meeting, and (5) commitment to the constitution.

48
The money scripts framework defines four unconscious biases toward money: (1) money worship, (2) money status, (3) money avoidance, and (4) money vigilant.

## ANSWER KEY FOR MODULE QUIZZES

### Module Quiz W2.1

1. Socioemotional wealth (SEW) recognizes that family businessowners sometimes make business decisions motivated by social and emotional factors rather than profit. The FIBER scale provides five examples of social and emotional factors: (1) family control, (2) family members identify with the firm, (3) binding social ties in the community, (4) emotional attachment, and (5) renewal of family bonds (succession planning). (LOS W2.a)
2. Felix is proud that Thomas and Bjorn are continuing the family legacy. This illustrates that the renewal of family bonds and succession planning has already taken place in the family business. Felix still enjoys an active role in the business community. This illustrates that Felix's social ties with the business community are still important to Felix, even though he is now retired. (LOS W2.a)
3. There are four stages in the development of a wealth identity: (1) honeymoon phase, (2) wealth acceptance, (3) identity consolidation, and (4) achieving balance. (LOS W2.a)
4. Anna Johansson is most likely in the wealth acceptance stage. She has been through the honeymoon stage with an initial spending spree and is now ready to start to decide what to do with her wealth. (LOS W2.a)

### Module Quiz W2.2

1. C The risk that a client may misunderstand or misinterpret the meaning of the values is termed polysemy. Social desirability is feeling pressured to answer the cards in a way expected by others. Overload bias is being overloaded and overwhelmed by too many choices of values cards. (LOS W2.b)
2. C Research across all demographic groups indicates that empathy is the most significant factor in building trust in advisor-client relationships. (LOS W2.b)

This is termed affect-based trust. Cognition-based trust is based on evidence of competence. The term socioemotional trust is fictitious. (LOS W2.b)

### Module Quiz W2.3

1. A This is classified as a preserver SFO. Both founder and entrepreneurial SFOs relate to wealth deriving from family businesses that have been sold.

A preserver SFO has two criteria:
1. Wealth has been owned for at least two generations. Arthur's grandfather started the family business.
2. The family still own the business. Arthur, Andrea, and Alice are still running the business and intend to continue doing so to continue the family legacy.

(LOS W2.c)

2. The Allinson family is most likely to be using an embedded SFO structure.

Embedded SFO structures are embedded within the operations of a family firm. This is the structure used within the operations of Allinson's Bakery, with employed bakery staff looking after both business and family matters. (LOS W2.c)

3. There are five common characteristics that distinguish UHNW clients:

1. They have more in common with institutions than private clients, because of the scale of their assets and their greater need for governance and organizational structure.

3. They have a longer time horizon.
4. They have an entrepreneurial approach to risk.
5. They have a greater need to collaborate with a wide range of specialists and service providers.

(LOS W2.c)
