READING W3

WEALTH PLANNING

# EXAM FOCUS

This reading has four relatively distinct LOS. The first deals with goals-based planning, which is building portfolios to address client goals rather than with traditional portfolio management methods. Make sure you can carry out the steps given the goals of the client and the available subportfolios. The second LOS addresses comprehensive wealth planning, where all facets of the client's investments and family business (if relevant) are considered when allocating portfolios. Know the three considerations of asset structuring and understand the basics of the wealth allocation framework—in particular, the three types of portfolios into which investor wealth is split. The next LOS deals with an overview of tax issues when providing private client advice. You are not expected to be a tax expert and do not need to know which countries use which tax systems, but a broad understanding of the different types of taxes, tax systems, and account types is required. The reading closes by discussing the liquidity constraints of a private investor: the amount of cash that needs to be drawn from a portfolio to meet client goals, and how the client might generate income to meet this liquidity requirement.

# MODULE W3.1: GOALS-BASED PLANNING

LOS W3.a: Formulate goals-based financial plans and recommend appropriate strategies to achieve an individual's goals-based financial plans.

Video covering this content is available online.

Goals-based planning (also called the behavioral portfolio framework) is designing client portfolios with the objective of meeting the client's goals, rather than maximizing risk-adjusted return or outperforming a benchmark.

Similar to the way a defined benefit pension fund manager focuses on measuring and managing the risk of the surplus (assets minus liabilities) of the plan, the focus of a goals-based portfolio manager is on the discretionary wealth of the client, defined as the client's assets minus their liabilities.

The goals of the client drive their liabilities. Typical investment goals for a private client include meeting lifestyle expenses that are not met by posttax income outside the investment portfolio; saving for retirement; paying for children's education; or purchasing a first or second home. These goals give rise to both explicit liabilities (e.g.,

52
mortgage payments and college fees) and implicit liabilities (e.g., present value of lifestyle spending). Client assets can also be both explicit (e.g., financial investments and savings or a primary residence) or implicit (e.g., the present value of future income, referred to as human capital).

For goals-based planning, an advisor should establish a family extended balance sheet that incorporates all explicit and implicit assets and liabilities of the client in order. A sample family extended balance sheet is displayed in Figure W3.1.¹

![img-0.jpeg](img-0.jpeg)
Figure W3.1: Sample Family Extended Balance Sheet

# Steps in Goals-Based Planning

Goals-based planning is executed in four steps:

1. Describe the goals of the client.
2. Quantify and prioritize goals.
3. Structure subportfolios to meet goals.
4. Manage the aggregate portfolio.

# Step 1: Describe the Goals of the Client

The client and the advisor should work together to identify financial goals. The following characteristics need to be established for each goal:

1. The time horizon needs to be established. Goals over the next 0–5 years are classified as short term, 5–15 years are intermediate, and goals over 15 years are long term.
2. Categorize lifestyle spending goals as nondiscretionary (essential or "incompressible" spending) and discretionary (nonessential). Discretionary goals can be further broken down into "nice to have" and "aspirational." These categories are related to the desired probability of meeting the goals. For example, nondiscretionary goals may have a desired 95% probability of being met (i.e., there is only a 5% chance the goal is not met). Discretionary nice-to-have goals may have a desired probability of 85%, and discretionary aspirational goals only 70%.
3. Outside of lifestyle spending goals, the client may have goals related to philanthropy (charitable work) and leaving an inheritance for future generations (referred to as legacy goals or dynastic goals). These goals are deemed to rank behind lifestyle

53
spending goals in terms of importance (with philanthropy more important than dynastic) and would have a lower required probability of success.

## PROFESSOR'S NOTE

The probability numbers just given are guidelines rather than strict definitions. The role of the advisor is to communicate effectively with the client such that goal categories can be agreed upon. You will not have to judge whether objectives are "nice to have" or "aspirational;" this will be made clear by the client in the case study, as in the example that follows. Know, however, that philanthropic goals rank behind lifestyle spending goals, and dynastic goals come last.

## Step 2: Quantify and Prioritize Client Goals

The second step of goals-based planning involves quantifying and prioritizing client goals. The following example demonstrates the prioritization process.

## EXAMPLE: The Rice family—prioritizing financial goals

Benjamin Rice, aged 50, and his wife, Heidi, aged 45, meet with their financial advisor. The couple recently sold their family business for USD 3,000,000 net of taxes and plan to consult and freelance to earn USD 250,000 annually, which covers their short-term lifestyle spending requirements. Both Benjamin and Heidi have workplace pension plans through the family business, from which they expect a modest income in retirement.

When asked about their financial goals, the Rices state the following:

- Retirement spending. The main priority of the Rices is their wish for an income above their moderate pension in retirement, which is expected to start in 15 years and last 20 years. They specify the following annual amounts, which are expected to increase in line with general inflation of 2%:

- Nondiscretionary needs: USD 80,000
- Discretionary nice to have: USD 40,000
- Discretionary aspirational: USD 20,000

- Children's education. The Rices have two children, currently aged 9 and 10. They expect the children to attend college, with tuition and living costs of USD 50,000 per child per year, rising at 3% per year. They describe this goal as their second priority and nondiscretionary, prudently assuming both children attend university for five years, starting in eight years.

- Entrepreneurial ambition. The Rices would like to set up another business in five years, but they describe this goal as discretionary. They estimate that USD 200,000 would be required to establish the business, and an extra USD 100,000 would be an aspirational goal to decrease their cost of financing the business.

- Philanthropy and legacy. The Rices have a goal of establishing a charitable foundation for research into autoimmune disease therapies in 35 years with a donation of USD 1,000,000. They state that this aspirational goal ranks behind the previously mentioned lifestyle spending needs. They do not expect to have

54
significant assets after this donation, but whatever is left can be bequeathed to the children.

Formulate the goals of the Rice family in terms of term, priority, and future quantity.

Answer:

| Goal | Term (Years From Now) | Length (Years) | Priority | Amount (USD) | Increase (Per Year) |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Retirement | 15 | 20 | Nondiscretionary lifestyle spending | 80,000 | 2% |
| | | | Discretionary lifestyle spending: “nice to have” | 40,000 | 2% |
| | | | Discretionary lifestyle spending: “aspirational” | 20,000 | 2% |
| Education | 8 | 5 | Nondiscretionary lifestyle spending | 100,000 | 3% |
| Entrepreneurial ambition | 5 | | Discretionary lifestyle spending: “nice to have” | 200,000 | |
| | | | Discretionary lifestyle spending: “aspirational” | 100,000 | |
| Philanthropy and legacy | 35 | | Aspirational | 1,000,000/ residual | |

Most goals and their priorities are explicitly defined in communication with the client. The education goal is USD 100,000 per year because there are two children with expected annual costs of USD 50,000 each.

Once the goals have been prioritized, they must be quantified. This involves calculating the present value of the amounts associated with each goal. This is demonstrated for the Rice family in the next example.

## EXAMPLE: The Rice family—quantifying financial goals

The financial advisor recommends using the following assumptions for the retirement spending goals:

- Discount rate for nondiscretionary lifestyle spending goals: 1%
- Discount rate for discretionary nice-to-have lifestyle spending goals: 5%
- Discount rate for discretionary aspirational lifestyle spending goals: 10%

The advisor uses a financial software package to calculate the following values at retirement (age 65):

- Value (at retirement) of nondiscretionary spending in retirement = 1,760,000
- Value (at retirement) of discretionary nice-to-have spending in retirement = 616,000
- Value (at retirement) of discretionary aspirational spending in retirement = 214,000

Calculate the present value of the retirement spending estimate for the Rice family.

# Answer:

All the values given are values at retirement when Benjamin Rice turns age 65 in 15 years. To turn these into present values, we simply need to discount them back by 15 years at the appropriate discount rate:

- Present value of nondiscretionary spending $= 1,760,000 / (1.01)^{15} = 1,515,975$
- Present value of discretionary nice-to-have spending $= 616,000 / (1.05)^{15} = 296,307$
- Value (at retirement) of discretionary aspirational spending $= 214,000 / (1.10)^{15} = 51,230$
- Total present value of the retirement spending estimate for the Rice family $= 1,515,975 + 296,307 + 51,230 = 1,863,512$

Using a similar logic, the present value of all the lifestyle spending needs of the Rice family is shown in the following table:

| Goal | Type | Initial Investment (USD Thousands) |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Retirement | Nondiscretionary | 1,516 |
| | Discretionary nice to have | 296 |
| | Discretionary aspirational | 51 |
| Education | Nondiscretionary | 480 |
| Entrepreneurial | Discretionary nice to have | 157 |
| Entrepreneurial | Discretionary aspirational | 62 |
| Total | | 2,562 |

# Step 3: Structure Subportfolios to Meet Goals

The objective of goals-based planning is to allocate appropriate subportfolios to each client goal (sometimes referred to as a behavioral objective). These subportfolios are diversified in their own right and are spread across the risk/return spectrum. The time frame and desired success probability of the goals drive the selection of the appropriate subportfolio. The general idea is that short-term essential goals are allocated safe subportfolios, while goals that are longer term and less essential are allocated riskier subportfolios.

A useful way to picture this process is to view goals in the form of a pyramid, with the most important goals at the base and the most aspirational goals at the top. We can view these goals as being met by a series of subportfolios of increasing risk as we move from the base to the tip of the pyramid, as in Figure W3.2.
![img-1.jpeg](img-1.jpeg)
Figure W3.2: Mapping Behavioral Objectives to Subportfolios

# PROFESSOR'S NOTE

Rather than being required to construct the subportfolios, you will likely be given a range of subportfolios to choose from and allocate to the client's goals. This will not necessarily be a one-to-one mapping of portfolios to goals; for example, there may a safe portfolio designed to meet short-term nondiscretionary goals, with a slightly higher-risk but still overall low-risk option for meeting intermediate nondiscretionary goals. The case is unlikely to leave any ambiguity regarding which portfolio is most appropriate for which client goal.

The aggregate portfolio constructed through goals-based planning is unlikely to be efficient in the mean-variance optimization sense (i.e., there may be an appropriate portfolio with a superior risk/return profile) because it ignores the correlation between subportfolios. However, building the portfolio this way is more likely to result in a portfolio that the client understands, and it helps to build a successful relationship of understanding and trust because the client can clearly link the portfolios to their goals. Through this behavioral-based approach, advisors can also address issues that often strain relationships with clients in times of stress, such as the need to consider the impact of unexpected inflation, intergenerational fairness, changes to lifestyle, uncertain needs, and any wish the client has to keep pace with general market returns.

We demonstrate Step 3 by revisiting the Rice family.

# EXAMPLE: Structuring subportfolios for the Rice family

The financial advisor presents the following five subportfolios (A-E):

- Subportfolio A is a low-risk safety reserve portfolio designed to meet nondiscretionary expenses in the short term (next 5 years).
- Subportfolio B is a low-risk portfolio designed to meet nondiscretionary expenses in the intermediate term (next 5-15 years).
- Subportfolio C is a low-risk portfolio designed to meet nondiscretionary expenses in the long term (over 15 years).
- Subportfolios D and E are higher-risk portfolios designed to meet discretionary lifestyle spending and other goals of lower priority.

Asset allocations for the subportfolios are shown in the following table:
58

| Subportfolios | | | | | |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Asset Class | A | B | C | D | E |
| Cash | 20% | 10% | 5% | 5% | 5% |
| Fixed income | 80% | 90% | 50% | 35% | 35% |
| Equity | 0% | 0% | 45% | 60% | 50% |
| Alternatives | 0% | 0% | 0% | 0% | 10% |

From the earlier example, the present values required to meet the lifestyle goals of the client are as follows:

| Goal | Type | Initial Investment (USD Thousands) |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Retirement | Nondiscretionary | 1,516 |
| | Discretionary nice-to-have | 296 |
| | Discretionary aspirational | 51 |
| Education | Nondiscretionary | 480 |
| Entrepreneurial | Discretionary nice-to-have | 157 |
| | Discretionary aspirational | 62 |
| Total | | 2,562 |

Based on the lifestyle spending goals of the Rice family and the available subportfolios, **recommend** an appropriate allocation to the subportfolios.

**Answer:**

First, we need to match the appropriate subportfolio to each lifestyle spending goal. The decision is driven by the time frame and the priority of the goals. An appropriate allocation is as follows:

- **Retirement spending.** Retirement spending is a long-term goal. Thus, appropriate allocations are as follows:
- Nondiscretionary needs: Subportfolio C
- Discretionary nice-to-have: Subportfolio D
- Discretionary aspirational: Subportfolio E

- **Children’s education.** Education spending is a nondiscretionary intermediate-term goal for which the most appropriate is Subportfolio B.

- **Entrepreneurial ambition.** These are discretionary short-term goals. The appropriate allocations are as follows:
- Discretionary nice-to-have: Subportfolio D
- Discretionary aspirational: Subportfolio E

**Step 4: Manage the Aggregate Portfolio**

Once we have allocated subportfolios to the lifestyle spending goals, we can consider allocating any remaining surplus to philanthropic and legacy goals. We will then have an allocation across subportfolios for our client that will suggest an overall aggregate asset allocation.
59

# EXAMPLE: Asset allocation for the Rice family

Given the subportfolio asset allocations and the recommended subportfolio allocations in the previous example, **determine** the appropriate overall asset allocation for the Rice family’s investment portfolio. Assume that any surplus over the amount required to meet lifestyle spending goals is allocated to the riskiest subportfolio.

## Answer:

The surplus funds available from the posttax business sale proceeds are USD 3,000,000 – USD 2,562,000 = USD 438,000. These funds can be allocated to the riskiest subportfolio, E.

Combining the recommended subportfolio allocations with the surplus allocation produces the following:

| Goal | Type | Initial Investment (USD Thousands) | Subportfolio |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Retirement | Nondiscretionary | 1,516 | C |
| | Discretionary nice-to-have | 296 | D |
| | Discretionary aspirational | 51 | E |
| Education | Nondiscretionary | 480 | B |
| Entrepreneurial | Discretionary nice-to-have | 157 | D |
| | Discretionary aspirational | 62 | E |
| Philanthropy and legacy | | 438 | E |
| Total | | 3,000 | |

Hence, this is the final asset allocation of the Rice family portfolio:

| | Lifestyle Spending Needs | | | | | | |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| | Retirement | | Education | | Entrepreneurial Ambition | | Philanthropy and Legacy |
| | Need | Nice-to-Have | Aspirational | Need | Nice-to-Have | Aspirational | |
| Subportfolio | C | D | E | B | D | E | E |
| Allocation (USD 000s) | 1,516 | 296 | 51 | 480 | 157 | 62 | 438 |

This gives an allocation across subportfolios as follows:

Allocation to Subportfolio A = 0.00%

Allocation to Subportfolio B = 480 / 3,000 = 16.00%

Allocation to Subportfolio C = 1,516 / 3,000 = 50.53%

Allocation to Subportfolio D = (296 + 157) / 3,000 = 15.10%

Allocation to Subportfolio E = (51 + 62 + 438) / 3,000 = 18.37%

Recall the asset allocations of the individual subportfolios:
| Asset Class | Subportfolios | | | | |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| | A | B | C | D | E |
| Cash | 20% | 10% | 5% | 5% | 5% |
| Fixed income | 80% | 90% | 50% | 35% | 35% |
| Equity | 0% | 0% | 45% | 60% | 50% |
| Alternatives | 0% | 0% | 0% | 0% | 10% |

Combining these subportfolios provides an aggregate asset allocation (rounded to the nearest whole percentage):

| Asset Allocation | |
| --- | --- |
| Cash | 6% |
| Fixed income | 51% |
| Equity | 41% |
| Alternatives | 2% |

For example, the number for fixed income is calculated as follows:

Fixed income allocation $= (0 \times 80\%) + (0.16 \times 90\%) + (0.505 \times 50\%) + (0.151 \times 35\%) + (0.1837 \times 35\%) = 51\%$

# Aligning Goals Over Different Life Cycle Stages

The advisor must be aware that the goals, time horizon, and human capital of the client will change across their lifetime. Typically, early in their career, clients will focus on developing their human capital and meeting short-term expenses. Longer-term goals such as saving for retirement typically become priorities later in the career development stage when financial capital is accumulated. In the preretirement phase, the focus turns to paying down debts and consolidating assets. In the retirement stage, deaccumulation of assets begins, with goals focused on maintaining lifestyle and generating income on which to live. Longer-term objectives relating to wealth transfer and inheritance will also be considered.

Monte Carlo simulation can play a key role in goals-based planning in that it can generate a distribution of possible outcomes, allowing the client to understand the probability of meeting goals with different asset allocations. This, in turn, can help inform the advisor of the client's risk tolerance. Monte Carlo simulations can also help an advisor assess the portfolio's resilience to adverse changes in the economy or the situation of the client.

# MODULE QUIZ W3.1

1. Which of the following statements regarding goals-based planning is most accurate?

A. In general, the safest subportfolio should be used to meet the highest-priority, short-term goals of the client.
B. Typically, the highest-risk subportfolio is used to meet philanthropic goals.
C. Goals-based planning requires a different subportfolio for each different goal of the client.

2. A 30-year-old client describes a family goal as follows: "We wish to purchase a vacation home that we can use in our preretirement and retirement years when we
are age 50. We view it as essential that we have at least GBP 500,000 available. This amount would purchase the bare-minimum property, which would likely require significant renovation. GBP 750,000 would be nice to have because then the property purchased would likely have no renovation requirements. An aspiration would be to have GBP 1,000,000 available to purchase a property with some luxury features."

Which of the following statements regarding this goal is most accurate?

A. The advisor should record a nondiscretionary goal equal to GBP 500,000 in 20 years.
B. The advisor should record a discretionary aspirational goal equal to GBP 1,000,000 in 20 years.
C. The advisor should consider philanthropic goals before recording the vacation home as a goal.

# MODULE W3.2: COMPREHENSIVE WEALTH PLANNING FOR PRIVATE CLIENTS

Video covering this content is available online.

LOS W3.b: Recommend and justify methods to manage a family's financial exposures holistically across their lifetime and retirement.

While the goals-based planning approach is useful for allocating assets based purely on client goals, a client is likely to have other key issues that need consideration in their financial planning. These issues could relate to the ownership, management, and intergenerational transfer of a family business; the minimization of tax liabilities and other costs; and risk management. Comprehensive wealth planning takes a holistic approach and considers these factors when advising clients.

Comprehensive wealth planning distinguishes between assets managed internally or externally.

Externally managed assets are assets and investments controlled by a manager outside the client's family, including investments managed by external investment managers and family offices. The external manager is employed by the client because they provide expertise that the family themselves cannot provide. This exposes the client to the external risk of the manager's strategy and market volatility.

Internally managed assets are directly controlled by the client or family. Examples include family businesses, privately owned real estate and other real assets, and the human capital of the client. These assets are likely to be significantly less liquid than externally managed assets and are subject to family risk, the risk that management decisions depend on a few key persons.

A family business should be considered as both a significant internally managed single-stock investment and a major source of human capital. When families are not capable or unwilling to continue running their family business, they should consider selling the business. While selling the business will increase liquidity and the ability to diversify the client's portfolio, other considerations include the succession plan to new management, the maintenance of family wealth after the sale, and the ease with which the business can be sold. There may also be emotional attachment to a family business, particularly if it has been in the family for several generations.

61
A family-run business is also likely to have issues relating to preserving intergenerational wealth. The advisor should work with the client to ensure that the family has a shared vision with respect to the business and a clear governance structure. Future generations should be financially literate and pursue independence and entrepreneurship themselves.

## Asset Structuring

Asset structuring is maximizing the value of a client's investments and business interests through the following three considerations:

### 1. Legal and physical location of assets

- The client can hold assets directly, or control a partnership or corporation that owns the assets (known as a holding company). The physical and corporate location of the assets determines the tax laws that apply for corporate income, capital gains, and asset transfer. The physical location of assets can also affect their value, particularly for real estate where value is usually inherently linked to the local economy.
- For a married couple, the local tax laws may also make a distinction between community property and separate property. Community property is deemed to be jointly owned by spouses and is divided equally in a divorce or on the death of a spouse. Separate property is deemed to be generated externally to the marriage; therefore, it is not partitioned in a divorce or at death.

### 2. Tax, legal, and other obligations

- To avoid tax and legal complications, any transactions in family assets between family members must be handled as a related-party arms-length transaction. This means that the transaction price is set as if the buyer and seller are independently acting in their own interests without being affected by any pre-existing relationship (even though a relationship exists). This rule is designed to prevent related parties from setting transaction prices that evade capital gains or transfer taxes.
- Assets should be structured such that business value is maximized based on after-tax cash flows. For example, for assets with high levels of income and with no desire to sell, a holding company could be established to own the assets in a country with low corporate income tax and higher capital gains tax. For other assets with low income but high potential growth in value, a holding company could be established in a country with high income tax rates and low capital gains taxes.
- When transferring assets from one generation to the next, the tax impact should be considered. A sale of assets by the company could result in both corporation tax and taxes for shareholders on income or gains (referred to as double taxation). The sale of company shares may trigger capital gains tax liability for the seller (this will be more efficient if capital gains tax rates are relatively low). Moving assets offshore to a location with low tax rates can also be beneficial.

## PROFESSOR'S NOTE

We will discuss tax strategies in greater depth in the next LOS. Here, we are focusing on investment advice while being tax efficient. A financial advisor likely needs to work with an expert tax consultant when structuring investments from a tax perspective.

### 3. Risk management

- Risk management in private wealth advice focuses primarily on using insurance to protect financial and human capital from unforeseen adverse events. The advisor and client should work together to establish the probability and impact of such events and assess whether the client can face the risk using their existing capital (self-insure) or purchase insurance.
- The risks to human capital are primarily morbidity (sickness or disability), which is managed through critical illness insurance, or mortality (death), managed through life insurance. A high-
earning private client will need human capital protection significantly greater than that provided by the state or government.

- Other risks that may need insuring include property and casualty risks relating to damage to property; and legal risk, particularly for high-profile, high-net-worth individuals who may be targeted by opportunistic lawsuits.
- The client may also wish to lawfully protect their assets from creditors or other claimants through holding structures such as limited liability companies and offshore trusts (if recognized by the relevant legal system). When using such structures, they must be legally valid and transparent for tax purposes.

# Comprehensive Assessment of Aggregate Wealth

A common issue with wealthy private clients is that they often have a large, illiquid, concentrated position in their assets—usually in the form of the family business. This causes a lack of diversification and potential liquidity issues for the client.

The advisor should work with the client to decide whether to keep managing the business and develop a long-term plan to sustain the client and future generations; sell the business; or use nonbusiness assets to diversify (or directly hedge) the specific risks inherent in the concentrated position.

# Comprehensive Perspective on Internal and External Assets

Advisors should consider the role that the client's assets are playing in the portfolio. We consider a wealth allocation framework (also called an aspirational investor framework), which categorizes client assets as being part of one of three subportfolios:

1. The safety portfolio is designed to meet nondiscretionary lifestyle spending (and short-term discretionary lifestyle spending) with minimal risk. The focus of this portfolio should be on preserving wealth—therefore, it should have a target real rate of return of zero. Cash, high-quality fixed income, a mortgaged primary residence, and unmortgaged second homes would be considered part of this portfolio.
2. The market portfolio is designed to meet longer-term lifestyle spending. These assets are a liquid and diversified portfolio of stocks and bonds, usually returning mid-to-high single-digit returns, and providing both income and growth.
3. The aspirational portfolio is high risk with potential for large gains and losses. This portfolio will contain the family business, investments in venture capital, leveraged real estate investments, and other concentrated high-risk positions. For clients with a successful family business, this can be the largest component of wealth.

The wealth allocation framework has the objective of each subportfolio meeting its goals, rather than to optimize the overall risk/return of the portfolio.

# EXAMPLE: Applying the wealth allocation framework

Jeremiah Taylor is a successful entrepreneur who has built a logistics and distribution business currently valued at GBP 25 million, which is wholly owned by Jeremiah and his wife, Laura. The family is comfortable with the management of the
business and does not wish to sell or transfer ownership in the short or medium term. The family has a private residence valued at GBP 4 million. They have, between them, savings and investments of GBP 7 million, allocated to diversified passive investment funds.

The Taylors state their investment objectives as maintaining their current lifestyle expenses and funding their children's education without relying on income from their business. They specify these as essential lifestyle goals. They also wish to reserve GBP 10 million for philanthropic causes and state this as a nonessential goal.

The Taylors meet with their financial advisor, who works with them to establish that GBP 6 million is required to pay current lifestyle expenses and fund their children's education.

Using the wealth allocation framework, evaluate the Taylors' ability to meet their goals based on their current wealth allocation. If appropriate, recommend a rebalancing of the portfolio to better align it with their goals.

Answer:

The wealth allocation framework splits the portfolio into three subportfolios. The current allocation of the Taylors' assets is as follows:

- Safety portfolio—private residence valued at GBP 4 million
- Market portfolio—diversified investment portfolio valued at GBP 7 million
- Aspirational portfolio—family business valued at GBP 25 million

The current lifestyle expenses and children's education (estimated to have a present value of GBP 6 million) are essential lifestyle goals; therefore, they need to be met with low-risk assets in the safety portfolio. Currently, the safety portfolio consists of their private residence, which is not available for meeting essential lifestyle expenses. Hence, the Taylors need to increase the safety portfolio by GBP 6 million, rebalancing from the liquid market portfolio.

This will result in the following allocation:

- Safety portfolio—private residence valued at GBP 4 million; cash and investment-grade fixed income: GBP 6 million
- Market portfolio—diversified investment portfolio valued at GBP 1 million
- Aspirational portfolio—family business valued at GBP 25 million

The philanthropic goal of reserving GBP 10 million is not currently met by liquid assets in the portfolio. While a balance of GBP 1 million can be held in the market portfolio to earn market returns, a better use of this allocation is likely to help mitigate the idiosyncratic risk of the large position in the family business.

The wealth allocation framework is similar, but not identical, to the goals-based planning framework we described earlier. There is obvious overlap between the way

64
that the portfolio is segregated into subportfolios to meet client goals. These two frameworks can be pictured alongside each other as per Figure W3.3. $^{3}$

![img-2.jpeg](img-2.jpeg)
Figure W3.3: Goals-Based Financial Planning and the Wealth Allocation Framework

As can be seen in Figure W3.3, the nondiscretionary goals of goals-based planning broadly align with the safety portfolio of the wealth allocation framework. Similarly, the discretionary goals of goals-based planning generally align with the market portfolio of the wealth allocation framework.

The key difference between the two strategies is the focus of the wealth allocation framework on the family business in the aspirational portfolio. It is not as simple as to say that the family business is aspirational—and, therefore, used to provide for goals in excess of lifestyle spending, such as philanthropy and legacy. Rather, the wealth-based allocation framework starts from the viewpoint that the family business is risky (because it is a large, illiquid, concentrated position), and then it considers the investor's objectives with respect to this business (income, growth, philanthropy, or legacy). It directly acknowledges that the family business should not be used to meet the lifestyle protection and market participation roles met by the safety and market portfolios, respectively. The wealth allocation approach also avoids the strict prioritization of goals that goals-based planning requires.

# MODULE QUIZ W3.2

1. Which of the following statements regarding internally managed assets versus externally managed assets is most accurate?

A. Externally managed assets tend to be more liquid than internally managed assets.
B. Internally managed assets should be used to meet non-lifestyle dynastic goals, whereas externally managed assets should be used to meet lifestyle spending needs.
C. Key person risk is a crucial risk factor for externally managed assets only.

2. The owner of a successful small private business is reaching retirement and is looking to sell the business. She has been approached by a private equity firm, which has valued the business at USD 1.5 million. The businessowner has a loyal management team that has worked there for many years, and she wishes to ensure that their roles are safe after the transaction. As a result, she encourages a management-led buyout and agrees to a valuation of USD 1.3 million. Which of the following statements regarding the management-led buyout is most accurate?
A. The transaction is a related-party arms-length transaction because all parties are acting in their self-interest.
B. The transaction is not a related-party arms-length transaction because the seller is allowing her relationship with the management team to affect her valuation.
C. The transaction is not a related-party arms-length transaction because the management team has a pre-existing relationship with the owner of the business.

# MODULE W3.3: TAXATION

LOS W3.c: Evaluate how the principles of taxation and taxes influence goals-based planning and holistic financial plans for individual investors.

Video covering this content is available online.

# Main Categories of Taxes

Regarding the main categories of tax, income tax applies to various types of income, including wages, rents, interest, and dividends. Capital gains tax is based on price appreciation (e.g., proceeds minus cost) upon disposal of an asset. Tax rates on capital gains often differ from income tax rates.

Other forms of taxation, less significant but still relevant to a private wealth advisor, include the following:

- Withholding tax. This is charged on interest and dividend income for nonresidents.
- Wealth/property tax. This is paid annually, based on the value of assets held.
- Stamp duties. This is a tax on the purchase price of real estate or financial assets. Rates may be higher for foreigners than for domestic investors.
- Wealth transfer tax. Examples include estate (inheritance) and gift taxes. Tax may be imposed on the party transferring, or on the recipient.

Double taxation occurs when an income stream is taxed twice. A common example occurs when investors suffer income tax on dividends that are paid out of after-tax corporate earnings. Many jurisdictions have provisions to reduce double taxation of dividends, including franking credits, which recognize that a certain amount of corporation tax has already been paid, usually leaving the investor to top up if their personal tax rate is higher (or lower dividend taxation rates if the stock is held for a minimum holding period—for example, 60 days in the United States).

# Tax Efficiency

Private wealth advisors need to consider tax efficiency in their decision-making. While tax rules vary across jurisdictions, some general rules usually apply:

1. Dividend tax rates are often lower than capital gains—which, in turn, are often lower than ordinary income tax rates. This implies that high-yield strategies (fixed income) tend to be less tax efficient because they are subject to relatively high ordinary income tax rates.
2. Long-term capital gains tax rates are often lower than short-term capital gains. This implies that higher turnover strategies tend to be less tax efficient because they realize gains, and associated capital gains tax, sooner and pay a higher rate of tax.

3. The ability to time entry into and exit from positions gives managers some control over the tax burden. For example, managers can sell losing positions to realize losses that can be offset against gains realized elsewhere in the portfolio, thereby decreasing capital gains taxes (see the discussion on tax loss harvesting later in this section).

4. Alternative investments may sometimes be tax efficient, but rules are typically complex and specialized. Trading strategies used by hedge funds can have complex tax rules.

5. The nature of a trading strategy can affect tax efficiency. For example, momentum (trend-following) strategies buy rising stocks and sell falling stocks to cut losses quickly. This tends to realize losses sooner than gains, pushing capital gains tax liabilities into the future.

6. The use of style boxes to categorize managers (e.g., large cap/mid cap/small cap) may force managers to sell stocks when a holding moves out of their style definition, triggering a tax liability. If an investor has allocated capital to a small-cap manager who is forced to sell a holding because its market cap is increasing—and has exposure to a mid-cap manager who buys the same holding—there could be a tax liability, even without the aggregate portfolio changing.

## Asset Location

The major types of investment account from a tax perspective are as follows:

- A taxable account (e.g., individual brokerage account) is taxed at the relevant rate for each type of investment return.
- A tax-deferred account (e.g., traditional individual retirement account) allows for pretax contributions and tax-free accumulation, but all amounts are taxed at withdrawal as ordinary income.
- A tax-exempt account (e.g., Roth IRA in the United States) allows after-tax contributions to accumulate on a tax-free basis and with no taxes on withdrawal.

Asset location is deciding which assets to hold in taxable, tax-exempt, or tax-deferred accounts to maximize their after-tax value.

## EXAMPLE: Tax-aware asset location

Consider an investor with equal-sized taxable and tax-exempt accounts (i.e., tax rules give them the ability to locate half of their investment portfolio in tax-exempt accounts). Their desired asset allocation is 50% fixed income and 50% equity. The expected returns of these asset classes in each account are as follows:

67
| Asset Class | Asset Location | |
| --- | --- | --- |
| | Taxable | Tax Exempt |
| Equity | 6.5% | 9.0% |
| Fixed income | 4.0% | 7.0% |

Calculate the expected return of the portfolio under the following asset location strategies:

1. Conventional wisdom. Hold assets with higher return in the tax-exempt account.
2. Tax-aware asset location. Hold the tax-inefficient asset class in the tax-exempt account.

# Answer:

1. Conventional wisdom would place the higher-returning assets into the tax-exempt account. In this case, $50\%$ of the portfolio is allocated to tax-exempt equity (earning $9.0\%$ ) and $50\%$ of the portfolio is allocated to taxable fixed income (earning $4.0\%$ ).

The total after-tax return of the portfolio $= (0.5 \times 9.0\%) + (0.5 \times 4.0\%) = 6.5\%$ .

2. A tax-aware (also called tax-smart) asset location places the less tax-efficient assets into the tax-exempt account. Because fixed income is usually less tax efficient due to high ordinary income tax rates that cannot be deferred, we allocate $50\%$ to tax-exempt fixed income (earning $7\%$ ) and $50\%$ to taxable equity (earning $6.5\%$ ).

The total after-tax return of the portfolio $= (0.5 \times 7.0\%) + (0.5 \times 6.5\%) = 6.75\%$ .

We next consider introducing investment strategies that are tax aware in that they are run in a tax-efficient manner. The investor now has the choices of asset location (taxable vs. tax exempt) and investment strategy (non-tax efficient vs. tax efficient).

# EXAMPLE: Tax-aware asset location and investment strategy

For the same investor in the previous example, a financial advisor has introduced new tax-efficient investment strategies for both equity and fixed income. The expected returns of these asset classes and strategies in each account are as follows:

| Asset Class | Asset Location | |
| --- | --- | --- |
| | Taxable | Tax Exempt |
| Equity | 6.5% | 9.0% |
| Tax-managed equity | 6.9% | 8.0% |
| Fixed income | 4.0% | 7.0% |
| Tax-managed fixed income | 4.5% | 4.5% |

Calculate the expected return of the portfolio under the following asset location strategies:
1. Partially tax-aware asset location and investment strategy. Allocate asset classes evenly across asset location (taxable vs. tax exempt) and run taxable investment accounts using tax-managed investment strategies.
2. Tax-aware location and investment strategy. Allocate tax-inefficient assets to tax-exempt accounts and run taxable investment accounts using tax-managed investment strategies.

Answer:

1. Allocating asset classes evenly across asset location (taxable vs. tax exempt) will split equity and fixed income evenly across taxable and tax-exempt accounts (i.e., 25% in each).

The 25% allocations to taxable fixed income and taxable equity will both be run using tax-managed strategies. The tax-exempt accounts will be run using non-tax-managed strategies.

| Asset Class | Asset Location | | Contribution to Return |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| | Taxable | Tax Exempt | |
| Equity | | 0.25 × 9.0% | 2.250% |
| Tax-managed equity | 0.25 × 6.9% | | 1.725% |
| Fixed income | | 0.25 × 7.0% | 1.750% |
| Tax-managed fixed income | 0.25 × 4.5% | | 1.125% |

The total after-tax return = 2.250% + 1.725% + 1.750% + 1.125% = 6.85%.

2. To be tax aware with respect to asset location, the tax-inefficient fixed income should be allocated to the tax-exempt account. This gives an allocation of 50% tax-exempt fixed income and 50% taxable equity.

To be tax aware with respect to investment strategy, the taxable equity should be run using a tax-managed strategy and earn an after-tax return of 6.9%. The fixed income allocation will be run using the current strategy and earn 7.0% (rather than switch to the tax-managed strategy and earn only 4.5%).

The total after-tax return of the portfolio = (0.5 × 6.9%) + (0.5 × 7.0%) = 6.95%.

PROFESSOR'S NOTE

The most important takeaway from these examples is the final strategy. Make sure you know how to allocate the portfolio being tax aware with respect to both asset location and investment strategy. Remember the following:

- Tax-aware asset location → tax-inefficient assets held in tax-exempt account
- Tax-aware investment strategy → taxable investments run using tax-managed strategy
70

# Tax Loss Harvesting

Tax loss harvesting is selling securities with embedded losses to offset realized gains elsewhere in the portfolio, thereby decreasing the capital gains tax liability for the year.

When tax loss sale proceeds are reinvested in similar assets that go on to make gains, tax loss harvesting is simply deferring taxation rather than avoiding tax completely. For example, an investor may have a security in their portfolio worth USD 10 that they bought for USD 15. If there are realized taxable gains elsewhere in the portfolio, they could realize a tax loss of USD 5 by selling the stock and use this loss to offset the gains to decrease taxes in the current period. However, if the proceeds of the tax loss sale (USD 10) are reinvested in a similar security that rises to USD 15 in the next period, if the asset is sold, there will be a realized gain of USD 5 that will trigger a tax liability.

Not all jurisdictions allow tax loss harvesting. Wash sale rules may exist that deny losses for tax purposes if the same or a substantially similar security is purchased within a certain time of realizing the loss.

# Tax Avoidance vs. Tax Evasion

In using strategies to reduce taxes, advisors must distinguish between tax avoidance, which is efficient tax planning within the spirit and letter of tax rules, and tax evasion, which is illegal and involves deception or fraud to conceal income sources that would otherwise be subject to tax.

While the line between aggressive tax avoidance and tax evasion is not always clear, it can generally be said that if an activity has no other purpose than to avoid taxes, the activity is likely not within the spirit of the tax rules—and therefore, probably unethical, and possibly illegal. For example, a deliberate attempt to offset tax losses through transactions that breach the spirit of clearly stated wash sale rules would be considered an attempt at tax evasion, rather than efficient tax avoidance.

# Types of Tax Systems

A tax haven is a jurisdiction with extremely low tax rates or no taxes for residents and foreign investors (e.g., Bermuda).

A territorial tax system taxes only income that is earned within that jurisdiction and does not tax citizens on income or gains earned overseas (e.g., Hong Kong SAR).

A worldwide tax system imposes taxes on all sources of income or gains, regardless of where they are earned (e.g., Switzerland). This type of regime could lead to double taxation if earnings generated overseas are taxed both abroad and at home. However, relief may be provided in the form of tax credits by the home jurisdiction for tax already paid in the source jurisdiction, or by tax treaties between governments that decrease tax rates on income subject to double taxation.

Often, taxes are imposed on only residents of the jurisdiction, so it is crucial to determine the jurisdiction in which the investor is a resident if they spend significant time in more than one country during a tax year. These residence rules are usually
based on the amount of time spent in the jurisdiction (e.g., you may be deemed a resident if you spend more than six months of the year in a certain country).

A citizenship-based tax system imposes a tax based on citizenship, regardless of where income is earned. This system is used by U.S. tax authorities and means that a U.S. citizen living and earning income in Singapore, for example, would be subject to taxes in the United States on income generated in Singapore (and anywhere else in the world). Note that the U.S. tax system also imposes taxes on the worldwide income earned by noncitizen residents, so a Singapore citizen who has permanent resident status in the United States (referred to as a green card) can be taxed on worldwide income, even if they are not currently residing in the United States.

Tax regimes are generally classified as flat taxes or progressive taxes. In a flat system, the same tax rate is paid, regardless of the amount of income. In a progressive tax system, the tax rate increases as the level of income increases.

Figure W3.4 shows seven global tax regimes delineated by whether the ordinary income tax rate is progressive or flat, and by the treatment of investment returns (i.e., interest, dividends, and capital gains).

W3.4: Seven Global Tax Regimes

| Tax Regime | Ordinary Income Tax Structure | Favorable Treatment for Interest Income? | Favorable Treatment for Dividend Income? | Favorable Treatment for Capital Gains? |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Common progressive | Progressive | Yes | Yes | Yes |
| Heavy dividend tax | Progressive | Yes | No | Yes |
| Heavy capital gain tax | Progressive | Yes | Yes | No |
| Heavy interest tax | Progressive | No | Yes | Yes |
| Light capital gain tax | Progressive | No | No | Yes |
| Flat and light | Flat | Yes | Yes | Yes |
| Flat and heavy | Flat | Yes | No | No |

The first regime, common progressive, is observed most frequently. The United States, United Kingdom, China, France, Italy, Japan, and many other countries fall under this category. There is, however, considerable variation in the special treatment of investment income—with some countries providing exemption for only part of investment income, and other countries providing exemption for all investment income. The light capital gain tax regime is the second-most-common regime, while only one country, Colombia, fell under the heavy capital gain tax regime.⁴

PROFESSOR'S NOTE

Do not invest too much time memorizing the details of this table. The main takeaway is that there are many different types of tax regimes. Most of the regimes define themselves quite obviously from their name. For example, a heavy capital gains tax regime has, rather predictably, low tax rates for interest and dividends. The only regime that does not define itself easily in
this way is flat and heavy, for which we must remember that there may still be low rates for interest income.

Tax regimes across the world are varied and complex. When advising private clients, a general rule is to establish citizenship first, then legal domicile (i.e., permanent residence), then location of assets/income, then ownership structure. These factors drive the tax exposure of the client. When dealing with complex international clients, a financial advisor must employ a tax specialist to address the tax implications of cross-border investments.

## EXAMPLE: Cross-border investing—ownership structure

Cecile Abayon is a citizen of the Philippines and lives there throughout the year. She is thinking of investing in property located in the United States. Some features of the relevant tax regimes to consider are as follows:

- The Philippines operates a territorial tax regime.
- The investment would be subject to U.S. income, capital gains, estate, and gift taxes.
- U.S. corporate capital gains tax rates are higher than individual capital gains tax rates.

Evaluate the various tax implications to Abayon of the following ownership structures:

1. Direct ownership
2. Ownership indirectly through U.S. corporation
3. Ownership indirectly through non-US corporation

## Answer:

**Option 1: owning property directly in nonresident individual name**

- Withholding tax may apply to gross, not net, rental income
- U.S. estate tax must be paid upon death on the U.S. asset

**Option 2: owning property indirectly via U.S. corporation (owned by Abayon or a non-U.S. corporation)**

- Withholding tax may apply to net rental income (i.e., local operating expenses in the United States can be offset against taxable income)
- Upon death, U.S. corporation shares subject to U.S. estate taxes
- Upon death, if U.S. corporation is liquidated and the assets sold, any capital gains are subject to higher corporate taxes than individual taxes

**Option 3: owning property indirectly via non-U.S. corporation**

- Upon Abayon's death, transfer of non-U.S. corporation shares not subject to U.S. estate tax

72
73

# EXAMPLE: Territorial vs. worldwide tax regimes

A client has exposure to investments in both their home jurisdiction and a foreign jurisdiction. **Discuss** the tax implications of their relevant tax regime being territorial versus worldwide.

## Answer:

### Home country investment portfolios

- With both territorial and worldwide tax regimes, investment portfolios in the home country are subject to taxes in the home country:
- Check relative tax rates on capital gains, dividends, and interest and reallocate more (less) investments to those types with lower (higher) tax rates.

### Foreign country investment portfolios

- A territorial regime in the home country will not tax income earned in the foreign portfolio; a worldwide regime in the home country would tax income earned in the foreign portfolio.
- For both territorial and worldwide tax regimes, withholding taxes paid in the foreign jurisdiction on gross income will likely apply:
- If there is a tax treaty, there may be a reduction in withholding tax rates for one or more types of investment income (e.g., interest and dividends).
- Whether there is a treaty or not to maximize after-tax return, check for any advantageous provisions in the foreign country where the following apply:
- Investment returns (e.g., capital gains) on investments held by foreign investors are not taxed in the foreign country.
- Investment income (e.g., interest, dividends) is not subject to withholding taxes.

### Wealth and estate taxes

- Check for any estate taxes and what items are included or excluded, as that may affect investment allocations or the investment holding structure (e.g., establish an offshore company to hold foreign investments to avoid foreign estate tax).
- Check for any estate tax treaties that may allow for an estate tax exemption in the foreign country. However, in some instances, obtaining the exemption may require disclosure of full net worth to the foreign country tax authorities, which the client may find undesirable.

# The Common Reporting Standard and Foreign Account Tax Compliance Act

To avoid tax leakage arising from tax evasion, automated tax information exchanges between countries is becoming much more widespread. The focus is on banks, investment managers, and wealthy families with international investments.

The Common Reporting Standard (CRS) was facilitated by the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development (OECD) and involves information exchange
among more than 100 jurisdictions. The CRS is also referred to as the Standard for Automatic Exchange of Financial Account Information.

The Foreign Account Tax Compliance Act (FATCA) originated in the United States as a means for U.S. taxpayers to pay tax on investment income earned outside the country. The FATCA applies to all financial institutions who have U.S. investors.

1. An investor is evaluating various assets and strategies for her portfolio. Based solely on tax effects, which type of investment would most likely be favored in a country with high tax rates on interest, dividends, and realized gains?

A. Growth stocks with moderate turnover.
B. Bonds with periodic payment of interest.
C. Value stocks held for a moderate period.

2. Which of the following assets would be the most appropriate asset to locate in a tax-deferred account rather than a taxable account?

A. Tax-exempt bonds.
B. High-growth stocks.
C. Corporate bonds.

3. An investor has a realized capital gain of USD 80,000 and is subject to a capital gains tax rate of 30%. The investor can sell another stock with a cost basis of USD 140,000 and a current market value of USD 90,000. The tax savings from harvesting the loss is closest to:

A. $9,000.
B. $10,000.
C. $15,000.

The client pays 40% tax on interest income and 20% tax on capital gains and dividends. The appropriate amount of equity as a percentage to hold in the tax-exempt portfolio is closest to:

A. 20% of the overall portfolio.
B. 35% of the overall portfolio.
C. 50% of the overall portfolio.

## MODULE W3.4: LIQUIDITY AND CASH FLOW PLANNING

Video covering this content is available online.

**LOS W3.d: Recommend appropriate liquidity strategies for goal-based planning and holistic financial plans.**

Typical liquidity requirements of a private investor include the following:

- Meeting short-term expenses not already met through posttax income outside the portfolio
- Holding an emergency cash reserve for unexpected adverse events
- Having the ability to react quickly to new investment opportunities
- Risk management of other cash demands on the portfolio, such as private equity fund drawdowns

Illiquid assets are expected to offer an extra return in terms of a liquidity premium to compensate for the liquidity risk associated with not being able to sell investments easily without offering significant discounts, particularly in times of market stress.

A client with too much exposure to liquidity risk faces a significant possibility that the portfolio will fail to meet high unexpected short-term cash obligations. On the other hand, a portfolio with too high a level of liquid assets risks missing out on long-term liquidity premiums that could be crucial in meeting the client's long-term goals. Liquidity planning is the balancing of this tradeoff.

Liquidity requirements typically evolve over a private client's life cycle. In the accumulation stage of life (up to age 50), the client is usually earning a substantial income to cover expenses, so liquidity requirements and income needs from the portfolio are low. The focus on growth for retirement goals implies illiquid assets can comprise a significant part of the portfolio. Starting at the preretirement stage (ages 50–65) and continuing through retirement (65+), the client's time horizon shortens, and after-tax income decreases, thus increasing the liquidity and income needs of the portfolio. The client should decrease illiquid holdings and hold more liquid instruments with less chance of significant drawdown.

Investors should consider the illiquid nature of family business investments and their human capital in general. Businessowners may build cash reserves or arrange asset-backed short-term loans to manage the liquidity risk of a concentrated position in a family business. Care should be taken to adhere to tax laws when drawing capital from the company. In particular, a loan from the family business to the individual could be reclassified as taxable compensation, leading to a tax liability.

# Portfolio Income Generation

Income generation, when required, can be done through the following investments:

- Fixed income, a liquid asset class (especially investment grade), is an obvious candidate for income generation through coupon payments. The investor still faces interest rate risk and credit risk, while returns are potentially low and subject to inflation risk. A ladder strategy invests across maturities, aiming to match the client's liquidity needs to minimize interest rate and reinvestment risk (however, any default risk will remain). Active strategies such as duration trading or sector/credit rating rotation may offer higher returns, but they may introduce higher fees and active risk into the portfolio.
- Listed equities typically offer liquidity. Stocks with high or growing dividend yields are appropriate for generating income. Fixed-dividend preferred stocks can provide stable income, though these may be less liquid.
- Alternative assets are significantly less liquid. Income-generating strategies such as real estate and infrastructure can provide inflation protection if their cash flows are contractually linked to inflation.

75
# Spending in Retirement

Once the client is retired, to minimize the risk of capital depletion, spending should primarily come from income earned by corporate, personal, or government (public) pension plans and other retirement savings.

Consider these main factors when estimating expected spending levels:

- Life expectancy. How comfortable is the investor with longevity risk (i.e., outliving their assets)?
- Inflation. Is the portfolio protected against unexpected inflation increasing spending levels?
- Asset allocation. What is the general risk level of the portfolio? What is the rebalancing policy?
- Spending flexibility. Can spending be reduced, if necessary? This may turn out to be essential.
- Taxes. Spending must come from posttax returns. Will tax rates change in the future?

A starting point when estimating expected spending is to use the 4% rule, which states that retirees can afford to spend 4% of their assets per year on an inflation-adjusted basis (the assumption here is that retirement will last for 30 years). In other words, if a client retires with USD 5 million, they can spend 0.04 × USD 5 million = USD 200,000 in the first year of retirement. If inflation is 2% over the next year, the spending level would increase to 1.02 × USD 200,000 = USD 204,000 in the second year.

More comprehensive spending rules can be set:

1. Nondiscretionary vs. discretionary spending rule. This allocates portfolio income to nondiscretionary goals first, then discretionary goals, then uses any surplus for saving and reinvestment.
2. Fixed percentage allocation rule. This uses fixed percentages of income for spending and reinvestment.
3. Adaptive (dynamic) spending rule. This links spending levels to external conditions such as portfolio performance.
4. Spending limits rule. This sets floors and ceilings for spending levels such that essentials are met without overspending.

For instance, consider a client who has just retired with a USD 10 million investment portfolio that generates income of USD 500,000 per year. The client specifies that currently, her annual nondiscretionary expenses are USD 250,000, and her annual discretionary expenses are USD 100,000. See the following:

1. Nondiscretionary vs. discretionary spending rule
- Nondiscretionary spending = USD 250,000
- Discretionary spending = USD 100,000
- Remaining USD 150,000 of income can be used for further investments or gifting

2. Fixed percentage allocation rule
- Nondiscretionary spending % = USD 250,000 / USD 500,000 = 50%

76
- Discretionary spending % = USD 100,000 / USD 500,000 = 20%
- Remaining investment or gifting as a percentage of income = USD 150,000 / USD 500,000 = 30%

3. Adaptive spending rule based on performance
- Nondiscretionary spending = USD 250,000
- Discretionary spending begins at USD 100,000 and moves directly in line with performance in subsequent years (e.g., if the portfolio increases by 5%, discretionary spending will increase to 1.05 × USD 100,000 = USD 105,000)

4. Spending limits rules
- Establish annual spending floor of USD 250,000 to meet essential expenses
- Set a ceiling for total spending to avoid overspending (e.g., a ceiling of USD 450,000 would avoid the risk of depleting capital by spending all income of USD 500,000)

We end this reading with a comprehensive liquidity planning example.

EXAMPLE: Liquidity planning

Bonnie DuBois, a 60-year-old U.S. citizen, is working with her financial advisor to plan her retirement. Together, they produce the following figures.

Assets
- Primary residence valued at USD 4 million with no mortgage
- Investment portfolio valued at USD 5 million
- Family business currently valued at USD 10 million, of which DuBois is the sole owner

Income
- Current salary: USD 400,000
- Annual income from family foundation on retirement = USD 100,000, taxed as ordinary income
- Annual corporate pension: USD 200,000 starting in 5 years, taxed as ordinary income

Expenses (expected to increase by 3% per year)
- Current annual living expenses: USD 300,000
- Current annual payments to support children: USD 120,000

Goals
- Sell business and retire in the coming year
- Purchase retirement holiday home for USD 1 million
- Invest USD 1.5 million in music journalism business
- Establish an emergency liquidity reserve equal to one year of current expenses

77
- Return objective of the portfolio is to meet ongoing expenses and grow in line with inflation, which is expected to be 3%

## Tax rates

- Ordinary income tax rate = 40%
- Capital gains tax rate = 20% (assume family business has a tax basis [i.e., cost for tax purposes] of 0)

Given this information, complete the following:

1. Formulate the liquidity constraint for DuBois over the coming year (Year 1).
2. Calculate the required return of the portfolio for Year 2, assuming that the existing investment portfolio remains valued at USD 5 million at the end of Year 1.

## Answer:

1. The liquidity constraint for the coming year must include all external inflows and outflows for the investment portfolio.

### Inflows in coming year

- Pretax proceeds from sale of business = USD 10,000,000
- Pretax income from family foundation = USD 100,000

**Total inflows = USD 10,100,000**

### Outflows in coming year

- Capital gains tax liability on family business sale = 0.20 × USD 10,000,000 = USD 2,000,000
- Income tax on income from family foundation = 0.40 × USD 100,000 = USD 40,000
- DuBois's living expenses = USD 300,000 × 1.03 = USD 309,000
- Payment to support children = USD 120,000 × 1.03 = USD 123,600
- Purchase retirement holiday home: USD 1,000,000
- Invest in music journalism business: USD 1,500,000

**Total outflows = USD 4,972,600**

The total net inflows/outflows in the first year = USD 10,100,000 - USD 4,972,600 = USD 5,127,400 inflow.

We must also note under the liquidity constraint that DuBois has stated she wishes to set up a liquidity reserve equal to the current year's expenses of USD 300,000.

(If we were asked about significant liquidity events, we would note that DuBois will no longer receive her salary of USD 400,000 once she retires.)

2. The return objective of the portfolio is to meet ongoing expenses and grow in line with inflation, which is expected to be 3%.

78
At the end of the next year, the investment portfolio will be equal to USD 10,127,400, the value of the existing investment portfolio (USD 5,000,000, as we assumed this value did not change during the year) plus the net external inflow of USD 5,127,400.

The net spending required by the portfolio in Year 2 is calculated as expected expenses minus expected income.

Expected expenses in Year 2

- DuBois's living expenses = USD 300,000 × 1.03² = USD 318,270
- Payment to support children = USD 120,000 × 1.03² = USD 127,308

Total expenses = USD 318,270 + USD 127,308 = USD 445,578

Expected income in Year 2

After-tax income from family foundation = USD 100,000 × (1 - 0.40) = USD 60,000

The net spending required from the portfolio = USD 445,578 - USD 60,000 = USD 385,578

Then, the required return of the portfolio to meet spending = USD 385,578 / USD 10,127,400 = 3.8%

The portfolio also needs to generate the inflation rate of 3% to maintain purchasing power. Therefore, the required return of the portfolio in Year 2 = 3.8% + 3% = 6.8%.

## MODULE QUIZ W3.4

1. A financial advisor uses the 4% rule to set appropriate spending levels for a client. Which of the following statements is least accurate regarding this process?

A. The financial advisor implicitly assumes that the client will live for 30 years after retirement.
B. The process removes longevity risk for the client.
C. If the client has a retirement portfolio of USD 5 million and inflation is expected to be 5%, then nominal spending in the third year is expected to be USD 220,500.

2. The amount of liquidity risk that a private wealth client should take in their portfolio is typically inversely related to:

A. increasing wealth.
B. advancing age.
C. longer time horizons.

## KEY CONCEPTS

## LOS W3.a

Goals-based planning is executed in four steps:

1. Describe the goals of the client.
2. Quantify and prioritize goals.
3. Structure subportfolios to meet goals.
4. Manage the aggregate portfolio.

The goals of the client should be prioritized in the following order:

1. Nondiscretionary lifestyle spending—highest required probability of success (usually around 95%)
2. Discretionary nice-to-have lifestyle spending (probability of success: around 85%)
3. Discretionary aspirational lifestyle spending (probability of success: around 75%)
4. Philanthropy (probability of success: less than 70%)
5. Legacy/dynastic (probability of success: less than 70%)

The present value of each goal is calculated using appropriate discount rates, then a diversified subportfolio is allocated to meet each goal. The time horizon and priority of the goal drives the selection of the appropriate subportfolio. Short-term high-priority goals should have the safest subportfolio allocated to them. As time horizons increase and priority levels fall, the risk of the appropriate subportfolio increases.

## LOS W3.b

Comprehensive wealth planning considers the structure of investments, estate planning, tax liabilities, and risk management when constructing portfolios.

Internally managed assets (e.g., private real estate, family business, human capital) are run by members of the client's family. Externally managed assets are run by management external to the family (e.g., fund managers and family offices).

Asset structuring has three considerations:

1. Legal and physical location of assets
2. Tax, legal, and other obligations
3. Risk management (primarily focused on insurance policies)

The wealth allocation framework categorizes client assets as being in one of the following:

1. The safety portfolio. This is designed to meet nondiscretionary and short-term goals with minimal risk. This portfolio comprises cash and high-quality liquid fixed income.
2. The market portfolio. This is designed to meet longer-term lifestyle spending and has diversified market exposure.
3. The aspirational portfolio. This is designed to improve the standard of living for the client through significant gains (and is subject to significant potential losses). This portfolio contains the family business, investments in venture capital, leveraged real estate investments, and other concentrated high-risk positions.

## LOS W3.c

The following are general observations on tax efficiency:

- Dividend tax rates are often lower than capital gains—which, in turn, are often lower than ordinary income tax rates. Equity tends to be more tax efficient than fixed

80
income, which is taxed on periodic income.

- Regarding asset location, allocate tax-efficient assets (e.g., equity) to taxable accounts, and tax-inefficient assets (e.g., fixed income) to tax-exempt or tax-deferred accounts.
- Higher turnover strategies tend to be less tax efficient because they realize gains and associated capital gains tax sooner and pay a higher rate of tax.
- Tax loss harvesting (selling losing positions to realize losses that can be offset against realized gains) can improve tax efficiency, but:
- if sale proceeds are reinvested and asset prices recover, this only defers taxes rather than avoids them; and
- it may be disallowed under wash sale rules.

Aggressive tax avoidance, where structures and practices have no other legitimate purpose than avoiding tax, are likely unethical and possibly constitute illegal tax evasion.

Here are types of tax systems:
- A tax haven is a jurisdiction with low or no tax.
- A territorial tax system taxes only income that is earned within that jurisdiction.
- A worldwide tax system imposes taxes on all sources of income or gains, regardless of where they are earned.
- A citizenship-based tax system imposes taxes based on citizenship, regardless of where income is earned.
- In a flat tax system, the same tax rate is paid on all income. In a progressive tax system, the tax rate increases as the level of income increases.

Here are holding structures:
- Using a holding company for a foreign asset may allow the offsetting of expenses against income.
- Using a holding company located in a tax haven for a foreign asset may allow for the transfer of holding company ownership without triggering tax liabilities.

Information-sharing agreements for tax purposes include the Common Reporting Standard (CRS) of the OECD and the Foreign Account Tax Compliance Act (FATCA) in the United States.

## LOS W3.d

There are typical liquidity requirements of a private investor:
- Meeting short-term expenses in excess of income
- Holding an emergency cash reserve for unexpected adverse events
- Taking advantage of new investment opportunities
- Risk management of cash demands on the portfolio

Liquidity planning is balancing the tradeoff between earning a liquidity premium on illiquid assets and having enough liquid assets to meet cash requirements.

81
Income generation to provide liquidity can be provided by the following:

- Fixed-income investments (in particular, ladder strategies with bond maturities spread across maturities to minimize interest rate and reinvestment risk)
- Dividend-paying equity
- Income-generating alternative assets, such as real estate and infrastructure

The following are retirement spending rules:

- 4% rule: set initial spending at 4% of portfolio and increase in line with inflation
- Set spending equal to discretionary and nondiscretionary goals and reinvest any balance
- Set fixed percentages of income for spending
- Adapt spending levels according to a contingency, such as portfolio performance
- Set limits on minimum and maximum spending amounts

Formulating a liquidity constraint for an investor involves considering all sources of income, subtracting all ongoing expenses, and adjusting for significant one-off liquidity events. Any stated desire to hold an emergency cash reserve should also be noted in the liquidity constraint.

## ANSWER KEY FOR MODULE QUIZZES

### Module Quiz W3.1

1. A In goals-based planning, the selection of subportfolio is primarily driven by the term and the priority of the goal. Shorter-term goals with higher priority require safer portfolios to ensure the high required probability of success of the client is met. Dynastic goals, rather than philanthropic goals, have the highest risk tolerance; therefore, they will typically be met using the greatest risk. Because goals with similar term and priority can be met with the same subportfolio, there does not have to be an individual subportfolio for each goal. (LOS W3.a)
2. A GBP 500,000 is clearly stated as an essential, nondiscretionary goal required in 20 years. Only an incremental GBP 250,000 in 20 years should be recorded as discretionary aspirational (i.e., the difference between the GBP 1,000,000 required for the luxury residence and the GBP 750,000 required for a vacation home without renovation requirements). The advisor should also record a discretionary nice-to-have goal of GBP 250,000 in 20 years, representing the difference between GBP 750,000 required for a home with no renovation requirements and a basic home valued at GBP 500,000. Philanthropic goals generally rank after lifestyle spending goals, which would be the classification for the vacation home goal. (LOS W3.a)

### Module Quiz W3.2

1. A Externally managed assets are those managed by external fund managers or a family office. These assets are likely to be more liquid than internally managed
assets, which typically comprise the client's residence, family business, and human capital.

Both externally managed and internally managed assets can be used to meet either lifestyle goals or non-lifestyle dynastic goals. Key person risk (losing a key entrepreneurial decision maker driving the value of a business) is a risk with internally managed assets. While it might be evident in some external management teams, it is much more likely to be a key risk for internally managed assets run by the family members themselves. (LOS W3.b)

2. B The transaction cannot be deemed to be an arms-length one because the pre-existing relationship between the seller and the buyer has affected the transaction price and caused it to be different from the fair valuation of the business. (LOS W3.b)

## Module Quiz W3.3

1. A Capital gains taxes can be deferred, thereby decreasing the present value of taxes. Bonds and value stocks will pay more of their return periodically on interest and dividends. (LOS W3.c)

2. C The after-tax return from standard (taxable) corporate bonds would benefit from locating them in the TDA because their income return is generally taxed. Because the returns from tax-exempt bonds are not subject to taxation, there is no benefit in placing such bonds in a TDA. High-growth stocks pay small dividends and provide most of their return from capital gains. By extending the holding period, tax deferral will be possible—even in a taxable account. (LOS W3.c)

3. C If the stock is sold, there is a capital loss of USD 50,000 (USD 90,000 - USD 140,000), so combined with the USD 80,000 capital gain, there is a net capital gain of USD 30,000. The tax is 30% × USD 30,000 = USD 9,000. If the stock is not sold, the taxes on the full gain are USD 80,000 × 30% = USD 24,000. The recognition of the capital loss would result in a tax savings of USD 15,000 (USD 24,000 - USD 9,000).

The benefit from harvesting the loss can be calculated more quickly as the capital loss times the tax rate: USD 50,000 × 30% = USD 15,000. (LOS W3.c)

4. A The fixed-income assets are less tax efficient and should be held in the tax-exempt portfolio. Because the tax-exempt account is 50% of the portfolio and the desired fixed income allocation is 30%, the rest of the tax-exempt portfolio (20% of the overall portfolio) is allocated to equity. (LOS W3.c)

## Module Quiz W3.4

1. B Using the 4% rule does not remove the longevity risk of the client outliving their asset. The 4% rule assumes that the client will live for 30 years in retirement. A client with USD 5 million in retirement savings would spend 0.04 × USD 5,000,000 = USD 200,000 in the first year of retirement, USD 200,000 × 1.05 = USD 210,000

83
in the second year, and USD 210,000 × 1.05 = USD 220,500 in the third year of retirement. (LOS W3.d)

2. B Private clients can take more liquidity risk in their portfolio when they have the lowest liquidity needs from the portfolio. Liquidity needs are low when the client has a higher wealth level because nondiscretionary spending needs will be a smaller proportion of the portfolio. Clients typically need to increase liquidity and generate income from their portfolios as they age and approach retirement, and their time horizon shortens. Hence, liquidity risk is typically inversely related to age. (LOS W3.d)
