READING W4

INVESTMENT PLANNING

EXAM FOCUS

This reading takes a practical look at current techniques used in asset allocation, tax management, retirement spending estimation, and performance attribution. These overlap significantly with areas we have seen at Levels I and II of the CFA program or elsewhere in Level III. We will signpost these, but candidates should focus on the areas that are unique to this reading.

In the asset allocation section, we discuss techniques to assess client preferences and set capital market expectations. Focus on the key takeaways for fixed income and equity forecasting and be sure to know the mean-variance optimization (MVO) formula for the utility of a portfolio. In the taxation section, be sure to understand the impact of taxation on the utility of a portfolio. In the section on retirement spending, focus on the key ideas relating to funding ratio return and the power of compounding, and make sure you can apply the basic annuity formula. In the final section on performance measurement and attribution, pay careful attention to the new application of risk-adjusted return measures to logarithmic returns.

MODULE W4.1: ASSET ALLOCATION

LOS W4.a: Recommend and justify portfolio allocations and investments for a private client.

Video covering this content is available online.

As we have seen across all three levels of the CFA curriculum, an asset allocation process produces optimal asset class weights for a client's portfolio given the client's goals and attitude to risk, along with our asset class forecasts (capital market expectations). Well-known research literature from Ibbotson and Kaplan (2000) suggests that the long-term asset allocation of a portfolio is the primary driver of its risk and return.

Client Goals and Attitude to Risk

A financial advisor must assess a client's risk tolerance. Methods include the following:

- Risk tolerance questionnaires assessing general attitudes to risk. Behavioral finance warns us that clients may provide answers that do not truly reflect their attitudes, particularly in times of stress, and advisors must be careful not to allow their own views to sway their client's responses. The framing of the question is also known to

85
affect responses (e.g., focusing on losses instead of gains for identical choices can yield different responses).

- Ongoing conversations to help the advisor understand what influences and experiences shape the client's attitude to risk. Understanding how the client generated their wealth (passive saving vs. active entrepreneurship) and how the client perceives risk also helps the advisor to understand the client's risk tolerance. These conversations can also inform the client as to how taking risk can generate the returns they need to meet their goals.
- Goals-based planning to segregate the client's wealth into subportfolios (or mental accounts) designed to meet client goals. We cover this in detail in the Wealth Planning reading.
- Modeling and simulation. Traditional asset allocation models such Harry Markowitz's mean-variance optimization (MVO) are simple, static models that only consider the average period. By simulating potential future outcomes for the client and capturing the uncertainty of key factors such as inflation and investment returns on cash flows, we better understand the importance of client goals and how they measure risk relative to these goals. We also better understand the impact of estimation errors on our capital market forecasts.

# Formulating Capital Market Expectations

A financial advisor needs to form expectations for asset class returns, volatilities, and covariances between asset classes. Use of historical data for capital market expectations (CMEs) is usually a starting point, but it can be dangerous because history is no guide to the future.

For example, consider a two-year zero-coupon bond with a yield of 4% on an annual-pay basis, priced at 100 / 1.04² = 92.456. If we buy this bond today and hold it to maturity in two years, we will earn a holding period return of (100 - 92.456) / 92.456 = 8.16% over the two years.

Now, consider the potential price of the bond in one year, when it has one year left to maturity, under the following scenarios:

| YTM in One Year | Price in One Year | Return in Year 1 | Return in Year 2 | Total Return Over Two Years |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| 3% | 97.087 | 5.01% | 3.00% | 8.16% |
| 4% | 96.154 | 4.00% | 4.00% | 8.16% |
| 5% | 95.238 | 3.01% | 5.00% | 8.16% |

The first row tells us that if one-year yields one year from now turn out to be 3%, then the bond will be worth 97.087 (100 / 1.03), the return for the first year is 97.087 / 92.456 - 1 = 5.01%, and the return for the second year is expected to be 100 / 97.087 - 1 = 3%. If, in this situation, we lazily extrapolated the historical 5.01% in Year 1 to be expected in Year 2, we would be wrong (worse still, we could have improved our forecast through basic knowledge of the nature of the investment). The compounded return is always 8.16% over two periods, no matter what yields evolve to be in the second year, assuming we hold the bond to maturity and no default. This means that a
strong return in the first period implies a weak return in the second period (and vice versa).

We can extrapolate this idea to other asset classes. Strong equity price increases are likely to lead to lower future returns, unless growth rates in expected earnings and dividends increase.

## Estimating the Return of Fixed Income

For investment-grade (IG) bonds, to estimate the returns of a constant-duration bond portfolio (i.e., a portfolio that is subject to ongoing rebalancing to maintain a target duration), we can use a well-known research result: Over an investment horizon of twice the bond portfolio's constant modified duration, the bond portfolio's total return will approximately equal its initial yield.

For example, consider a portfolio with a constant modified duration of 8 and an initial yield of 5%. If we hold the portfolio over an investment horizon of 2 × 8 = 16 years, we will earn a total return close to 5%. If our investment horizon is much shorter or longer than 16 years, there is no such implication of having a return close to 5%.

Another way of putting this is that if we wish to minimize the dispersion of future realized total returns over a 16-year investment horizon, we should hold a fixed-income portfolio with a modified duration of 16 / 2 = 8.

## PROFESSOR'S NOTE

This result comes from research by Langetieg, Leibowitz, and Kogelman, published in Financial Analysts Journal (1990), Volume 46, Issue 5, which can be accessed through CFA Institute. You do not need to memorize names or dates; take the result at face value. Peruse the details of the report at your leisure after the exam.

For shorter-term duration indices and cash, returns (i.e., short-term rates) are generally expected to mirror inflation over the long term.

For high-yield (HY) bonds, we must forecast a probability of default and a loss given default and incorporate them into our forecast:

expected annual return of HY bonds = [(1 - PD) × YTM] - [PD × LGD]

where:

PD = annual probability of default of the HY bonds in the portfolio

YTM = yield to maturity of the HY bonds

LGD = loss given default (expressed as a positive number)

The first term represents the nondefaulting part of the portfolio earning the YTM of the portfolio. The second term represents the defaulting part of the portfolio suffering losses.

## EXAMPLE: Forecasting returns for a HY bond index

A bond index has a duration of 10 years, YTM of 6%, annual probability of default of 3%, and average loss given default of 65%.

87
Calculate the expected annualized return of the HY bond index.

Answer:

The expected annual return of HY bonds = $[(1 - 0.03) \times 6\%] - [0.03 \times 65\%] = 3.87\%$.

# Estimating the Return of Equity

Expected returns are harder to forecast for equity securities than for fixed-income securities due to their variable cash flows to shareholders (i.e., dividends paid out of earnings).

It is tempting to start equity forecasting by linking expected returns of equity to the expected growth rate in the economy, reasoning that economic growth drives growth in corporate earnings, which in turns drives growth in dividends. However, evidence in research literature suggests dividends of listed firms tend to grow more slowly than the economy over the long term (even after accounting for relatively recent increases in stock buybacks). This dilution effect can be attributed partly to high growth rates in unlisted equities that are not accessible through public securities.

&gt; **PROFESSOR'S NOTE**
&gt; The research paper here is Bernstein and Arnott's "Earnings Growth: The 2% Dilution," published in *Financial Analysts Journal* (2003), Volume 59, Issue 5, alongside more recent research papers. Once again, take the result at face value in the interests of time efficiency.

As we have seen before in the CFA curriculum, a simple re-arrangement of the Gordon growth model can solve for the fair expected return of a stock market as:


\text{expected return} = \text{forward dividend yield} + \text{growth of dividends}


Another technique that introduces valuation as a consideration is the cyclically adjusted price-to-earnings ratio (CAPE). The CAPE is a price-to-earnings ratio where earnings are inflation adjusted (to look at real earnings) and averaged over 10 years (to remove the temporary impact of the business cycle). A high value implies overvaluation and lower forward-looking returns, and vice versa.

# Estimating the Returns of Other Asset Classes

For other asset classes such as real assets, expected returns can be linked to fundamental return drivers (e.g., linking commodity futures prices to supply and demand factors driving spot prices) or mapping returns to a forecast for a well-established asset class such as public equity and adjusting.

# Estimating the Risk of Asset Classes

Measures of asset class risk typically start with estimating variance and covariance, creating forecasts by using shrinkage techniques applied to historical sample data. In simple terms, an analyst performs shrinkage by combining historical covariance

88
matrices with matrices reflecting their views on the future. This process is designed to reduce the (usually high) sampling error present in historical data.

Recall that for illiquid assets such as alternatives, the lack of regular marking to market leads to variances and covariances being underestimated. As when estimating returns, two approaches for estimating risk are from basic fundamentals, or by adjusting well-established asset class risk estimates such as public equity.

Consider a venture capital fund comprising 20 individual private equity investments, with 2x leverage. We can build up the fund risk using the standard deviation of a small-cap stock (say, 30%), adjusting for leverage to make a 60% volatility. Then, this is reduced through diversification (assuming independent returns) to $60\%/\sqrt{20} = 13.4\%$. We divide by the square root of the number of independent positions in the fund because we are technically combining the variances of the individual assets in the portfolio, then taking a square root to get to combined volatility. In reality, positions in a fund are unlikely to be truly identical and independent, but this calculation acts as a starting point to assess overall risk.

Other measures of risk commonly used are value at risk (VaR), expected shortfall (ES), and stress testing. We will reacquaint ourselves with VaR and ES later in this reading.

## Portfolio Optimization and Asset Allocation

The fundamental purpose of asset allocation is to identify portfolio weights that maximize the satisfaction (or utility) of the client with respect to meeting their goals subject to their risk tolerance, preferences, and constraints. One of the many ways to measure utility is Markowitz's mean-variance optimization (MVO), which uses the following formula:


\text{utility} = \text{portfolio expected return} - \frac{\gamma}{2} \times \text{portfolio variance}


where:

$\gamma =$ client's coefficient of risk aversion (a number typically between 1 and 10, where 10 is high risk aversion)

## PROFESSOR'S NOTE

We have met this utility function before at previous levels of the CFA program (though you may have seen $\gamma$ written as $\lambda$ or $A$). This simply states that the satisfaction a client gets from a portfolio is directly related to the portfolio's return and negatively related to the portfolio's variance, scaled up by the client's risk aversion level divided by two. The division by two is calibrates our model to the risks and returns we empirically observe in markets.

Common constraints imposed on the optimization include limits on shorting or leverage; maximum or minimum bounds for asset classes, geography, or sectors; and minimizing transaction costs.

In practice, MVO is often a theoretical starting point for a client's optimal portfolio. A client may express asset class preferences that the advisor can reflect by modifying the weights of the MVO portfolio. An advisor can also adjust the weights of asset classes to reflect their insights into expected asset class returns (overweighting/underweighting, according to view). Any modifications should consider the impact on the overall

89
portfolio's expected return and risk. The impact on portfolio risk of an asset class depends not only on the volatility of the asset class, but also the correlation of the asset class with the rest of the portfolio. An asset that appears to have low return and high risk may improve the risk-adjusted return of the portfolio if correlations with other assets are low enough.

## Asset-Liability Modeling and Surplus Management

Comprehensive wealth planning should consider all the assets and liabilities of the client (both explicit and implicit) in a holistic personal balance sheet. The difference between assets and liabilities is referred to as the client's net worth, discretionary wealth, equity, or surplus. The manager should, like a defined pension benefit fund manager, engage in asset-liability management (ALM) techniques to focus on investing client assets to maximize the utility of the surplus.

&gt; **PROFESSOR'S NOTE**
&gt; Comprehensive wealth planning is covered in the Wealth Planning reading.

Here are some points to note about this process:

1. A client's surplus is naturally subject to a leverage multiplier, in a similar way to corporate equity. For example, if client assets are EUR 7,000,000 and liabilities are estimated to be EUR 6,000,000, the client has a surplus of EUR 1,000,000 and a leverage multiplier of EUR 7,000,000 / EUR 1,000,000 = 7x. This means that a decrease in assets of EUR 1,000,000 / EUR 7,000,000 = 14.3% would reduce the surplus to zero. The probability of this happening can be assessed through making assumptions about the distribution of asset returns. For example, if the expected return of assets is 8% with volatility of 11%, and we assume returns are approximately normally distributed, then a 14.3% loss is $(-14.3\% - 8\%)$ / 11% ≈ 2 standard deviations away from the mean, and we can conclude that the probability of the surplus being reduced to zero in any given year is approximately 2.5%.

2. A client with a negative surplus can improve their net worth by increasing savings or reducing planned spending. Taking more risk with assets to increase returns is only acceptable if this is reasonable from a risk objective perspective.

3. An ALM focus will align the assets with the liabilities and decrease the volatility of the surplus.

4. The main liability of the client is likely to be the present value of planned spending, which is uncertain and likely to change over time. The client and advisor must commit to an ongoing review (at least annually) to assess the client's spending plans and adjust the portfolio if necessary.

Alongside ALM surplus optimization, cash flow modeling is an important part of investment planning. This is just as important for young families contributing cash into portfolios as they save for retirement as it is for older retired couples drawing on their portfolios to meet living expenses.

90
91

# MODULE QUIZ W4.1

1. Which of the following statements regarding forecasting fixed-income returns is least accurate?

A. To minimize dispersion in the realized return of a constant-duration, investment-grade fixed-income portfolio, investors should target a modified duration equal to their investment horizon.

B. Assets that exhibit above-average returns are subsequently expected to exhibit below-average returns unless the cash flows from the asset increase above original expectations.

C. The return from a high-yield fixed-income portfolio can be decomposed into the performing bonds in the portfolio earning the portfolio YTM and the nonperforming part of the portfolio suffering the loss given default.

2. Which of the following statements regarding the forecasting of asset class risks and returns is most accurate?

A. Equity dividend growth rates should be assumed to grow in line with GDP.

B. Returns to equity indices are easier to forecast than returns to fixed-income indices.

C. Shrinkage estimators can generate covariance matrix forecasts that incorporate both historical samples and a matrix based on the views of the analyst performing the forecast.

# MODULE W4.2: TAX MANAGEMENT

LOS W4.b: Discuss the tax efficiency of investment across various asset types and recommend various tax management strategies for asset allocation.

Video covering this content is available online.

We saw in the previous section that the utility of a portfolio is given by the expression:


\text{utility} = \text{portfolio expected return} - \frac{\gamma}{2} \times \text{portfolio variance}


For taxable investors, asset allocation should be performed on a posttax basis. For an effective tax rate of $tx$, we can write:


\begin{array}{l}
\text{posttax expected return} = \text{pretax expected return} \times (1 - tx) \\
\text{posttax standard deviation} = \text{pretax standard deviation} \times (1 - tx) \\
\text{posttax variance} = \text{pretax variance} \times (1 - tx)^2
\end{array}


Substituting these posttax expressions into the formula for utility gives:


\begin{array}{l}
\text{utility} = \text{pretax expected return} \times (1 - tx) - \frac{\gamma}{2} \times \text{pretax variance} \times (1 - tx)^2 \\
= (1 - tx)\left[\text{pretax expected return} - \frac{\gamma}{2} \times \text{pretax variance} \times (1 - tx)\right]
\end{array}


The impact of taxes is, therefore, to multiply the entire utility function by $(1 - tx)$, and to multiply the final variance term of the expression by $(1 - tx)$. Remember that the goal of MVO is to set portfolio weights to maximize this utility. This is unaffected by multiplying the entire utility function through by $(1 - tx)$; we will still have the same utility-maximizing weights as we did before taxes were considered.
However, the multiplication of the final variance term by another factor of $(1 - \mathrm{tx})$ has an interesting impact on the optimization. Essentially, this term reduces the impact of variance on utility relative to return and implies that the investor has become less risk averse after considering taxes. This is because the tax authorities participate in the returns of the portfolio—and in doing so, they absorb some of the risk of the portfolio, giving the investor the ability to be less risk averse.

## General Taxation Considerations

Tax laws in different jurisdictions around the world are complex and varied. However, we can apply some general rules when constructing a tax-efficient portfolio:

- To encourage long-term investment, tax rates on long-term capital gains are usually lower than those on short-term gains. Dividends are often taxed as ordinary income, but in some countries, there is a minimum holding period, after which the tax rate decreases and the dividends are classified as **qualifying dividends**.

- For fixed-income securities, amortization of a premium or discount to par is generally taxed as interest income in the period earned. An exception to this rule is U.S. municipal bonds, where coupon interest is exempt from taxation, but discounts are taxed as capital gains.

- The taxation of derivatives is specific to the nature of the contract, and sometimes is related to whether the underlying position is also held.

- Conventional fiat currency gains and losses in the spot market are typically taxed as ordinary gains and losses.

- Cryptocurrency taxation rules have not yet been fully developed in many jurisdictions. Gains and losses are often taxed in a similar way to property such as real estate.

- Real estate income and capital gains are taxed in a similar fashion to other investment returns. Property taxes may be payable to local authorities, though this is often deductible against taxable income, as is depreciation. On sale, tax authorities may require tax to be paid on accumulated depreciation that was not actually realized in market value (referred to as **depreciation recapture**). Capital improvements in the property can affect both capital gains and depreciation recapture calculations.

- Pooled investment funds like mutual funds, hedge funds, and limited partnerships generally pass through tax liabilities to investors. For a limited partnership, carried interest paid to the general partner is taxed as capital gains.

- A trust that is set up to hold assets may be recognized as an independent tax entity, depending on the tax laws (though trusts typically have progressive tax bands with tax rates that increase at lower income levels than for individuals).

- Wash sale rules may disallow a tax loss if a similar security is bought within a short time frame (in the United States, this is within 30 days before or after the sale of identical securities).

- Tax treaties may be available to investors with foreign investments to decrease the impact of double taxation due to foreign withholding taxes. Cross-border investing is

92
complex, and sometimes can have an adverse effect (e.g., investors domiciled in a different country to a tax-advantaged account may find themselves subject to taxes on the returns in the account from their domestic tax authorities).

- Alternative minimum tax (AMT) rules may exist, which require wealthy individuals to pay at least a minimum amount of tax. In effect, these rules could disallow certain tax-advantaged asset returns (e.g., municipal bond returns) or remove the tax-advantaged status of an investment structure.

# Centralized Portfolio Management

Research has proposed that private wealth clients would benefit from a centralized dealing function to reduce costs and increase tax efficiency and general portfolio management efficiency. Under such a system, all trades conducted on behalf of the client are directed through a central trading desk operated similar to the centralized dealing desks of buy-side fund management firms. Such an approach could decrease taxes by improving the timing of realized capital gains, while decreasing turnover in family portfolios.

Here are some points on managing tax efficiency for a family:

- Where assets are held in a joint account, consider wash sale rules across the trades of both account holders.
- Positions may have been built up over time at different entry prices (referred to as tax lots). The investor should optimize the disposition of tax lots to be tax efficient (if possible, allocate the highest-cost tax lot first to defer capital gains into the future).
- Determine tax drag, which is the impact of taxes on investment returns due to a potential trade, and include it in the portfolio optimization process.
- Different family members may be in different tax brackets, which may affect their optimal trade from a tax perspective.
- Ensure that the aggregate optimal trades for individual and joint accounts align with the family investment policy at the broad family portfolio level.
- The family should work with the financial advisor to forecast how the optimal asset allocation may change over time, given foreseeable events in the lives of the family (e.g., retirement or expected inheritance). Potential tax benefits from forecasting changes in the circumstances of the family include the following:

- Deferring capital gain realization and taxes when risk tolerance levels are forecast to increase
- Avoiding illiquid asset classes that would cause liquidity, portfolio turnover, and adverse tax issues when the client retires and requires more liquidity from their investment portfolio

# Tax-Efficient Portfolio Construction: Direct Indexing vs. Portfolio Customization

Direct indexing is a passive investment strategy aiming to incorporate tax-aware strategies (e.g., tax loss harvesting) so that the portfolio outperforms the benchmark on an after-tax basis.

93
The portfolio customization approach to tax efficiency prioritizes the client's goals, including making tax-efficient allocations and trading decisions, rather than focusing on outperforming a benchmark.

## Dealing With Concentrated Positions

An investor with a large, concentrated position typically has the need to diversify, but the investor would also trigger a large capital gains tax liability, should they sell the concentrated position. One solution is to run a fully diversified complementary portfolio to which the concentrated position can be transitioned over time. Any losses generated in the portfolio can be used to offset capital gains from the concentrated position, thereby mitigating capital gains tax liabilities. If the client does not have significant losses in the complementary portfolio to offset capital gains in the concentrated position, they are presumably making strong returns across their entire portfolio, putting them in a healthy overall position to meet the taxes due.

## Taxable Portfolio Optimization

As stated earlier, the portfolio optimization process should be performed on an after-tax basis.

The returns of the portfolio that drive tax liabilities can be broken down into two different types:

1. There are uncertain returns in the future (that have variance).
2. There are known past returns that have led to unrealized capital gains tax liabilities. These past returns are not uncertain; therefore, they do not affect variance. We can adjust the tax-aware utility function by subtracting these known capital gains tax liabilities from the expected return of the portfolio.

A taxable portfolio optimization must be aware of the tax issues we have discussed related to the following:

- Minimizing short-term capital gains at high tax rates
- Tax loss harvesting while tracking and preventing wash sales
- Tracking qualified versus unqualified dividends
- Differentiating between different tax lots and optimizing tax lot disposition when realizing capital gains
- How turnover (trading activity) in the portfolio affects tax efficiency (a tax-aware optimization is likely to recommend significantly lower turnover in the portfolio to defer capital gains taxes)

## MODULE QUIZ W4.2

1. Which of the following statements regarding a tax-aware utility function is most accurate?

A. The tax-aware utility is reduced by a factor of (1 - tax rate).
B. The impact of considering taxes effectively decreases the risk aversion level of the investor.
C. The optimal portfolio is the same using a tax-aware utility function as it would be using a tax-unaware utility function.
2. Which of the following statements regarding tax-aware investment planning is most accurate?

A. Under the direct indexing approach to tax-efficient investing, the client's portfolio aims to match the index on a pretax basis, but outperform the index on a posttax basis.
B. A complementary portfolio is used to hedge the nonsystematic risk of a concentrated position in a client's portfolio that cannot be sold immediately due to large capital gains liabilities.
C. Alternative minimum tax rules can be used to decrease the tax drag of a taxable private wealth client's portfolio.

# MODULE W4.3: RETIREMENT

Video covering this content is available online.

LOS W4.c: Discuss and recommend appropriate wealth management planning approaches for retirement from legal, taxation, and jurisdictional perspectives.

Financial planning for retirement involves two distinct stages: the accumulation stage (preretirement) and the decumulation stage (retirement). In the accumulation stage, funds are saved toward a target value based on estimated spending needs in retirement and bequest desires. In the decumulation stage, funds are spent down. Key risks in the decumulation stage are inflation affecting spending levels and the longevity of the client (i.e., the client living longer than expected).

A key metric to assess a client's preparedness for retirement is their funding ratio, defined as:


\text{funding ratio} = \frac{\text{assets}}{\text{liabilities}}


If we base the funding ratio on the extended balance sheet of the family, including both explicit and implicit assets and liabilities (in particular, the present value of spending in retirement and desired bequests), then we can say a funding ratio greater than 1 implies that a family has positive net worth and is saving adequately for retirement. A funding ratio less than 1 implies that the client needs to improve their income or savings, earn higher investment returns, or decrease their desired spending levels.

The funding ratio return is simply the percentage change in the funding ratio during a period:


\text{funding ratio return} = \frac{\text{funding ratio}_{\text{END}}}{\text{funding ratio}_{\text{START}}} - 1


Through a little algebra, this can be restated as:


\text{funding ratio return} = \frac{\text{asset return} - \text{liability return}}{1 + \text{liability return}}


To comprehensively measure the change in the funding ratio, the ending asset value should add contributions to the portfolio and remove withdrawals. Similarly, the ending liability value should increase by liabilities incurred during a period and decrease by
liabilities removed during the period. Hence, the asset return and liability return used in the equation just listed are not purely investment returns in the conventional sense, because they include these adjustments to principal.

A useful feature of the funding ratio is that it does not depend on the client's absolute level of wealth; therefore, it is comparable across clients. Two clients with the same asset and liability returns will have the same funding ratio return, regardless of the level of the funding ratio.

## Saving for Retirement

The power of compounding when saving over the long term can be approximated by the Rule of 72, which states that money invested at a rate of $r$ per year doubles in value after approximately $72 / r$ years. For example, an investor saving at a rate of $5\%$ per year will double their money in approximately $72 / 5 = 14.4$ years (note that $1.05^{14.4} = 2.02$).

The importance of starting to save for retirement early in life is seen from the fact that an investor making regular contributions to their savings account will generate approximately half of their terminal wealth from contributions made in the first $72 / r$ years of saving. This is demonstrated in the following example.

## EXAMPLE: Saving for retirement

A private wealth client is planning to save for retirement on their 65th birthday. They intend to contribute USD 1,000 at the start of each year to a savings account earning $5\%$ per year.

1. Calculate the account value on retirement if the client starts saving on their 24th birthday (start of Year 25).
2. Calculate the account value on retirement if the client starts saving on their 34th birthday (start of Year 35).
3. Calculate the annual savings required if the client wishes to have the same terminal wealth calculated in Question 1, but starts saving on their 34th birthday, as in Question 2.
4. Demonstrate that approximately half the terminal wealth is generated in the first $72 / 5 = 14$ years (rounded to the nearest whole year) for the situation in Question 1.

## Answer:

1. Using the TVM keys of the financial calculator, we calculate the future value of an annuity due (i.e., an annuity where payments happen at the start of periods) as follows:

$2^{\text{nd}}$ PMT $2^{\text{nd}}$ ENTER (sets calculator to BGN mode to deal with annuities due)

41 N; 5 I/Y; 0 PV; -1,000 PMT; CPT FV = 134,232

2. Using the same process with a different number of years, assuming the calculator remains in BGN mode we calculate as follows:

96
31 N; 5 I/Y; 0 PV; 1,000 PMT; CPT FV = 74,299

By delaying the start of the saving by only 10 years (approximately a quarter of the original saving period), the client loses nearly half of their potential terminal wealth.

3. If the client wishes to have a terminal wealth of USD 134,232 but does not start saving until their 34th birthday, the annual savings amount is calculated as follows (BGN mode):

31 N; 5 I/Y; 0 PV; 134,232 FV; CPT PMT: -1,807

4. The first 14 years of payments have a present value given by the following (BGN mode):

14 N; 5 I/Y; -1,000 PMT; 0 FV; CPT PV = 10,394

This is the present value when the client starts saving at the start of Year 25 (i.e., at time 24). Compounding this forward to retirement in 41 years gives a terminal wealth of USD 10,394(1.05)⁴¹ = USD 76,829.

The proportion of the terminal wealth that comes from contribution in the first 14 years is USD 76,829 / USD 134,232 = 57% (close to the approximation of 50%).

## PROFESSOR'S NOTE

Take care when counting the number of years. If the client is making contributions at the start of each year and they begin in their 25th year, the first payment occurs at time 24 (on their 24th birthday) and continues until their final year of working, when they make a contribution at time 64 (the start of Year 65). We are assuming here that no final payment occurs on the retirement date, because this is when decumulation begins.

## Decumulation

Successful decumulation in retirement involves using the client's retirement wealth to meet spending and bequest desires without oversaving or undersaving. This simple idea is often hard to achieve in practice due to uncertainty in forecasting spending rates, inflation, investment returns, and the client's lifespan. Health span (how long a client will live without the need for ongoing medical care) is also an important factor. The decumulation issue is more pronounced for women, who are expected to live longer than men, yet generally have poorer health at a more advanced age.

Social Security plans provided by governments and defined benefit pensions operated by employers are designed to address the decumulation issue by providing known payments for as long as the retiree is alive. These plans have suffered from increasing liabilities due to increases in life expectancy and aging populations. Many defined benefit plans have been closed to new members while state pension schemes are struggling to maintain funded status.

Those without adequate Social Security or defined benefit plans have two main approaches to decumulation:

1. Regular withdrawals from a retirement portfolio. Manage a portfolio in retirement that is expected to generate a positive real return, which is spent down steadily over
the client's lifespan. The client is still exposed to the risk of poor investment performance, longevity risk, and inflation risk elevating spending levels.
2. Annuitization. Purchase an annuity from an insurer, paying an initial lump sum in return for a subsequent stream of payments over the investor's remaining life. This method exposes the investor to the credit risk of the annuity provider.

## Regular Withdrawals

When using regular withdrawals, decumulation rules that have been suggested in research include the following:

- Base withdrawals on a stated percentage of portfolio assets. Assuming a lifespan of 30 years in retirement and a 50/50 equity/bond portfolio, withdraw 4% in the year of retirement and adjust this withdrawal for inflation going forward. This rule has become the conventional starting point for decumulation advice. Another study including non-U.S. data over a different time frame suggests initial withdrawal rates should be lower, at 2.26%. A good general rule for withdrawal rates is that they should not be above the real return of the portfolio, to avoid the risk of capital depletion.

## PROFESSOR'S NOTE

We met this first result in the Wealth Planning reading, where it was referred to as the 4% rule.

- Base withdrawals on remaining life expectancy. Set withdrawals to the value of the portfolio divided by the remaining life expectancy of the client (subject to a maximum of 20% to manage longevity risk).

## Annuitization

Annuities are designed to manage longevity risk. Payments from the insurance company providing the annuity continue until the investor's death. However, they have historically been unpopular due to high fees and exposure to the credit risk of the annuity provider. Annuity providers also faced adverse selection, in that investors more likely to live longer were more inclined to purchase annuities. This caused annuity prices to be high.

In more recent times, increases in the popularity of annuities have mitigated adverse selection issues, and the products have become more attractively priced. Deferred annuities, with payments starting at a future date, have also emerged. Investors can use these (usually cheap) annuities to manage their longevity risk. An investor with a deferred annuity that starts at age 85 could spend down their portfolio up to that age, knowing that if they live longer, the deferred annuity will provide payments until their death.

For a finite annuity over $N$ years with growth rate in payments of $g$, we can state:

\text{initial payment} = \frac{\text{lump sum} \times (\mathrm{E}[\mathrm{r}] - \mathrm{g})}{1 - \left(\frac{1 + \mathrm{g}}{1 + \mathrm{E}[\mathrm{r}]}\right)^{\mathrm{N}}}


where:

initial payment = first annuity payment to the investor at the end of the first year

lump sum = price of the annuity, paid at the start of the first year by the investor

$\mathrm{E}[\mathrm{r}] =$ expected return on the invested lump sum (effectively a discount rate applied to annuity payments)

## PROFESSOR'S NOTE

This formula is a little intimidating when we first see it, and there is not an easy way to navigate it using a financial calculator, but it is not actually used in any calculation in the curriculum. Just be sure to familiarize yourself with the simpler formula that follows.

When the growth rate $g$ is equal to the required return $E[r]$, the expression just listed does not produce a meaningful result. In this special case, it simplifies to:


\text{initial payment} = \frac{\text{lump sum} \times (1 + \mathrm{E}[\mathrm{r}])}{\mathrm{N}}


## PROFESSOR'S NOTE

This is a simpler and more intuitive expression. When $g = E[r]$, any growth in cash flows is discounted out of the cash flow's present value. We know, therefore, that the present value at time 1 of any subsequent payment is simply equal to the initial payment, implying that the present value of all payments at time 1 is equal to $N \times$ the initial payment. This means that the lump sum (i.e., present value at time 0) = $(N \times$ initial payment) / $(1 + E[r])$, and the result just listed quickly follows.

## EXAMPLE: The power of compounding

A private wealth client expects to have retirement savings at age 65 of USD 134,232, and to live for 25 years in retirement. She intends to purchase an annuity on her retirement date to fund her spending in retirement, and she expects returns of $3\%$ and a growth rate in cash flows of $3\%$. Calculate the following:

1. The amount the investor expects to receive at the end of the 1st year of retirement
2. The amount the investor expects to receive at the end of the 25th year of retirement

## Answer:

1. Using the formula just listed:


\text{initial payment} = \frac{\text{lump sum} \times (1 + \mathrm{E}[\mathrm{r}])}{\mathrm{N}} = \frac{\text{USD 134,232} \times (1.03)}{25} = \text{USD 5,530}


2. The amount received in the 25th year (24 years of growth between the payment in Year 1 and the payment in Year 25) = USD 5,530 × (1.03)$^{24}$ = USD 11,242.

99
# Innovations in Retirement Planning

Investors who are driven by the fear of outliving their savings tend to oversave, while investors who fail to understand the power of compounding and the need to start saving early for retirement tend to underscore. The decumulation problem has become more of an issue for investors generally in recent times due to the closure of defined benefit pensions and the movement toward defined contribution plans, where investors are not promised a defined payment in retirement.

Innovations in retirement planning that help individuals save and accumulate efficiently are built on the following three key ideas:

1. Longevity risk is more easily managed for a group of investors than it is for a single investor. Diversification allows institutions to offer attractively priced products to pools of investors. While research suggests that, above age 75, longevity risk is greater than the market risk of equities, insurance companies may be able to offer cost-efficient deferred annuities to manage this longevity risk.
2. Institutions generally are more cost efficient than individuals in managing investments.
3. Some risks naturally hedge each other. For example, longevity risk linked to retirement payments is negatively correlated with long-term care costs because retirees in poor health tend to take fewer retirement payments but require more long-term care.

Three recent innovations in retirement security are as follows:

1. Modern tontines. These are pooled annuities where a group of individuals each pay a lump sum into a portfolio, and members receive annuity payments for the rest of their lives. As pool members die, their share of the portfolio is distributed to their dependents.
2. Retirement security bonds. These provide a deferred inflation-adjusted income stream beginning later in the investor's life (e.g., when they turn age 65). These have been successfully implemented by the government of Brazil.
3. Combined offsetting insurance. This provides both long-term care insurance and retirement income. Because these risks are naturally hedged for the insurance company, products can be attractively priced.

## MODULE QUIZ W4.3

1. Two investors, Pedro and Paulo, have the same retirement spending liabilities and the same portfolio asset allocation of 50% stocks and 50% bonds. Pedro has a funding ratio greater than 1, and Paulo has a funding ratio of less than 1. If stocks fall and bonds rally, it is most likely that:

A. Pedro's funding ratio return will exceed Paulo's funding ratio return.
B. Paulo's funding ratio return will exceed Pedro's funding ratio return.
C. Pedro and Paulo will have the same funding ratio return for the period.

2. A corporate employer is considering providing deferred annuity payments to its workforce with the following details:

- Should the employee reach age 85, the employer will provide payments of USD 20,000 per year at the end of each of the following 10 years.
- The interest rate used to price the annuity is 3%.
- There is 50% of the workforce that is expected to live to age 85.
- Long-term interest rates are 5%.

The approximate cost to the company of providing the benefit to a 30-year-old employee (assuming they live for the full 10 years from age 85) is closest to:

A. USD 5,276.
B. USD 5,828.
C. USD 11,657.

# MODULE W4.4: PERFORMANCE EVALUATION AND ATTRIBUTION

Video covering this content is available online.

LOS W4.d: Evaluate the success of an investment program for a private client based on portfolio reporting and review.

Performance evaluation comprises two parts: (1) performance measurement, which is the calculation of a particular type of return for a specific period; and (2) performance appraisal, which is the identification of the sources of return.

## Performance Measures Based on Return

When assessing the active return of a portfolio relative to a benchmark, the benchmark must be relevant to the market, asset class, and currency of the portfolio. For a portfolio returning $R_{\mathrm{p}}$ in a period, while its benchmark returns $R_{\mathrm{B}}$ and the risk-free rate is $R_{\mathrm{f}}$, we can state some basic definitions:

- Arithmetic active return for the period $= R_{\mathrm{P}} - R_{\mathrm{B}}$
- Excess return $= R_{\mathrm{P}} - R_{\mathrm{f}}$
- Logarithmic active return $= \ln (1 + R_{\mathrm{P}}) - \ln (1 + R_{\mathrm{B}})$
- Logarithmic excess return $= \ln (1 + R_{\mathrm{P}}) - \ln (1 + R_{\mathrm{f}})$

## PROFESSOR'S NOTE

Recall that these logarithmic returns are simply continuously compounded returns. They are often preferred in financial modeling because when we take arithmetic averages over multiple periods, the effect of compounding is preserved.

We must also consider the difference between arithmetic and geometric average returns. For example, a fund that gains $30\%$ in the first period and loses $30\%$ in the second period has an average arithmetic return of $(30\% - 30\%) / 2 = 0\%$, but its average geometric return is $\sqrt{1.3 \times 0.7} - 1 = -4.6\%$. This geometric average return reflects the impact of compounding and shows that volatility causes geometric average returns to be lower than arithmetic average returns over multiple periods. This means that a portfolio could underperform its benchmark on a geometric average return basis, even when the arithmetic average returns of the portfolio and the benchmark are the same. (For example, if the benchmark for the portfolio above returned $+10\%$ and $-10\%$,
its arithmetic average would be the same as the portfolio $[0\%]$ , but its geometric average would be higher at $\sqrt{1.1 \times 0.9} - 1 = -0.5\%$ .

Note that the average arithmetic logarithmic return, which reflects compounding, would show the portfolio return to be deficient relative to the benchmark, as per the following table:

| Period | Rp | Rg | Ln(1 + Rp) | Ln(1 + Rg) |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| 1 | 30% | 10% | 26.24% | 9.53% |
| 2 | -30% | -10% | -35.67% | -10.54% |
| Cumulative two-period return | -9% | -1% | -9.4% | -1.0% |
| Arithmetic average | 0% | 0% | -4.7% | -0.5% |

Note that the cumulative two-period return is geometrically compounded for actual returns such as for $\mathrm{R_p}$ : $-9\% = (1.3 \times 0.7) - 1$ , but is simply added for continuously compounded returns (e.g., $\mathrm{R_p}$ : $-9.4\% = 26.24\% - 35.67\%$ ).

If a benchmark is well specified, active returns should come from a stable distribution without serial correlation—reflecting the fact that active positions are relatively idiosyncratic in nature, not systemic. In other words, if the manager seems to be earning active returns (i.e., returns greater than the benchmark) from systemic sources, the benchmark is likely misspecified because it should already represent systemic return (i.e., passive returns earned from exposure to risk factors).

# Risk-Adjusted Measures of Performance

Recall the well-known risk-adjusted return measures we have met many times before in the CFA program: the Sharpe ratio (excess return relative to the standard deviation of returns) and the information ratio (active return relative to the standard deviation of active returns). Just as logarithmic returns were useful to assess the impact of compounding for returns, they can be useful when calculating risk-adjusted return measures. We define the logarithmic Sharpe ratio and logarithmic information ratio as follows:

```txt
logarithmic Sharpe ratio $=$ mean(excess logarithmic returns) standard deviation(excess logarithmic return)
```

```txt
logarithmic information ratio $=$ mean(active logarithmic returns) standard deviation(active logarithmic return)
```

# EXAMPLE: Logarithmic risk-adjusted return measures

Continuing with our sample data used earlier for a portfolio return versus its benchmark is the following:
| Period | Rp | Rg | Lu (1 + Rp) | Lu (1 + Rg) | Lu (1 + Rg) | Active Return | Active Log Return | Excess Return | Excess Log Return |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| 1 | 30% | 10% | 26.24% | 9.5% | 2.96% | 20% | 16.7% | 27% | 23.3% |
| 2 | -30% | -10% | -35.67% | -10.5% | 2.96% | -20% | -25.1% | -33% | -38.6% |
| Arithmetic mean | 0% | 0% | -4.7% | -0.5% | 2.96% | 0% | -4.2% | -3% | -7.7% |
| Standard deviation | 42.4% | 14.1% | 43.8% | 14.2% | 0.0% | 28.3% | 29.6% | 42.4% | 43.8% |

Assuming a periodic risk-free rate of $3\%$ , calculate the following:

1. The Sharpe ratio and the logarithmic Sharpe ratio for the portfolio
2. The information ratio and the logarithmic information ratio for the portfolio

# Answer:

1. Sharpe ratio = mean excess return / standard deviation of portfolio = -3% / 42.4% = -0.07

Logarithmic Sharpe ratio = mean excess log return / standard deviation of excess log returns = -7.7% / 43.8% = -0.176

The logarithmic Sharpe ratio, which accurately reflects the impact of compounding, is much lower than the Sharpe ratio based on actual returns.

2. Information ratio = mean active return / standard deviation of active return = 0% / 28.3% = 0

Logarithmic information ratio = mean active log return / standard deviation of active log returns =

-4.2% / 29.6% = -0.14

The logarithmic information ratio, which accurately reflects the impact of compounding, is much worse than the information ratio based on actual returns.

Calculations like those performed in this example are often done using monthly data. To scale up a return-risk metric from monthly to annual, we multiply the numerator by 12 to move from monthly returns to annual returns and scale up the volatility measure in the denominator by the square root of 12 (under the assumption that monthly returns are identical and independent). Thus, we scale the whole risk-adjusted measure up by a factor of $\frac{12}{\sqrt{12}} = \frac{\sqrt{12}}{12}$ to move from a monthly to an annual statistic.

The risk-adjusted metrics just described assume that standard deviation is the appropriate measure for risk. An issue with standard deviation is that it measures average distance from the mean without regard for whether returns are above or below the mean. Other methods for measuring risk focus on losses (i.e., returns below the mean or below a target return). Some of these measures, which we have met before in the CFA program, include the following:

- Value at risk (VaR). The VaR of a portfolio is a loss that is expected to occur with a stated frequency over a specified time frame. For example, if a portfolio has a $95\%$ VaR of USD 10 million over two weeks, we conclude there is a $5\%$ probability that portfolio losses will exceed USD 10 million over a two-week time frame.
- Expected shortfall (ES). Also known as conditional VaR, this measures the expected loss when VaR is exceeded. In other words, it answers the question, "What is the average loss in the 5% of times when two-week losses exceed USD 10 million?" If two portfolios have similar VaR, the one with the higher ES has the most negatively skewed distribution of returns.
- Semistandard deviation. This is a standard deviation calculated by setting all positive deviations above the mean to zero, thereby focusing on negative deviations (or downside).
- Sortino ratio. This is a similar measure to the Sharpe ratio, but it bases excess returns on returns above a stated minimum acceptable return (MAR), and it uses a volatility measure based on semideviation below the MAR. Just as for the Sharpe ratio, there is an analogous ratio for the Sortino ratio based on logarithmic returns, referred to as the logarithmic Sortino ratio:


\text {Sortino ratio} = \frac {\text {mean (return)} - \text {MAR}}{\text {semideviation (return)} - \text {MAR}}

\begin{array}{l} \text {logarithmic Sortino ratio} = \\ \frac {\text {mean (logarithmic return)} - (\text {logarithmic MAR})}{\text {semideviation (logarithmic return} - \text {logarithmic MAR})} \end{array}


# Accounting-Based Performance Attribution

The Brinson-Hood-Beebower (or accounting-based) attribution model breaks portfolio active return down into three components:

1. The allocation effect measures the active return impact of a portfolio manager overweighting or underweighting sectors or asset classes relative to the benchmark:


\text {allocation effect} = \sum (\text {active sector overweight or underweight} \times \text {benchmark sector or asset class return})


2. The selection effect measures the active return impact of a portfolio manager selecting securities that are different to the benchmark:


\text {selection effect} = \sum (\text {weight of benchmark} \times \text {active sector or asset class return})


3. The interaction effect measures the interaction of the first two components. If a manager is a good stock picker and overweights those sectors, then the manager will exhibit a positive interaction effect:


\text {interaction effect} = \sum (\text {active sector overweight or underweight} \times \text {active sector or asset class return})


# EXAMPLE: Accounting-based attribution

A private wealth client currently has an allocation of 60% stocks and 40% bonds, holding only passive indexed portfolios. A financial advisor recommends that the client move to a 75%/25% stock/bond allocation and invest in actively managed funds. Relevant performance data for the following year are as follows:

- Stock index: +8%
- Bond index: -2%
- Actively managed stock fund: +9%
- Actively managed bond index: $-3\%$

Assuming that the client followed the financial advisor’s advice, relative to maintaining the client’s current portfolio allocation, calculate the following:

1. Allocation effect
2. Selection effect
3. Interaction effect

Answer:

1. Allocation effect = $\sum$(active sector overweight or underweight $\times$ benchmark sector or asset class return)

Following the advisor’s recommendation gives an allocation effect of:


[ (0.75 - 0.60) \times 8\% ] + [ (0.25 - 0.40) \times -2\% ] = 1.5\%


The advisor generates $1.5\%$ of active return from overweighting stocks and underweighting bonds.

2. Selection effect = $\sum$(weight of benchmark $\times$ active sector or asset class return)

Following the advisor’s recommendation gives a selection effect of:


[ 0.60 \times (9\% - 8\% ) ] + [ 0.40 \times (-3\% - (-2\% ) ] = 0.2\%


The advisor generates $0.2\%$ of active return from their selection of actively managed funds. While the equity manager outperformed, the bond manager underperformed the passive index. Therefore, the only reason we have a positive selection effect is because of the higher weight for equities in the benchmark.

3. Interaction effect = $\sum$(active sector overweight or underweight $\times$ active sector or asset class return)

Following the advisor’s recommendation gives an interaction effect of:


[ (0.75 - 0.60) \times (9\% - 8\% ) ] + [ (0.25 - 0.40) \times (-3\% - (-2\% ) ] = 0.3\%


The advisor generates $0.3\%$ of active return from the interaction of overweighting equity and being good at selecting fund managers in the equity asset class. Being underweight bonds and poor at selecting managers in the bond asset class also have a positive contribution to the interaction effect.

The portfolio return before the advisor’s recommended changes (effectively, the benchmark) is $(0.60 \times 8\%) + (0.40 \times -2\%) = 4\%$. The return of the portfolio after the changes is $(0.75 \times 9\%) + (0.25 \times -3\%) = 6\%$. The extra $2\%$ active return is explained by summing the allocation $(1.5\%)$, selection $(0.2\%)$, and interaction $(0.3\%)$ effects.

The accounting-based attribution method requires data for the positions of the fund and the benchmark. When such data are not available, an analyst can perform factor-based attribution, regressing manager active returns against indices representing the returns from well-known risk factors (e.g., credit spreads, size, value). When a manager has a statistically significant positive correlation between their active return and a risk factor, we can conclude that the manager is generating active returns from this factor.

105
Both accounting-based and factor-based attributions can be used to monitor managers over time to make sure they are adhering to their stated investment strategy.

# Statistical Significance and Manager Monitoring

When monitoring manager performance, we must realize that past performance is a small sample from the manager's true performance distribution, and that the level of sample error in the data is likely to be high.

To adjust for the noise of sample error in historical data, an analyst should perform a $t$-test to assess whether a manager's true mean return is nonzero.

The $t$-statistic for the observed sample mean of historical manager performance of size $N$ is given by the following:


t = \frac {\text{sample mean}}{\text{sample standard deviation}} \times \sqrt {N}


The $t$-statistic represents the number of standard errors the sample mean is away from a hypothesized mean of zero. When the $t$-statistic is outside the range of $-2$ to $+2$, we can reject the null hypothesis that the manager's true return is zero with $95\%$ confidence.

## PROFESSOR'S NOTE

Recall from basic quants how the volatility of the sample mean is sample standard deviation $(s)$ divided by $\sqrt{N}$ (referred to as the standard error of the sample mean). The number of standard errors that the sample mean is away from a hypothesized mean of zero, which is what the $t$-statistic measures, is as follows:


t = \frac {\text{sample mean} - 0}{s / \sqrt {N}}


All that is happening with the expression given in the text here is that the zero is being ignored, and we are multiplying through by a factor of $\sqrt{N}$ instead of dividing through by an expression with it in the denominator.

If we observe annual returns with a sample mean of $5\%$ and a sample standard deviation of $15\%$, we know we need to observe $N$ years so that the associated $t$-statistic is above 2:


2 = \frac {5 \%}{15 \%} \times \sqrt {N}


Therefore, $N = [2 \times (15\% / 5\%)]^2 = 36$ years.

Waiting this amount of time to collect statistically significant data is not practical. Many software packages have algorithms designed to decrease the amount of data required to achieve statistical significance (e.g., the CUSUM [cumulative sum] statistical process control scheme).

Once we identify statistically significant underperformance for a manager, we should investigate its cause (e.g., poor allocation effect). We must consider whether the cause of the underlying underperformance is consistent with the manager's stated investment

106
process to identify where deviations from stated strategy have occurred (either deliberately or by mistake). We can then decide how to respond to the underperformance; options range from dialogue with the manager to termination. In any event, we should share the results of the analysis with the manager to improve their future performance.

## Manager Selection

Key considerations outside of basic performance analysis when selecting and monitoring managers include the following:

- Does an active manager’s performance justify their higher fees?
- Is the manager’s investment philosophy robust and adaptable to changing market conditions? Does it fit with the client’s needs? Are appropriate data available to analyze manager performance?
- Is the manager’s fee structure likely to cause them to take inappropriate levels of risk? Many performance fee structures reward managers for outperformance, but they do not punish them for underperformance, leading to option-like fees that encourage risk-taking.
- Does sufficient data exist to monitor managers adequately and investigate effectively when evidence of underperformance emerges?

## MODULE QUIZ W4.4

1. A financial advisor who recommends overweighting a sector in which they have poor stock selection skills is most likely to exhibit:
- A. underperformance versus the benchmark.
- B. a negative interaction effect for the sector.
- C. a negative allocation effect for the sector.

2. Fund Manager P has the same monthly arithmetic average return as Fund Manager Q, but Manager P’s returns have twice the volatility of Manager Q. Based on this information, Manager P will most likely have a:
- A. lower geometric average return than Manager Q.
- B. higher average logarithmic return than Manager Q.
- C. higher logarithmic information ratio than Manager Q.

## KEY CONCEPTS

### LOS W4.a

Methods to assess client risk tolerance include the following:

- Risk tolerance questionnaires
- Conversations with the client
- Goals-based planning techniques to allocate subportfolios to meet client goals
- Modeling and simulation to help understand the impact of misestimation of inputs to the asset allocation process
Asset classes with above-average historical returns will go on to underperform if expected growth rates and cash flows do not increase.

Here are points regarding forecasting returns:

- For investment-grade bonds: over an investment horizon of twice the bond portfolio's constant modified duration, total return of the portfolio will be approximately equal to its initial yield
- For short-term duration bonds and cash: short-term rates = inflation over the long term
- For high-yield bonds: expected annual return = [(1 - PD) × YTM] + [PD × -LGD]

- PD = annual probability of default of the high-yield bonds in the portfolio
- YTM = yield to maturity of the high-yield bonds
- LGD = loss given default

- For equity: expected return = forward dividend yield + growth rate of dividends

- Research literature shows that dividends of listed companies tend to grow about 2% slower than the economy over the long term, referred to as the dilution effect

Here are points regarding forecasting risk:

- Shrinkage techniques can be used to reduce the impact of historical sampling error on estimates for variance and covariance for public assets.
- The risk for nonpublic assets such as private equity can be estimated by adjusting the risk of similar publicly traded assets.

When performing portfolio optimization, aim to maximize the utility of a portfolio to a client, given their preferences, typically measured as:

utility = portfolio expected return - $\frac{\gamma}{2} \times$ portfolio variance

This is where $\gamma$ = the client's coefficient of risk aversion (a number typically between 1 and 10).

When advising a private client, an advisor should focus on asset-liability modeling techniques applied to all the assets and liabilities of the client and maximize the utility of the surplus (assets - liabilities).

## LOS W4.b

For taxable investors with an effective tax rate of $tx$:

utility = (1 - tx)[pretax expected return - $\frac{\gamma}{2} \times$ pretax variance $\times$ (1 - tx)]

The effect of considering taxes is to reduce the impact of variance on utility more than it reduces the impact of returns, effectively making the investor less risk averse.

Here are general observations on taxes:

- Longer-term tax rates tend to be lower than short-term tax rates.
- Amortization of premium or discount is usually taxed as income for fixed-income securities.

108
- Depreciation of real estate is tax deductible, but is often reclaimed by tax authorities through depreciation recapture if the property is sold above the depreciated book value.
- Pooled investment structures generally pass tax liabilities of the fund through to investors.
- Wash sale rules may apply, and need to be considered at the joint account level when they exist.
- Tax lots (holdings bought at different prices) should be optimized to minimize capital gains taxes on disposition of assets. In general, selling higher-cost tax lots sooner delays capital gains taxes.
- Tax treaties may alleviate double taxation due to withholding taxes on foreign income.
- Alternative minimum tax (AMT) rules may set a floor on how much tax a private client must pay.

Centralized portfolio management, where all trades for the client are executed by a centralized trading desk, may improve the tax efficiency of the portfolio.

Two approaches to tax-efficient portfolio construction include the following:

1. Direct investing. Match the benchmark on a pretax basis, and use tax-aware strategies to outperform the benchmark on a posttax basis.
2. Portfolio customization. Prioritize tax efficiency as a client goal.

When dealing with concentrated positions, an advisor can employ a fully diversified complementary portfolio in which tax losses are used to offset taxable gains realized through selling the concentrated position over time.

## LOS W4.c

Financial planning for retirement involves accumulation (saving) and decumulation (spending).

A key metric assessing a client's level of preparedness for retirement is their funding ratio, defined as:


\text{funding ratio} = \frac{\text{assets}}{\text{liabilities}}


A funding ratio greater than 1 implies that the family has positive net worth and is adequately saving for retirement.

A financial advisor should optimize the funding ratio return of the portfolio, defined as:


\text{funding ratio return} = \frac{\text{asset return} - \text{liability return}}{1 + \text{liability return}}


The Rule of 72 states that money invested at a rate of $r$ per year doubles in value after approximately $72 / r$ years. Regular contributions to a savings account will generate approximately half the terminal wealth from contributions made in the first $72 / r$ years of saving.

109
Two approaches to decumulation in retirement are managing a portfolio from which regular withdrawals are made, or purchasing an annuity to provide payments to the retiree for life.

Established rules for regular withdrawals include the following:

- Base withdrawals on a percentage of assets (e.g., 4% and subsequent adjustment for inflation based on life expectancy in retirement of 30 years)
- Base withdrawals on remaining life expectancy (e.g., spending 1 / life expectancy subject to a cap of 20% of the portfolio)

For a finite annuity over $N$ years with growth rate in payments of $g$, we can state:


\text{initial payment} = \frac{\text{lump sum} \times (\mathrm{E}[\mathrm{r}] - \mathrm{g})}{1 - \left(\frac{1 + \mathrm{g}}{1 + \mathrm{E}[\mathrm{r}]}\right)^{\mathrm{N}}}


where:

- initial payment = first annuity payment to investor at the end of the first year
- lump sum = price of annuity paid at the start of the first year by the investor
- $\mathrm{E}[\mathrm{r}]$ = expected return on invested lump sum
- $\mathrm{g}$ = growth rate in annuity payments
- $\mathrm{N}$ = term of annuity in years

In the special case when $g = \mathrm{E}[\mathrm{r}]$, this reduces to:


\text{initial payment} = \frac{\text{lump sum} \times (1 + \mathrm{E}[\mathrm{r}])}{\mathrm{N}}


Innovations in retirement planning include modern tontines, retirement security bonds, and combined offsetting insurance.

## LOS W4.d

Regarding performance measures based on return, here are some basic definitions:

1. Arithmetic active return = $\mathrm{R_P} - \mathrm{R_B}$
2. Excess return = $\mathrm{R_P} - \mathrm{R_f}$
3. Logarithmic active return = $\ln(1 + \mathrm{R_P}) - \ln(1 + \mathrm{R_B})$
4. Logarithmic excess return = $\ln(1 + \mathrm{R_P}) - \ln(1 + \mathrm{R_f})$

Volatility in returns causes geometric returns over multiple periods to be lower than arithmetic average returns, which fail to reflect compounding.

Because logarithmic returns reflect compounding, volatility in returns also cause the average arithmetic logarithmic return to be lower than the arithmetic average return of a portfolio.

Classic risk-adjusted return measures include the Sharpe ratio (excess return relative to the standard deviation of returns) and information ratio (active return relative to the standard deviation of active returns). These can also be stated on a logarithmic basis:
111
```markdown
logarithmic Sharpe ratio = $\frac{\text{mean}(\text{excess logarithmic returns})}{\text{standard deviation}(\text{excess logarithmic return})}$

logarithmic information ratio = $\frac{\text{mean}(\text{active logarithmic returns})}{\text{standard deviation}(\text{active logarithmic return})}$

Other downside measures of risk include value at risk (VaR), expected shortfall (ES), and semistandard deviation.

The Sortino ratio is similar to the Sharpe ratio but defines excess returns as returns above a stated minimum acceptable return (MAR):


\text{Sortino ratio} = \frac{\text{mean}(\text{return}) - \text{MAR}}{\text{semideviation}(\text{return} - \text{MAR})}


The following is regarding accounting-based performance attribution:

- The allocation effect measures the active return impact of a portfolio manager overweighting/underweighting sectors or asset classes in their portfolio relative to the benchmark:

allocation effect = $\sum$ (active sector overweight or underweight × benchmark sector or asset class return)

- The selection effect measures the active return impact of a portfolio manager selecting stocks in their portfolio that are different to the benchmark:

selection effect = $\sum$ (weight of benchmark × active sector or asset class return)

- The interaction effect measures the interaction of the first two components. If a manager is a good stock picker and overweights those sectors, then the manager will exhibit a positive interaction effect:

interaction effect = $\sum$ (active sector overweight or underweight × active sector or asset class return)

Statistical significance when appraising manager performance is evident (with 95% confidence) when the $t$-statistic takes a magnitude greater than 2, where $N$ is the sample size:


t = \frac{\text{sample mean}}{\text{sample standard deviation}} \times \sqrt{N}

## Module Quiz W4.1

2. C An analyst performs shrinkage by combining historical covariance matrices with matrices reflecting their views on the future.

Research shows a dilution effect to dividend growth rates, which causes dividends to grow slower than GDP. Cash flows to equity are more variable;

## Module Quiz W4.2

1. B When adjusting the MVO utility function for the impact of taxes (with tax rate $tx$), we use posttax returns and posttax variances given by the following:


\text{posttax expected return} = \text{pretax expected return} \times (1 - tx)

\text{posttax variance} = \text{pretax variance} \times (1 - tx)^2


Thus, the whole utility function is multiplied by a factor of $(1 - tx)$, while the variance is reduced by another factor of $(1 - tx)$. This reduction in variance relative to returns can be viewed as decreasing the impact of variance on the investor's utility, as if the investor became less risk averse. The rationale is that because the government absorbs some of the variance of the portfolio through taxing investment returns, the investor can tolerate slightly more risk. The fact that taxes effectively change the risk aversion level of the investor means the optimal portfolio will change. (LOS W4.b)

2. A Direct indexing aims to match the index on a pretax basis, but outperform the index on a posttax basis. A complementary portfolio is a diversified portfolio created such that its tax losses can be offset against large capital gains realized from a highly appreciated concentrated position. Alternative minimum tax rules increase the amount of tax that wealthy investors pay through removing the tax advantage of instruments and structures. (LOS W4.b)

## Module Quiz W4.3

1. C The funding ratio return (FRR) measures the change in the funding ratio (assets / liabilities) for the period. The FRR depends only on the change in the assets and liabilities of the investors, not the size of the funding ratio itself. Given that Pedro and Paulo have the same asset allocation, their asset return will be the same. Given that their liabilities are also the same, the change in liabilities will be equal. Thus, their FRRs will also be equal. (LOS W4.c)

2. B The value of the 10-year annuity at the age of 85 is calculated as follows (END mode):


10 \text{ N}; 3 \text{ I/Y}; 20,000 \text{ PMT}; 0 \text{ FV}; \text{ CPT PV} = 170,604


Adjusting this value for the probability of survival to age 85 (50%) and discounting back from time 85 to time 30 (because the employee is 30 years old) gives a current cost:


\text{Current cost} = (170,604 \times 0.50) / 1.05^{55} = \text{USD } 5,828.


(LOS W4.c)

1. B The interaction effect combines active sector weights with active returns. In this case, the active sector weight will be positive because the advisor has
recommended an overweight, but the active return in the sector will be negative because they have poor stock selection skills. Therefore, the interaction effect, which is the product of active sector weight and active return, will be negative.

There is no way to assess how the overall portfolio has performed relative to the benchmark purely from the allocation and selection effect of one sector. We do not know from the information in the question whether the decision to overweight the sector was incorrect, which would lead to a negative allocation effect. (LOS W4.d)

2. A Volatility in returns causes geometric returns over multiple periods to be lower once compounding is reflected. Therefore, Fund Manager P will have a lower geometric return than Fund Manager Q. Logarithmic returns reflect compounding; therefore, they are decreased by volatility. Because Manager P has more volatile returns, they will also have lower average logarithmic returns. Information ratios, whether logarithmic or otherwise, are dependent on active returns versus a benchmark. Because we have no information about benchmarks and we cannot assume they are the same, we cannot make a statement regarding the relative logarithmic information ratios of the two managers. (LOS W4.d)
