READING W5

PRESERVING THE WEALTH

EXAM FOCUS

This reading focuses on the risks that individuals and families face and the strategies they can deploy to protect and preserve their wealth. Risk management and responses to risk are critical to learn, as well as understanding mortality risk, longevity risk, health risk, and disability risk and how they can affect human and financial capital. Life insurance, annuities, and property and liability insurance offer some protections against these risks and are important to differentiate. Because inflation can have a significant negative impact on wealth, it is important to understand the different asset classes and their correlations as a hedging tool to protect against inflation. Finally, exchange rate risks present vulnerabilities for investors with assets and liabilities in differing currencies. Numerous strategies can be deployed to protect against this risk.

MODULE W5.1: RISKS AFFECTING HUMAN CAPITAL

LOS W5.a: Analyze the types of risks relevant to human capital.

&gt; Video covering this content is available online.

Risk management involves identifying threats to assets and determining an appropriate strategy to manage these threats. The risk management process involves five components:

1. Identification
2. Measurement
3. Evaluation
4. Risk response
5. Monitoring

The framework used to manage risk is the asset-liability management (ALM) framework. A key element of this framework is a client's surplus, which is their assets less their liabilities. Naturally, a higher surplus provides more investment alternatives and a greater capacity to absorb risk.

Risk responses are based on assessing both the probability of a risk event occurring and its impact, if it does occur.

114
High-probability risks are captured by **loss control** responses, which are efforts to avoid or mitigate risk:

- With **risk avoidance** (high-probability, high-impact risks), the possibility of incurring this risk is eliminated.
- With **risk mitigation** (high-probability, low-impact risks), **loss prevention** involves reducing the probability of a loss, whereas **loss reduction** involves reducing the size of the loss.

Low-probability risks are addressed via the following two approaches:

- With **risk transfer** (low-probability, high-impact risks), this can occur through insurance or through contracts like fixed-rate loans.
- With **risk acceptance** (low-probability, low-impact risks), retaining risks is more feasible with a larger surplus and a lower vulnerability to risk events.

## Employment Risk

**Human capital** can be thought of as the mortality-adjusted net present value of expected labor income. The importance of human capital is evident from early in an individual’s career—all the way through their capital accumulation phase. Human capital must be balanced with **financial capital**, which includes components such as a client’s investments, real estate, and expected inheritances. The higher the risk to human capital, the less risk can be borne through financial capital.

Human capital is a byproduct of three components:

1. **Earnings correlation**. The more correlated with capital markets and economic cycles a client’s employment earnings are, the greater the risk tied to earnings.
2. **Earnings volatility**. This consists of the probability of employment and the volatility associated with annual earnings given employment.
3. **Replaceability**. This refers to the ease with which a client could replace their income if they changed jobs or employers. The more transferable the client’s skill set, the less risk the client has for replacing their income.

A client’s earnings stream can be compared to a bond investment (lower correlations with market returns, less volatility, and easy to replace) or an equity investment (higher correlations with market returns, more volatility, and difficult to replace).

## Mortality Risk

The risk here is that premature death removes the value of human capital. This is a greater risk early in a client’s career. Although life expectancies have improved dramatically over the last century, an individual’s health span has not seen a commensurate improvement. Gender (females tend to live longer), family history, and overall health risks and vulnerabilities can have a significant impact on human capital.

115
# Longevity Risk

Longevity risk is the potential for clients to "outlive their money," fully depleting financial capital before death. This risk increases (1) in the absence of guaranteed retirement benefits, (2) when income replacement levels are inadequate, or (3) when access to health and medical care is either limited or unaffordable. Even a client relying on pension income can have longevity risk due to inflation. Lifespan uncertainty adds to the challenge of managing this risk. Although building a retirement plan that extends an expected lifespan to well over 100 years can reduce this risk considerably, doing so may diminish a retiree's quality of life.

We can use several methods to estimate the capital needed to support a retiree's lifestyle. One approach is to estimate a withdrawal rate and divide it into a target annual retirement spending amount. For instance, if an estimated annual spending rate is 5%, the amount of capital needed to support $100,000 in annual spending is $100,000 divided by 0.05 = $2,000,000.

An alternative approach is to estimate future cash flows and assign a probability to each, reflecting the likelihood that it will be needed (a survival probability). For spouses, joint probabilities of either spouse surviving become relevant for spending forecasts. The following two equations are useful in determining the survival probability and the present value of the spending need:


p(\text{survival}) = p(\text{husband survives}) + p(\text{wife survives}) - [p(\text{husband survives}) \times p(\text{wife survives})]

\mathrm{PV}(\text{spending need}) = \sum_{j=1}^{N} \frac{p(\text{survival}_j) \times \text{spending}_j}{(1 + r)^j}


Inflation will affect retirement spending in varying ways, as some expenses may be more vulnerable to inflation than others. A spending analysis can categorize the different expense types and expected amounts, which can be further refined by survival probabilities. Due in large part to shared expenses (e.g., home, cars, food), annual spending needs for a couple are less than twice the needs for an individual.

Because mortality risk can be hedged with life insurance, discounting future spending needs at the real risk-free rate (rather than the expected return on assets used to fund spending needs) is appropriate. However, due to the risk of investing in capital markets and the potential of underestimating retirement capital needs, a safety reserve should be established. The objectives of a safety reserve include creating a capital cushion and potentially providing additional discretionary spending or bequests, depending on capital market performance. Because of the uncertainty associated with the markets, family obligations, and spending patterns over time, the safety reserve helps to reduce the impact of these elements.

Bengen's¹ work in 1994 on withdrawal rates and historical returns showed that a 5% withdrawal rate over a 30-year time frame depleted a portfolio 25% of the time, assuming a 50/50 mix of equities and bonds. The biggest three factors in driving the success or failure of a withdrawal program to address longevity risk include the following:

116
1. Time horizon. Longevity risk increases with longer retirement periods. Early retirement in good health increases longevity risk.
2. Expected portfolio return. Capital market assumptions and asset allocation decisions are critical because lower expected returns on investments increase longevity risk.
3. Withdrawal rate. Higher withdrawal rates stemming from a greater reliance on retirement fund income naturally increase longevity risk.

Directly related to longevity risk is the **probability of ruin**, which is the estimated likelihood that an investor will not be able to meet a specific financial goal or obligation due to depleting their financial assets. This probability can be decreased by planning for a long retirement, which should reduce the risk that an investor will deplete their savings.

# Mortality Tables vs. Monte Carlo Analysis

A good example of mortality tables is illustrated through the Internal Revenue Service (IRS) required minimum distribution (RMD) rule. This rule requires retirement account investors to withdraw a certain amount each year, based on age and life expectancy.

Monte Carlo analysis can be used to enhance withdrawal estimates by accounting for capital market risk and returns, inflation-adjusted expenses, liquidity needs, taxes, variable spending patterns, and the safety reserve. While mortality tables use the risk-free rate for discounting cash flows, Monte Carlo analysis incorporates expected asset returns driven by market expectations. Ideally, a Monte Carlo analysis should not assume normally distributed returns. Actual historical returns tend to have fat tails and skew to the left, which can make withdrawal programs less sustainable and increase required capital.

In 2005, Milevsky and Robinson² provided a sustainable spending rate calculation methodology that approximated Monte Carlo simulations and accounted for financial market risk and lifespan uncertainty. Figure W5.1 reflects their analysis of ruin probabilities, assuming a 50/50 equity and bond mix.

Figure W5.1: Ruin Probability (Portfolio: 50% Equity, 50% Bonds)

| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| | | 2.00 | 3.00 | 4.00 | 5.00 | 6.00 | 7.00 | 8.00 | 9.00 | 10.00 |
| Endowment | Infinity | 6.7% | 24.9% | 49.0% | 70.0% | 84.3% | 92.5% | 96.6% | 98.6% | 99.4% |
| 50 | 78.1 | 1.8 | 6.4 | 14.0 | 24.0 | 35.2 | 46.3 | 56.8 | 66.0 | 73.8 |
| 55 | 83.0 | 1.8 | 6.3 | 14.0 | 24.0 | 35.1 | 46.2 | 56.7 | 65.9 | 73.7 |
| 60 | 83.4 | 1.5 | 5.2 | 11.6 | 20.1 | 29.9 | 40.1 | 50.0 | 59.1 | 67.2 |
| 65 | 83.9 | 1.1 | 4.0 | 9.0 | 15.8 | 24.0 | 32.8 | 41.8 | 50.5 | 58.5 |

| 75 | 85.7 | 0.5 | 1.7 | 3.9 | 7.2 | 11.4 | 16.3 | 21.9 | 27.8 | 33.9 |

# EXAMPLE: Ruin probability analysis

A 60-year-old investor has $5 million available to maintain his lifestyle into retirement. Using the information provided in Figure W5.1 and assuming a portfolio distribution aligned with the figure, what amount can the investor withdraw each year to have a 95% level of confidence that he will not run out of money?

Answer: A 95% confidence level equates to a ruin probability of 5%, so the maximum probability of exhausting his assets should not exceed 5%. Based on the table in Figure W5.1 and an age of 60, a spending rate of $3 per every $100 in assets has a ruin probability of 5.2%. The investor can withdraw approximately 0.03 × $5 million = $150,000 each year with 95% confidence that the portfolio will last for the remainder of his life.

| Ruin Probability (Portfolio: 50% Equity, 50% Bonds) | | | | | | | | | | |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Retirement Age | Median Age (Death) | Real Annual Spending Based on $100 (U.S. Dollars) | | | | | | | | |
| | | 2.00 | 3.00 | 4.00 | 5.00 | 6.00 | 7.00 | 8.00 | 9.00 | 10.00 |
| Endowment | Infinity | 6.7% | 24.9% | 49.0% | 70.0% | 84.3% | 92.5% | 96.6% | 98.6% | 99.4% |
| 50 | 78.1 | 1.8 | 6.4 | 14.0 | 24.0 | 35.2 | 46.3 | 56.8 | 66.0 | 73.8 |
| 55 | 83.0 | 1.8 | 6.3 | 14.0 | 24.0 | 35.1 | 46.2 | 56.7 | 65.9 | 73.7 |
| 60 | 83.4 | 1.5 | 5.2 | 11.6 | 20.1 | 29.9 | 40.1 | 50.0 | 59.1 | 67.2 |

Annuity pricing can be used to estimate the retirement funds needed to achieve a target spending level. If, for example, an individual wishes to spend $150,000 per year on an inflation-adjusted basis during their retirement, an inflation-adjusted fixed annuity paying 4% interest would require spending $150,000 / 0.04 = $3,750,000 to fund their retirement needs.

# Sequence-of-Returns Risk

Retirement programs that suffer losses early produce more severe consequences than ones that suffer losses later. Financial capital depletion that results from early losses increases the impact of longevity risk, which becomes highly problematic when initial returns are low, when returns are highly volatile, or when withdrawal programs follow significant negative returns.

Individuals can reduce the impact of this risk by working longer or adjusting spending needs downward, which should reduce the need for large withdrawals during down market periods. Deferred annuities are another approach to reducing the impact of early capital depletion. (If withdrawals are not needed, ending wealth is not affected by return sequences.)

Dollar cost averaging is an investment strategy in which the investor contributes periodic fixed amounts to an investment account. This allows them to benefit from market volatility by purchasing more shares at lower prices (and fewer shares at higher prices). The opposite of this strategy is periodic fixed withdrawals, where the investor is hurt by market volatility because fixed outflows that occur during market downturns require the sale of more shares at reduced prices.
To minimize longevity risk, a balance must be made between saving and spending. Although counterintuitive, saving too much or spending too little can diminish the benefits of having retirement assets. Working longer increases human capital, but it also decreases the years during which an individual benefits from retirement.

Ultra-wealthy individuals naturally have reduced longevity risk, but even those who are not in that status can manage the withdrawal rate to reduce this risk and the probability of ruin.

## Health Risk

Health risk represents the costs associated with unexpected injuries or illnesses and the impacts these have on human capital. As health risks increase, expected earnings decrease and uncertainty increases. Costs related to health risk include direct costs (health care expenses) and indirect costs (disruptions due to illness).

## Disability Risk

Disability relates to injuries or illnesses that inhibit continuous employment and often require specialized transportation, medical care, and other accommodations. Disability can be defined in several ways, including (1) an inability to work in any occupation, (2) an inability to perform tasks in jobs aligned with one's experience and education, or (3) an inability to complete tasks related to an individual's current job.

The expected value of human capital is directly related to the type of occupation one holds and the potential for danger. Many employers offer short-term and long-term disability plans to protect employees from earnings losses.

Long-term care is needed when daily living activities require support. The costs of long-term care often rise at a pace that exceeds inflation and can be substantial for both the recipient and caregivers.

## MODULE QUIZ W5.1

1. If a new risk has a low probability of occurrence but a high impact if it should occur, which of the following risk responses is most appropriate?

A. Transfer.
B. Avoidance.
C. Acceptance.

2. Historically, securities market returns have typically skewed to the:

A. left with fat tails.
B. right with fat tails.

119
C. right with thin tails.

# MODULE W5.2: RISK MANAGEMENT STRATEGIES

Video covering this content is available online.

**LOS W5.b: Describe and recommend strategies to manage risks to human capital.**

As described earlier, human capital is essentially the value of expected future earnings. There are many ways to approach the risks associated with human capital. The negative impact of earnings volatility and high correlation to equity markets can be avoided by career choices that provide a steady earnings stream as well as skills that are easily transferrable to other jobs or industries. For any events that could have a high impact and low probability of occurrence, insurance is an effective risk transfer strategy. From a risk retention standpoint, building an emergency reserve of three to nine months of living expenses can protect against earnings disruptions. However, there is an opportunity cost associated with placing funds in a reserve.

## Life Insurance

Life insurance involves transferring risk to a third party and serves as a hedge against the risk of premature death for the wage earner. The insurance itself is designed to cover the difference between future earnings and future spending. To calculate the amount of life insurance needed, two methods can be used:

1. The **human life value method** is an asset-based method used to quantify human capital by estimating the difference between the net present value of future earnings and incremental future expenses.
2. The **needs analysis method** is a liability-based method focused on the present value of death-related costs in the short term and ongoing living expenses for surviving family members in the long term. The final life insurance need is the difference between total funds required and existing assets.

Choosing an insurance company is a key decision because the insurer must be counted on to weather adverse market situations and fulfill its obligations. Cost is an important factor as well, with rating agency information serving as a useful tool in picking a provider.

A basic life insurance contract has four primary parties (although they are not always four distinct parties):

1. **Insured.** This is the individual or entity requiring life insurance.
2. **Policyholder.** This is the owner of the policy and the premium payer (often, but not always, the insured).
3. **Insurance company.** This is the entity that provides the coverage and collects the premiums.
4. **Beneficiary.** This is the individual or entity who receives the death benefit.
In addition to information on these parties, a life insurance policy will include elements such as the term, the type (permanent or term), the death benefit (face value), limitations on the death benefit if applicable, the premium schedule, the contestability period, and policy rider modifications.

Although there are many types of life insurance offerings, the two primary types are term and permanent. Term life insurance covers the insured for a specific period, and unless renewed, it will expire if the insured survives through the end of the term. Some term policies have escalating premiums, while others offer level-term premiums that stay constant throughout.

Permanent life insurance covers the insured for life, generally with fixed premiums that are partially used to generate cash value. Relative to term insurance premiums, permanent life insurance premiums tend to be higher. Permanent life insurance may be viewed as a combination of a term policy and an investment account that generates cash value. The face value is paid out to the beneficiaries when the insured dies, with the cash value often going to the insurer. Over time, as the insurance value declines, the cash value increases. The policyholder can access the cash value through withdrawals at the policy's maturity/termination or through loans, but any withdrawals or unpaid borrowings will reduce the death benefit payout.

Permanent policies may include a nonforfeiture clause, which allows policyholders to access some of their benefits, even with missed premium payments. These options can include an extended term option (cash value converted into a term policy), a cash surrender option (paying out the current cash value), and a reduced paid-up option (cash value used to buy a single-premium whole life policy).

Within the permanent life insurance category, several varieties are available:

- Whole life insurance provides insurance coverage for the life of the insured. These policies can be participating life insurance (policyholders share in the insurer's profits) or nonparticipating life insurance (benefits remain fixed, regardless of the insurer's profits or performance).
- Endowment life insurance is whole life with a limited coverage period. The face value is paid to the policyowner if the insured is still alive when the coverage period ends.
- Universal life insurance provides policyholders with flexibility regarding investment options (for cash value) and premium adjustments.

Life insurance is an obvious choice for risk mitigation related to employment and mortality. Life insurance also provides other benefits in the areas of liquidity for beneficiaries, estate planning for meeting tax obligations, tax-sheltered savings, immediate financial expense coverage, and legacy goals like charitable bequests. Life insurance wrappers involve placing investment portfolios into a life insurance policy designed to minimize tax on investment income.

## Managing Longevity Risk

A popular way to mitigate longevity risk is to use annuities, which offer income for life to the annuitant and potentially their spouse. Defined benefit pension plans (employer
121
bears the risk) and defined contribution plans (employee bears the risk) are other mechanisms to mitigate longevity risk. Another approach is to assume a very long lifespan, which will naturally reduce the withdrawal amounts but may reduce quality of life.

During the phase of life when an investor is taking withdrawals (the decumulation phase), asset allocation strategies used to manage volatility include the following:

- Static asset allocation. Amounts allocated to individual asset classes are fixed.
- Dynamic asset allocation. Amounts allocated to individual asset classes will be adjusted during the retirement stage.

A dynamic withdrawal program can be used to add flexibility and reduce longevity risk. Examples of dynamic withdrawal strategies include the following:

- In years after negative returns, not adjusting the annual withdrawal amounts for inflation
- Incorporating a flexible spending strategy to minimize the impact of market downturns
- Using dividend and coupon payments to cover withdrawals and ongoing expenses, while only using capital gains during positive years
- Applying upper and lower limits on the withdrawal rate to manage the impact of up markets or down markets

# EXAMPLE: Dynamic withdrawal strategy

Lisa Reid has a $5 million investment portfolio, with annual withdrawals initially set to 4% of the initial portfolio value to start on Year 1 and adjusted each year for a 2.5% inflation rate. She establishes a range for her withdrawals of 3.25%–4.75%.

In Year 1 of her retirement, Reid withdraws $200,000 (= $5,000,000 × 4%). The market declines 5%, which reduces her portfolio value to $4,560,000 (= $4,800,000 million post-withdrawal × 95%). In Year 2 of her retirement, Reid withdraws $205,000 (= $200,000 × 1.025). The market declines another 3%, reducing the value of Reid's portfolio to $4,224,350 (= $4,355,000 post-withdrawal × 97%).

In Year 3, the calculated inflation-adjusted withdrawal of $210,125 (= $205,000 × 1.025) will be 4.97% of the portfolio value of $4,224,350. This exceeds the upper withdrawal limit of 4.75%. The capped withdrawal would, therefore, be $200,657 (= $4,224,350 × 4.75%). Subsequent market increases will allow Reid to resume her planned inflation-adjusted withdrawal amounts, with potentially large market increases allowing her to withdraw more than planned if the calculated amount were to fall below the lower bound of 3.25%.

Laddered bonds may be a beneficial strategy to use for retirees who have set withdrawal requirements and who value the predictability of withdrawals. Bond ladders have varying noncallable fixed-income securities set to pay interest and mature at intervals aligned with retirement spending needs. The payout is similar to fixed

122
annuity payments. This strategy is helpful to manage interest rate risk, but on its own, it will not address longevity risk. Deferred annuities that pay out later in life can be combined with a bond ladder that provides cash for the earlier stage of retirement.

Based on the work of Richard Thaler,³ another means of managing longevity risk is to categorize investments into "mental buckets" designed to meet objectives tied to goals, or to when cash needs are likely to occur. The alignment of these buckets with natural mental accounting tendencies makes it easier for investors to track their goals. However, a strategy such as this can result in ongoing asset rebalancing, the negative mental impact of equity exposure volatility, and potentially redundant investment positions.

## Annuities

As noted earlier, annuities are another effective tool used to manage longevity risk. An annuity will provide payments over a specific period at equal intervals.

Like a life insurance contract, a basic annuity contract has four primary parties (although they are not always four distinct parties):

1. Annuitant. This is the beneficiary of the annuity payments.
2. Contract owner. This is the owner of the annuity (often, but not always, the annuitant).
3. Insurer. Typically, this is the insurance company that sells the annuity.
4. Beneficiary (where applicable). This is the individual or entity who receives the death benefit.

A traditional immediate fixed annuity guarantees income in exchange for what is often a significant up-front investment. Even with the relatively high cost, annuities have been shown to outperform approaches that are more "self-managed" such as bond laddering. We can state the spending improvement for a given year as $Q = (1 - p) / p$, where $p$ is the annuitant's survival probability.

The key differences among annuities are in their payment amounts (fixed vs. variable) and their timing (deferred vs. immediate). Tradeoffs must be made between the cost of the premiums, flexibility for the annuitant and beneficiary, length of coverage, and the amount of the payouts. Because each payment includes principal, interest, and mortality credits, factors such as health, age, and gender will affect the income yield.

## Fixed Annuities

- Fixed annuity. This offers an annual income yield equal to a fixed percentage of the initial investment. The income yield is an annual calculation, but the payments to the annuitant can be made monthly, quarterly, or annually.
- Single-premium immediate annuity (SPIA). Also known as a payout annuity, this involves a single lump-sum investment exchanged for a stream of annual income (starting immediately) over a preset term or over a lifetime.
- Inflation-adjusted payments. These are annuity payments that will grow by a specific percentage each year or adjust based on the actual inflation rate.

123
- Period certain annuities. These are payments for a set number of periods.
- Lifetime annuity. This provides annuitants payments for the remainder of their lives.
- Life annuity with period certain. This is similar to a period certain annuity, except that it has a minimum number of payments, even if the annuitant dies during the annuity period (which means a beneficiary will get the residual).
- Life annuity with return of premium. The beneficiary receives a payment if the annuitant dies before receiving payments equaling the initial investment.
- Joint life annuity. Also known as a survivor option, this is where payments span the lifetimes of two annuitants (typically spouses).
- Deferred fixed annuity. This provides a payout that begins in the future. Investments earn returns during the deferral period. The two options for the annuitant at the end of the deferral period include (1) annuitizing the investment, converting it into an immediate fixed annuity; or (2) terminating the contract by cashing out the value of the accumulated investment.
- Advanced life deferred annuity (ALDA). Also known as longevity insurance, this is an annuity purchased later in life with a lump-sum payment. The benefits of an ALDA include the return earned on deferred annuity payments, the transfer of risk to the insurance company, and the setting of a defined time frame (the deferral period) for the investment horizon.

While single-premium annuities are one form, another is incrementally purchased annuities paid for over accumulation periods. The annuity accrues investment returns and interest over this period, which is followed by a payout phase. This can be a beneficial alternative for investors who cannot afford the large premium needed for a SPIA.

## Variable Annuities

- Immediate variable annuities. These are similar to SPIAs. An individual spends a lump sum and purchases a lifetime income stream. The payments to the annuitant and their beneficiaries vary based on how well the underlying investment performs. An assumed investment return (AIR) can be set, which will determine the initial payment and future payment adjustments based on investment returns relative to the AIR.
- Deferred variable annuities. Payments do not begin until after a period during which the annuitant contributes to the investment account. The following elements should be considered with deferred variable annuity investments:

- Flexibility. Compared to the guaranteed lifetime income of immediate fixed annuities, variable annuities allow for withdrawals from the underlying subaccount.
- Volatility. Payments for variable annuities are linked to investment performance, although income floors may be added to protect against significant market downturns.
- Fixed annuity option. There is a potential conversion option to an immediate payout annuity once the deferral period is over, although the payout itself and final investment value remain uncertain.
- Return of premium. This is designed to ensure that the beneficiary receives the full premium if the annuitant dies during either the deferral period or the payout period.

124
- Guaranteed minimum withdrawal benefit (GMWB). This is a rider that guarantees a fixed percentage of the initial investment for life.

The annual benefit from an annuity has three components: return of premium, interest accrued on the remaining investment, and mortality credits. Mortality credits enhance income for surviving annuitants, with the tradeoff coming from the cost of insurance.

An annuity offers more income certainty compared to self-insuring longevity risk, but it can lead to lower lifetime income and lower wealth upon death. The retirement income efficient frontier is used to model the balance between ending wealth and income security as shown in Figure W5.2.

![img-3.jpeg](img-3.jpeg)
Figure W5.2: Retirement Income Efficient Frontier

Investor demand for annuities is driven by several factors, including preferences for guaranteed lifetime income, risk aversion to investing, longer life expectancies, reduced income from other sources like pensions and retirement plans, and a potentially reduced desire to gift assets to heirs. Annuities offer security against outliving assets, with fixed annuities targeting longevity risk more effectively than variable annuities.

# Managing Property Risk

Liability risk is the potential legal obligation associated with causing property damage or bodily harm to others. Liability insurance is obtained to manage this risk. Property insurance is designed to reduce the risks tied to assets such as homes and automobiles.

A personal umbrella liability policy is used to supplement home and auto insurance and will pay claims when limits are reached on home or auto insurance. Umbrella policies tend to be cost-effective and generally inexpensive. For high-net-worth and ultra-high-net-worth individuals, this type of coverage is critical. The safeguarding of financial assets should be the emphasis of this insurance coverage.

Earlier, we described using life insurance to manage employment and mortality risk exposure based on two methods: the asset-based human life value method and the
liability-based needs analysis method. Here, we expand on these by quantifying the final life insurance need.

To quantify the human life value method, the following equation is used:


\text{human life value} = \frac{\sum_{j=1}^{n} \frac{\text{income}(1 - t_{\text{emp}}) + \text{benefits} - \text{expense}}{(1 - t_{\text{ins}})(1 + g)^j}}{(1 + r)^{j-1}}


where:

- income = pretax income received from employment
- $t_{\text{emp}}$ = tax rate applicable to employment income
- benefits = nontaxable employee benefits that will disappear once the individual is no longer working
- expense = family expenses attributable to the insured that will no longer be borne by the family after the insured is deceased
- $t_{\text{ins}}$ = tax rate on the life insurance proceeds
- g = annual growth rate (which includes inflation and career advancement)
- r = discount rate for future cash flows
- n = number of years (mortality adjusted) over which earned income is expected

The output of this calculation, less whatever insurance the insured already has, produces the additional amount of coverage needed.

To apply the needs analysis method, the first step is to determine how much cash will be needed when the insured passes away. This will typically include final expenses, debt payoffs, an emergency fund, and funding future education costs. Next is to estimate capital needs to fund living expenses and any other ongoing obligations. This includes expenses for the surviving spouse and children. The potential future earnings of the surviving spouse can help offset the need, as can capital available from existing life insurance, cash, savings, and retirement benefits. Total needs less total available capital equals the amount of life insurance that needs to be acquired.

While these two methods are highly unlikely to produce an equivalent amount of additional life insurance needed, they can provide a range within which to work. Once the range is established, the type of policy (term vs. whole) and associated costs factor into the decision. Needs will also naturally change over time.

## PROFESSOR'S NOTE

Even though the LOS only says to "describe and recommend," there is an example in the CFA text using the previous equation to calculate the amount of life insurance needed using both the needs analysis method and the human life value method.

A. lifetime annuity.
B. joint life annuity.
C. life annuity with period certain.

# MODULE W5.3: MANAGING INFLATION

LOS W5.c: Recommend planning and investment strategies to mitigate the corrosive influence of inflation on preserving purchasing power.

Video covering this content is available online.

Inflation occurs when price levels rise over time, which reduces purchasing power and represents a significant long-term risk. Because inflation increases the nominal value of implicit liabilities (i.e., the future spending needs of individuals), assets must increase at least at a level commensurate with inflation to avoid wealth erosion. The negative impact of inflation hurts all investors, although wealthier investors are in a better position to absorb the impact.

The three types of inflation include the following:

1. Demand-pull inflation. Increasing demand causes prices to rise, which play out in the form of wage increase needs to offset the rising cost of living. Companies paying higher labor costs often have to increase their prices to maintain profit margins.
2. Cost-push inflation. Rising costs for businesses (labor and production) cause prices to rise.
3. Unanchored inflation expectations. Household and firm behavior reflects higher expected prices.

Regardless of its origin, inflation can accelerate relatively quickly and can take a long time to calm. Inflation in developed markets typically averages between 2% and 2.5% per year. This can vary in any given period, as inflation can be close to 0% in some periods and can exceed 8% in high inflationary periods. Inflation tends to be lower in developed markets than in emerging markets because the economic tools available to stem inflation may not be as efficient in emerging markets. Political instability and deliberate devaluation of the home currency can also play a role.

# Inflation Risk and Measures of Inflation

Regardless of how low or high inflation is, assuming it is positive, it will erode purchasing power. An inflation rate of 2.5% leads to an increase in consumer prices of 110% over 30 years. The inflation rate itself is measured as the percentage change in a price index like the Consumer Price Index (CPI). Components of the CPI include the costs of housing and utilities, transportation and communication, food and beverages, education and recreation, medical care, furniture, apparel, and other items. Some of
these components (like health care and education) often outpace the overall rate of inflation, further accentuating the need for financial planning to cover those costs.

Refined measures of inflation are often used to differentiate the spending patterns of subsets of the population. For example, in the United States, the CPI-E (Consumer Price Index for the Elderly) is designed to reflect spending patterns for individuals aged 62 and older. The weightings are adjusted to reflect increased emphasis on components like health care and decreased emphasis on components like education.

# Inflation Hedging Strategies

As with many other situations, high-net-worth individuals are less sensitive to inflation than those with lower income or wealth levels, as the former are better able to adjust their spending in response to inflation. The extent to which assets serve as natural inflation hedges affects the relative impact of rising price levels. Jewelry and art serve as valuable hedges against inflation, but those are luxury items held in greater quantity by individuals with greater net worth. Insurance can be used to hedge against inflation. Prepayment options for insurance policies and college tuition can help protect against rising costs in these categories.

To protect against inflation, an investor's assets must increase in value to offset the negative impact on liabilities and spending. An investment goal incorporating an inflation factor beta can be expressed as follows:


R_{\text{goal},t} = \alpha_0 + \beta_{\text{infl,goal}} \pi_t + \varepsilon_t


where:

R = return (nominal growth) of the investment goal

t = investment period

$\beta_{\text{infl,goal}} = \text{inflation factor beta of investment goal}\pi = \text{inflation (expected and unexpected)}\varepsilon = \text{error term}$

To calculate the inflation factor beta, the following equation is used:


\beta_{\text{infl,goal}} = \rho \frac{\sigma_{\text{goal}}}{\sigma_{\text{infl}}}


where:

$\rho = \text{correlation coefficient between inflation and the investment goal return}\sigma = \text{relative volatilities for inflation and the goal, respectively}$

Inflation can be measured based on market prices, but nominal returns are determined using discounted cash flow analyses, which are naturally subjective. For approximately 100 years (1926-2022), inflation in the United States averaged 3% per year, with volatility approaching 4%. The need to hedge against inflation becomes more critical due to increases in investor risk aversion, the investment time horizon, relative volatility for the investment goal, the correlation between inflation and the investment goal, and the relationship between the two as measured by R-squared.

The inflation hedge ratio is equal to the relative notional exposure amount for a hedged asset needed to offset any pre-existing exposure to inflation. The equation can be expressed in two ways, with the second as a special condition of the first. If the goal

128
is to fully eliminate inflation risk, the desired inflation exposure is set to zero and the second equation is used.


\mathrm {H} _ {\text {i n f}} = \left(\frac {\beta_ {\text {t a r g e t}} - \beta_ {\text {i n f , g o a l}}}{\beta_ {\text {i n f , h e d g e}}}\right) \left(\frac {\text {g o a l}}{\text {h e d g e}}\right)

\mathrm {H} _ {\text {i n f}} = - \left(\frac {\beta_ {\text {i n f , g o a l}}}{\beta_ {\text {i n f , h e d g e}}}\right) \left(\frac {\text {g o a l}}{\text {h e d g e}}\right)


where:

$\beta_{\mathrm{target}} =$ desired amount of inflation exposure

goal = amount (nominal) of investment goal to be hedged

hedge = amount of the hedging asset

Because it is unrealistic to hedge an entire portfolio against inflation, a more realistic approach is to hedge against rising prices for anticipated lifetime expenses. For a hedge to be effective, correlations are critical. If an asset class has a positive correlation with inflation, it can still effectively serve as an inflation hedge. Fixed-rate bonds offer set cash flows and have negative correlations to inflation. As interest rates in the marketplace rise, the nominal interest and principal payments are discounted at higher rates, which decreases their value. During high inflation periods, these investments naturally perform poorly.

Based on source data from Dimson, Marsh, and Staunton (2023), the following table lists correlations by asset class between real returns (for each class) and inflation.

Figure W5.3: Correlations Between Local Inflation and Real Asset Returns by Asset Class (1900-2022)

| Asset Class | Correlation |
| --- | --- |
| Gold | 0.34 |
| Commodity futures | 0.21 |
| Spot commodities | 0.10 |
| International equities | -0.25 |
| International commercial real estate | -0.31 |
| International housing | -0.37 |
| Inflation-protected bonds | -0.41 |
| Global 60:40 (equities/bonds) | -0.42 |
| International bonds | -0.59 |
| International bills | -0.80 |

# Inflation-Linked Fixed Income

To serve as an effective inflation hedge, the real return for an asset (which is its nominal return less the inflation rate) needs to be stable during periods of both high and low inflation and over time. Fixed-income investments, as noted previously, are especially vulnerable to high inflation. To protect against this risk, some fixed-income instruments (like inflation-linked gilts in the United Kingdom and Treasury Inflation-Protected Securities [TIPS] in the United States) feature variable coupon payments tied to the inflation rate. Even with this feature, these investments are vulnerable to fluctuating
rates and do not provide the hedge effectiveness one might expect (as Figure W5.3 shows, their real returns have a -0.41 correlation with inflation).

## Inflation-Linked Annuities

Inflation-adjusted annuities are used to manage both longevity risk and purchasing power risk. National retirement programs like Social Security adjust their benefits to account for inflation changes. Although inflation-adjusted annuities come at a relatively higher cost, the income they generate is highly correlated with inflation. However, the index used to determine annuity payments may not exactly align with what the retiree actually experiences, and fixed percentage increases may not properly compensate for the real inflation impact.

## Borrowing

Taking short positions in negatively correlated assets can also serve as an effective hedge against inflation. For instance, a short position in fixed income (i.e., borrowing) can be beneficial because future outflows to pay back the money will have lower real values due to inflation. Drawbacks of this approach include the addition of leverage to the family balance sheet; assets may be needed as collateral to secure the loan; and inflation risk can be reintroduced if the borrowed funds are reinvested in fixed-income instruments.

## Equity

Although equities are thought to be an effective hedge against inflation, the data from Figure W5.3 shows that the average correlation between inflation and real equity returns is -0.25. This implies relatively poor real returns when inflation is high. If companies can increase prices to compensate for their own increases in costs, they will maintain profit margins and serve as valuable investments during inflationary times. Also, equities will naturally perform better even during high inflation if the economy overall continues to grow.

## Commodities

Whether through direct investments in the spot markets or through indirect investments in derivatives, commodities can serve as a valuable inflation hedge. Production processes often involve commodities as inputs, although CPIs are measures of output prices. Based on Figure W5.3, commodities in the spot market have a 0.10 positive correlation with inflation. Somewhat diminishing this positive correlation is the annual volatility associated with commodities:

- Commodity futures. Because trading and storing physical commodities can be challenging and costly, commodity futures may be a more feasible strategy for many investors. Figure W5.3 shows a positive correlation of 0.21 with inflation. Over the long term, the correlation may prove even more beneficial. Based on work by Ilmanen (2022), energy futures may be useful in hedging cost-push inflation, industrial metals futures may be useful in hedging demand-pull inflation, and precious metals (e.g., gold and silver) may be useful in hedging inflation caused by banking crises and excessive monetary policy. In the context of an overall investment

130
portfolio, the lack of correlation with fixed income and equity returns provides a valuable diversification strategy.

- Gold. Based on Figure W5.3, gold has the highest correlation (0.34) with inflation relative to the other asset classes. Gold is seen as protecting against economic crises and as a store of value. Evidence has shown that gold prices have shown significant volatility around the CPI, which means that the real price of gold has not remained constant as price levels have changed. This calls into question the effectiveness of gold as a true inflation hedge over both the short and long term.

## Real Estate

Real estate as an investment encompasses both residential and commercial property. Commercial real estate includes subdivisions such as industrial buildings, offices, retail, hotels, hospitals, and multifamily dwellings. Much like with equities, even though real estate is thought to serve as an effective inflation hedge, its returns correlation with inflation has been negative.

## Inflation and Taxes

Taxes paid by investors and companies are based on nominal (not real) income and returns. As nominal returns increase based on expected inflation, the taxes paid on those returns increase as well.

### EXAMPLE: The impact of inflation on taxes

An investor has a one-year investment that generates a pretax return of 8%. The applicable capital gains tax rate is 22%. The nominal after-tax value (equivalent to the real after-tax value with no inflation) is, therefore, equal to $1 + 0.08 \times [1 - 0.22] = 1.0624$. Therefore, a $1 investment will be worth $1.0624 after taxes.

If the investor earns the same 8% real return in an environment with 6% inflation (and, therefore, a 14% nominal pretax return), how does the real value compare to the previous real value of $1.0624?

### Answer:

$1 + 0.14 \times [1 - 0.22] = 1.109$ in nominal terms. After adjusting for the 6% inflation rate, the real value is $(1.109 / 1.06) - 1 = 1.0464$. This value of $1.0464 is less than the $1.0624 calculated when there was no inflation.

The following equation is used to determine the after-tax future value for investments taxed using the capital gains rate:


\mathrm{FVIF}_{\mathrm{cgb}} = (1 + \mathrm{r})^{\mathrm{n}} (1 - \mathrm{t}_{\mathrm{cg}}) + \mathrm{t}_{\mathrm{cg}} \mathrm{B}


where:

$\mathrm{FVIF}_{\mathrm{cgb}} =$ future value interest factor

$\mathrm{r} =$ nominal pretax return

$\mathrm{n} =$ number of periods

$\mathrm{t}_{\mathrm{cg}} =$ capital gains tax rate

$\mathrm{B} =$ cost basis as a proportion of current market value
The longer the time horizon and the higher the tax rates, the more impact inflation has on the real after-tax values of investments.

## MODULE QUIZ W5.3

1. An investor earns an 8% nominal pretax return on an investment, with inflation equal to 3%. If the capital gains tax rate is 20%, the investor's real return after accounting for inflation and taxes is closest to:
A. 3.3%.
B. 4.0%.
C. 5.0%.

2. To hedge against inflation and diversify her portfolio, an investor will most likely choose an asset that has:
A. no correlation to either inflation or other portfolio assets.
B. a positive correlation to inflation and negative correlation with other portfolio assets.
C. a negative correlation to inflation and positive correlation with other portfolio assets.

## MODULE W5.4: EXCHANGE RATE RISK AND MITIGATION STRATEGIES

Video covering this content is available online.

**LOS W5.d: Describe how exchange rates influence asset allocation and planning as well as approaches to mitigate the exchange rate risk.**

Exchange rate risk is the risk that individuals and entities face due to fluctuations in foreign exchange rates (the rates at which one currency can be exchanged for another). For investors, a good strategy will involve investing in places with stable currencies and political regimes. Rationales for spreading assets and liabilities across multiple jurisdictions include the following:

- Asset protection from inflation and intentional devaluations of currency. Pressure to reduce debt burdens and increase exports may lead to a government devaluing its own currency.
- Legal protection from third-party claims. Investments in countries where personal liability is limited can be attractive to investors.
- Favorable taxation regimes. Locating assets in jurisdictions where income and capital gains are subject to lower tax rates can be beneficial.
- Protection of assets from confiscation by the government. The stronger the rights regarding private property, the better for investors.

## Balance Sheet Impact of Exchange Rate Risk

To illustrate the impact that exchange rate risk can have on a family's balance sheet, assume the following for the Jacobs family (husband, wife, one son) residing in London, England, but planning to eventually move back to the United States. Also assume a current exchange rate of 1.25 USD/GBP:

132
- The present value of human capital for Rex and Karen Jacobs, paid in GBP, is £8 million; given the current exchange rate, this equates to £8 million × 1.25 = $10,000,000
- Cash holdings in GBP = £75,000 ($93,750)
- Investments in GBP = £1,500,000 ($1,875,000)
- Investments in U.S. dollars = $950,000
- Real estate in London: £800,000 value ($1,000,000), with an outstanding mortgage of £250,000 ($312,500)
- Current spending of £150,000 per year, equating to a present value of £1.1 million; U.S. dollar equivalent = £1.1 million × 1.25 = $1,375,000
- Cost for their son to attend college in the United States = $250,000
- Retirement needs in the United States (present value) = $8,000,000
- Gifts identified in the Jacobs estate = $500,000

Figure W5.4: Jacobs Family Balance Sheet

| Categories and Line Items | Asset (A) / Liability (L) | Value in GBP | Value in USD (1.25/GBP) | Notes |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Human capital | A | 8,000,000 | 10,000,000 | Human capital for both Rex and Karen Jacobs |
| Cash holdings in GBP | A | 75,000 | 93,750 | |
| Investments in GBP | A | 1,500,000 | 1,875,000 | |
| Investments in USD | A | | 950,000 | |
| Real estate asset value | A | 800,000 | 1,000,000 | |
| (Mortgage on real estate) | L | 250,000 | 312,500 | |
| Spending until retirement | L | 1,100,000 | 1,375,000 | Present value of GBP: 150,000 per year |
| Future (college) | L | | 250,000 | |
| Spending needs in retirement | L | | 8,000,000 | |
| Gifts | L | | 500,000 | |
| Surplus | A–L | | 3,481,250 | |

It is useful to state the exchange rate as USD/GBP because the "base currency" for the Jacobs' human capital, some of the investments, the real estate, and their spending in London until retirement is the pound sterling (GBP), while the "price currency" here is U.S. dollars in that most of their retirement goals will need to be met when they retire to the United States. The risk they face comes from changes in exchange rates during their wealth accumulation phase in London.

In Figure W5.4, the Jacobs only have $950,000 in investments denominated in U.S. dollars, but their dollar-denominated liabilities ($8,750,000) and surplus ($3,481,250) total $12,231,250. Because the vast majority of their assets are denominated in pounds, they are effectively long pounds and short dollars with an exposure of $11,281,250 (= $12,231,250 - $950,000). Any depreciation in the pound will decrease the Jacobs' surplus.
The equation used to determine the net asset decline is as follows:


\text{net asset decline} = \text{return}_{\text{base currency}}(\text{asset}_{\text{base currency}} - \text{liabilities}_{\text{base currency}})


where:


\text{return}_{\text{base currency}} = \text{percentage change in the exchange rate of the base currency}


expressed in terms of the price currency; in the Jacobs' example,


\left(\frac{S_{t, \text{price}} / \text{base}}{S_{t-1, \text{price}} / \text{base}} - 1\right)

\text{asset}_{\text{base currency}} = \text{amount of assets denominated in the base currency in terms of the price currency}

\text{liabilities}_{\text{base currency}} = \text{amount of liabilities in the base currency, expressed in terms of the price currency}


The negative impact to the Jacobs family surplus (base of $3,481,250) is illustrated here based on three levels of pound depreciation:

- 5% depreciation: $11,281,250 \times 0.05 = \$564,063$ (surplus decrease of 16.2%)
- 10% depreciation: $11,281,250 \times 0.10 = \$1,128,125$ (surplus decrease of 32.4%)
- 20% depreciation: $11,281,250 \times 0.20 = \$2,256,250$ (surplus decrease of 64.8%)

A wealth manager could consider several approaches for the Jacobs family:

- Passive hedging for short-term and medium-term coverage: shorting GBP/USD forwards or futures, with a notional equivalent to the currency mismatch ($11,281,250)

- A rollover strategy may be incorporated to extend the term, but rollover risk and higher transaction costs have to be considered; longer contract maturities may add costs and reduce tax benefits, reducing the benefit of hedging

- Discretionary hedging where the hedge ratio stays within a range (e.g., 85%–115% of the mismatch)
- A carry trade strategy (as opposed to futures/forwards): borrowing pounds and investing in U.S. dollars
- Identify investment funds that are priced in pounds, but invest in dollar-denominated instruments

Currency (exchange rate) risk poses the following challenges to investors, even those whose exposure is heavily geared toward a currency as generally stable as the U.S. dollar:

- There is no single currency that will universally serve as a store of value under all scenarios.
- There is a natural imbalance between the universe of investable assets and world gross domestic product.
- Due to the political climate globally, the U.S. dollar may face restrictions on usage.

Wealthy families face even more risk due to their global exposure in terms of revenues, expenses, and objectives, their mission to preserve wealth (in real purchasing power), their likelihood of living in multiple places, and the challenges of defining their liabilities with predictability or granularity. A customized currency basket may be a

134
better hedging tool against liability exposures and to manage financial objectives, with weightings for the currencies aligned with the liabilities or objectives. Alternatively, in situations where currency exposures are not well defined or known, a currency index with weightings tied to macroeconomic factors may be beneficial. GDP by country can be used to assign weightings.

A currency basket should be thought of as a unit of account against which to measure returns, as opposed to it being a short-term performance metric. There are two primary advantages of implementing a currency basket index on top of a currency overlay approach:

1. There is a more systematic and disciplined currency allocation.
2. The chosen currency basket does not drive asset allocation decisions.

## MODULE QUIZ W5.4

1. An investor who will be moving to London in the future has EUR 2 million in current assets and GBP 5 million in future liabilities. The current exchange rate is 0.85 GBP/EUR. The investor's position and vulnerability is best described as:

A. short euros and long pounds with an exposure of GBP 4.25 million.
B. short euros and long pounds with an exposure of EUR 4.25 million.
C. long euros and short pounds with an exposure of GBP 3.30 million.

2. Based on historical data from 1900 to 2022, which of the following asset classes would be most beneficial as a hedge against inflation?

A. Spot commodities.
B. Commodity futures.
C. International equities.

## KEY CONCEPTS

## LOS W5.a

The risk management process involves five components: (1) identification, (2) measurement, (3) evaluation, (4) the risk response, and (5) monitoring. The framework used to manage risk is the asset-liability management (ALM) framework. A key element of this framework is a client's surplus, which is their assets less their liabilities.

Risk responses (which are driven by probability and impact) include risk avoidance, risk mitigation, risk transfer, and risk acceptance.

Human capital is thought of as the mortality-adjusted net present value of expected labor income. Human capital, which is a byproduct of earnings correlation, earnings volatility, and replaceability, must be balanced with financial capital (e.g., a client's investments, real estate, and expected inheritances).

Mortality risk is the risk that premature death removes the value of human capital. Longevity risk is the potential for fully depleting financial capital before death.

A safety reserve should be established to reduce the impacts of the capital markets, family obligations, and spending patterns over time.

135
The probability of ruin is the estimated likelihood that an investor will not be able to meet a specific financial goal or obligation due to depleting their financial assets.

Health risk represents the costs associated with unexpected injuries or illnesses and the impacts these have on human capital. Disability relates to injuries or illnesses that inhibit continuous employment.

## LOS W5.b

For events that could have a high impact and low probability of occurrence, insurance is an effective risk transfer strategy. Life insurance involves transferring risk to a third party and serves as a hedge against the risk of premature death for the wage earner. The insurance itself is designed to cover the difference between future earnings and future spending. To calculate the amount of life insurance needed, two methods may be used:

1. Human life value method: an asset-based method estimating the net present value of future earnings less incremental future expenses
2. Needs analysis method: a liability-based method focused on the present value of death-related costs in the short term and ongoing living expenses for surviving family members in the long term

The two primary types are term and permanent. Term life insurance covers the insured for a specific period, and unless renewed, it will expire if the insured survives through the end of the term. Permanent life insurance covers the insured for life, generally with fixed premiums that are partially used to generate cash value.

A popular way to mitigate longevity risk is to use annuities, which offer income for life to the annuitant and potentially their spouse. Similar to a life insurance contract, a basic annuity contract has four primary parties: the annuitant, the contract owner, the insurer, and the beneficiary.

With annuities, the biggest differences lie in the payment amounts (fixed vs. variable) and the timing (deferred vs. immediate).

Fixed annuities include options such as single-premium immediate annuities (SPIAs), inflation-adjusted payments, period certain annuities, lifetime annuities, life annuities with period certain, life annuities with return of premium, joint life annuities, deferred fixed annuities, and advanced life deferred annuities (ALDAs).

Variable annuities include immediate variable annuities and deferred variable annuities with options related to conversion options, returns of premiums, and guaranteed minimum withdrawal benefits.

An annuity offers more income certainty, but it can lead to lower lifetime income and lower wealth upon death. The retirement income efficient frontier is used to model the balance between ending wealth and income security.

Liability risk is the potential legal obligation associated with causing property damage or bodily harm to others. Liability insurance is obtained to manage this risk. Property insurance is designed to reduce the risks tied to assets such as home and automobile. A personal umbrella liability policy is used to supplement home and auto insurance and will pay claims when limits are reached on home or auto insurance.

136
137

# LOS W5.c

Inflation occurs when price levels rise over time, which reduces purchasing power and represents a significant long-term risk. The three types of inflation include demand-pull inflation, cost-push inflation, and unanchored inflation expectations. The inflation rate itself is measured as the percentage change in a price index like the Consumer Price Index (CPI).

The extent to which assets serve as natural inflation hedges will drive the impact of rising price levels. To protect against inflation, an investor's assets must also increase in value to offset the negative impact on liabilities and spending.

The inflation hedge ratio is equal to the relative notional exposure amount for a hedged asset needed to offset any pre-existing exposure to inflation. For a hedge to be effective, correlations are critical. If an asset class has a positive correlation with inflation, it can effectively serve as an inflation hedge. Fixed-rate bonds offer set cash flows and have negative correlations to inflation. Some fixed-income instruments feature variable coupon payments tied to the inflation rate.

Inflation-adjusted annuities are used to manage both longevity risk and the risk associated with reductions in purchasing power.

Taking short positions in negatively correlated assets can also serve as an effective hedge against inflation.

Although equities are thought to be an effective hedge against inflation, their real returns have been relatively poor when inflation is high.

Whether through direct investments in physical commodities in the spot markets or through indirect investments in the commodities derivatives markets, commodities can serve as a valuable inflation hedge. Somewhat diminishing this positive correlation is the annual volatility associated with commodities. Relative to other asset classes, commodity futures and gold have performed relatively well as inflation hedges.

Real estate (residential and commercial) is another hedge option, although correlations with inflation have been negative historically.

Taxes paid by investors and companies are based on nominal (not real) income and returns. As nominal returns increase based on expected inflation, the taxes paid on those returns increases as well. The longer the time horizon and the higher the tax rates, the more impactful it is on the after-tax real values of investments.

# LOS W5.d

Exchange rate risk is the risk that individuals and entities face due to fluctuations in exchange rates (the rates at which one currency is exchanged for another). Rationales for spreading assets and liabilities across multiple jurisdictions include asset protection from intentional devaluations of currency and inflation, legal protection from third-party claims, favorable taxation regimes, and protection of assets from government confiscation.

Exchange rates can have a significant impact on an investor's overall net asset position when they have current assets in one currency and future liabilities in another
currency. To hedge against the risk of exchange rate changes, the approaches a wealth manager could consider include passive hedging for short-term and medium-term coverage; discretionary hedging where the hedge ratio stays within a range; a carry trade strategy; and choosing investments priced in the same currency as the current assets, but which invest in the currency of the future liabilities.

Currency (exchange rate) risk poses challenges because there is no single currency that will universally serve as a store of value under all scenarios; there is a natural imbalance between the universe of investable assets and world GDP (gross domestic product); and due to the political climate globally, even the U.S. dollar may face restrictions on usage.

For wealthy families, they face even more risk due to their global exposure in terms of revenues, expenses, and objectives; their mission to preserve wealth (in real purchasing power); their likelihood of living in multiple places; and the challenges of defining their liabilities with predictability or granularity. A customized currency basket may be a useful hedging tool. When combined with a currency overlay approach, the advantages of a currency basket index include a more systematic and disciplined currency allocation and a currency basket that does not drive asset allocation decisions.

### Module Quiz W5.1

1. A For risks that have a low probability but high impact, the best response is risk transfer, where the burden of the risk is shared by an outside party such as an insurance company. Risk avoidance is appropriate for high-probability, high-impact risks, while risk acceptance is appropriate for low-probability, low-impact risks. (LOS W5.a)
2. A Actual historical returns tend to skew left (negative skew) with fat tails (positive excess kurtosis). (LOS W5.a)

### Module Quiz W5.2

1. C A life annuity with period certain will provide a minimum number of payments, even if an annuitant passes away before the period ends. The beneficiary will receive the remainder of the payments. A lifetime annuity provides payments to the annuitant for the remainder of his or her life, and a joint life annuity provides payments to two annuitants (usually spouses) until both are deceased. (LOS W5.b)
2. C Term life insurance covers the insured for a specific period, such as 20 years. If the insured lives beyond the term period, there is no payout. This form of insurance (regardless of whether premiums gradually increase or remain level) is typically cheaper than whole life or endowment life insurance because there is less risk for the insurer. Some term policies have lower initial premiums that, over time, will increase as the insured gets older, while others will offer a level-term premium

138
where the premiums are higher initially but remain constant throughout the term. (LOS W5.b)

## Module Quiz W5.3

1. A $1 + 0.08 \times [1 - 0.20] = 1.064$ . After adjusting for the $3\%$ inflation rate, the real return equals $(1.064 / 1.03) - 1 = 0.033$ , or $3.3\%$ . (LOS W5.c)
2. B An effective hedge against inflation will involve an asset that has a positive correlation with inflation (i.e., as inflation increases, the real returns on the asset class increase as well). To improve diversification in a portfolio, an investor should add assets that are negatively correlated to each other. (LOS W5.c)

## Module Quiz W5.4

Based on the current exchange rate, the EUR 2 million in assets will translate to $0.85 \times$ EUR 2 million = GBP 1.7 million. Relative to GBP 5 million, this equates to an exposure of GBP 3.3 million. (LOS W5.d)
2. B Based on historical returns (Figure W5.3 as a reference), commodity futures had the highest positive correlation (0.21) to inflation relative to spot commodities (0.10) and international equities (-0.25). The higher the positive correlation, the more effective the hedge. (LOS W5.d)
