READING W6

ADVISING THE WEALTHY

EXAM FOCUS

High-net-worth individuals often need to deal with a wide range of issues such as citizenship, inheritances, succession plans, and tax efficiency. Candidates must understand the pros and cons of dual citizenship and perform the calculations involved in double taxation relief. Relative value calculations for a gift-or-bequest decision are also important. A strong understanding of the strategies to generate full or partial liquidity from a private business is essential. Because over-the-counter derivatives are paramount in hedging concentrated exposures in publicly traded companies, candidates must understand the role of derivatives in hedging. Other important concepts here include equity monetization strategies without a sale, including the ones involving derivatives; cognitive and emotional biases in the context of a concentrated position; and special considerations for managing the wealth of athletes and actors.

MODULE W6.1: MULTIJURISDICTIONAL FAMILIES

LOS W6.a: Discuss and recommend appropriate citizenship, nationality, and legal residency approaches for private clients.

Videocovering this content is available online.

Citizenship

The rise in globalization has resulted in more individuals living and working abroad for extended periods. Such a lifestyle can complicate tax matters. Citizenship and taxation are usually congruent, but issues with individual residency may arise that allow for tax-planning opportunities. Some jurisdictions assess taxes based solely on residency, regardless of citizenship; while others assess tax on all of their citizens, regardless of residency.

Obtaining citizenship in a country typically occurs in one of the following methods:

- Birth. Most countries allow those born in the country to obtain citizenship in that country.
- Extended descent. Numerous countries allow for citizenship based on the citizenship of grandparents (and great-grandparents, in some cases).

140
- Naturalization. Naturalization involves an individual initially entering the country by legal means and then residing in the country for a required time. Before naturalization, a citizenship exam may be required that covers topics such as culture, history, and language. Some countries allow citizenship in one country only, which means naturalization in these countries requires renouncing other citizenships. Other countries permit multiple citizenships.
- Marriage. Marriage to a citizen of a country is a frequently used method of obtaining citizenship and is related to naturalization. A common condition for the spouse seeking citizenship is residency in the country for a minimum number of years.
- Investment. A significant financial contribution to a country, including real estate acquisition, business investment, and deposits of funds in financial institutions, may be a quick way to obtain citizenship. Citizenship by investment is most common in Caribbean countries.

# Dual Citizenship

Dual (or even multiple) citizenship is possible in many instances and comes with numerous potential advantages and disadvantages. Countries such as the United States, United Kingdom, and Australia have dual citizenship provisions, while others such as Argentina have such provisions only with specific countries (e.g., Italy). Dual citizenship is often manageable, but with more than two citizenships, treaty provisions are often insufficient to compensate for the problem of double taxation.

Potential advantages of dual citizenship include the following:

- Asset diversification. The ability to access a wider range of investment opportunities is a benefit of multiple citizenships. This is especially relevant for wealthy families to spread their investments globally and avoid restrictions some countries impose on foreign investments and property ownership.
- Global mobility. Multiple passports, including a passport to a country where it is difficult to obtain a travel visa, often facilitate world travel.
- Tax optimization. With multiple citizenships, an individual can minimize overall global taxes payable by residing in a country with a lower effective tax rate or taking advantage of legally permissible tax planning activities.
- Education and health care. Acquiring multiple citizenships may allow for the choice of where best to educate one's children and access the best or lowest-cost health care.
- Political safety net. Geographic mobility and the ability to change one's country of residence is an advantage during political crises. Risk of expropriation of assets by a given country may be mitigated through appropriate asset transfers to another, more stable country of which the individual is a citizen.

Potential disadvantages of dual citizenship include the following:

- Legal and administrative burden. Significant bureaucracy and complex filing requirements may outweigh the benefits of dual or multiple citizenship. Legal and financial risk may arise from erroneous tax filings or insufficient documentation to support residency.

141
- Double taxation. Taxation of the same income or assets by both countries is a risk if the individual lacks a sufficient grasp of the relevant tax treaties and filing requirements for each jurisdiction. Another tax-related risk is the failure to properly report foreign accounts, which are often complicated and require expert help.
- Cultural challenges. From a purely nonfinancial perspective, one may lack a solid sense of identity and social acceptance when moving between countries without a stable presence in any of them.
- Political and economic risks. Expropriation of assets is a key risk in less stable countries. Additionally, some countries have mandatory military service of a minimum duration or frequency, which may pose a compelling disadvantage in remaining a citizen or even prompt some to renounce their citizenship.

# Residency

Residency is the starting point for determining tax liabilities. The definition of residency is not consistent among all countries, which may lead to an undesirable result of being considered a resident in more than one country and having to pay taxes in multiple countries. However, tax treaties between countries may reduce or eliminate double taxation.

**Tax domicile** is a person's country of residency for tax purposes. Countries use many different tests to determine residency, which may include subjective standards such as personal ties (e.g., family, house) or economic ties (e.g., own a local business) to the country, or objective measures such as the number of days residing within the country's borders.

**Residence jurisdiction** is the most prevalent type, whereby a country taxes the income of its residents, whether generated inside or outside the country. In other words, all residents of the country (whether they are citizens or noncitizens) are taxed on their worldwide income. The country does not impose residence jurisdiction on its citizens who are non-residents.

Under **source jurisdiction** (or a **territorial tax system**), a country levies taxes on all income generated within its borders, whether by citizens or non-citizens.

# Relief From Double Taxation

Due to overlapping tax systems, two countries may claim the right to tax the same income or assets. In a **residence-residence conflict**, for example, two countries claim residence for the same individual and taxing authority over the individual's worldwide assets and income. Alternatively, two countries could claim authority over the same income in a **source-source conflict** (think of a multinational company with operations generating income in several countries).

In another possible double taxation scenario, an individual might be subject to residence jurisdiction and receive income from assets in a foreign country that has source jurisdiction. This is a **residence-source conflict** because the individual's worldwide assets and income are taxed by the residence jurisdiction, and income generated by the foreign assets is taxed again under the source jurisdiction.

142
Some countries have adopted policies that help relieve double taxation:

- The **exemption method** provides complete relief from double taxation. Suppose an individual is subject to a 50% residence tax and has $100 of income from a source-taxation country with a tax rate of 40%. Under the exemption method, $40 is owed to the source country, but the entire $100 is exempt from tax in the residence country. For this method, the tax rate equals the foreign tax rate.

- The **credit method** also provides complete relief, but not always the lowest total tax. In this case, $40 is owed to the source country and $50 to the residence country, but the taxpayer can claim a $40 credit against the $50, leaving only $10 owed to the residence country. This makes the total tax owed to both countries $50. Had the rates been reversed, $50 would have been owed to the source country and $40 to the residence country, but credit for taxes paid would reduce the residence country tax bill to zero. The total tax bill would still be $50. The tax rate for the credit method is Max(domestic tax rate, foreign tax rate).

- The **deduction method** only partially resolves a residence-source conflict. Under the deduction method, the individual pays the full tax to the source country and is only allowed to deduct the amount of taxes paid to the source country in calculating total worldwide income. The tax rate for this method is: domestic tax rate + foreign tax rate – (domestic tax rate × foreign tax rate).

## EXAMPLE: Residence-source conflict

An individual living in a country that bases income tax on residency has total worldwide income of $1.5 million. Of that amount, $0.6 million is generated in a source jurisdiction country. The domestic country charges 40% income taxes on worldwide income, and the source country charges 35% taxes on income generated within its borders.

Determine the income taxes paid on the foreign source income and the country or countries to which it is owed under the following methods:

1. Exemption method
2. Credit method
3. Deduction method

## Answer:

1. Exemption method
- Source country tax: $600,000 × 35% = $210,000
- Residency country: the income is exempt
- Total taxes: $210,000

2. Credit method
- Source country tax: $600,000 × 35% = $210,000
- Residency country tax would have been: $600,000 × 40% = $240,000, and taking a $210,000 credit leaves $30,000 owed to residence country
- Total taxes: $240,000

143
144

# 3. Deduction method

- Source country tax: $600,000 × 35% = $210,000
- Residency country taxable income: $600,000 - $210,000 = $390,000; tax is $390,000 × 40% = $156,000 to the residence country
- Total taxes: $366,000. [Alternatively, 40% + 35% - (40% × 35%) = 61%, and 61% × $600,000 = $366,000.]

Foreign tax credits are applied differently by different jurisdictions. For example, some countries allow for credit of all three of gift, estate, and income taxes, while others only allow for one or two of these. Also, the three tax relief methods just described are general in nature; a given country may use one of the methods but with some alterations, or perhaps use a completely different method specific to that country.

# Double Taxation Treaties (DTTs)

DTTs are another means of overcoming the effects of double taxation. The OECD Model Treaty is the basis of all DTTs and recommends either the credit or exemption methods. The model treaty also contains provisions involving the sharing of taxpayer information between countries. This may motivate some taxpayers to avoid residing in a country that has a DTT with the country where the taxpayer earns most of their income. The model treaty also contains provisions to fight **treaty shopping**, or benefiting from tax treaty provisions despite having no legitimate economic basis in the countries involved.

Under the model treaty, the residence country is usually given priority for taxation (including capital gains), although an exception exists for real estate, where taxes are due in the country in which the real estate is located (the source state). Withholding tax rates are generally limited to 15% for dividends and 10% for interest, which covers the tax liability to the source country under the exemption method. Additional taxes may be due to the residence country when using the credit or deduction method.

Most DTTs concentrate on income taxes because not all tax jurisdictions have estate or gift taxes. Also, residence-residence conflicts may be resolved through a series of tiebreakers outlined in the model treaty as follows (in order): (1) location of permanent home; (2) where most business or employment is conducted; (3) location of principal dwelling; (4) country of citizenship.

# Ceasing Tax Residency

Ceasing tax residency in one country often involves setting up permanent residency in, and transferring day-to-day activities to, a new country. The intent of the new residency must be long-term in nature. Factors that support establishing long-term tax residency in a new country include a purchase or long-term lease of a dwelling in the new jurisdiction and a spouse or partner and dependent family members moving to the new country.

Changing residency within a country (e.g., states within the United States) is usually much easier and is commonly done to reduce overall tax liabilities.
145

# Renouncing Citizenship

To avoid residence taxation, individuals may renounce their citizenship and move to a less-strict jurisdiction. For example, because the United States taxes its citizens on their worldwide income, some taxpayers renounce their U.S. citizenship to reduce their overall tax liabilities. Another possible reason to renounce citizenship is to legally exempt oneself from military duties, especially in a country prone to conflict.

Some residence jurisdictions impose an exit tax. The amount is usually based on gains on the assets leaving the jurisdiction as the individual sold the assets and realized the gains (known as a deemed disposition). The exit tax could include a tax on income earned for a period (called a shadow period) following the expatriation.

For example, consider an individual relocating to Country B (lower tax rates) from Country A (higher tax rates) where Country A imposes a 20% exit tax on deemed capital gains. Deemed capital gains are based on the fair market value of the assets (assume $8 million) immediately preceding the exit and the original cost of the assets (assume $3 million). Therefore, the exit tax payable to Country A is calculated as 20% × ($8 million – $3 million) = $1 million. This is a one-time payment, after which those assets will incur no further tax in Country A. If Country A and Country B have a tax treaty, it may provide for a credit of the taxes paid to Country A against future taxes owed in Country B.

# Investment Visas

Investment visas are an easier way to stay in a country and be considered a legal resident, compared to full citizenship. Work visas and student visas are often a short-term means of residency for employment or education. In some countries, visa options may ultimately grant permanent residency, but not full citizenship.

Investor visas usually provide residency to an individual on the condition that they provide investment capital for local businesses. Full citizenship may eventually be granted, but not always.

Golden visas go much further. In addition to the usual residency and work permits, they may provide full citizenship in exchange for substantial investments. In that regard, golden visas provide a means for the wealthy to move their assets to a country that may offer them preferential tax treatment and protect their assets from creditors.

## MODULE QUIZ W6.1

1. Sal Smythe resides in Country X, which imposes a residence tax of 30% on investment returns. He is considering an investment in Country V, which has a source tax of 20% and an assumed return of $100. What is the tax he owes to Country X if there is a deduction method tax treaty?

A. $10.
B. $24.
C. $30.

MODULE W6.2: COMPLEX FAMILIES

Video covering this content is available online.

LOS W6.b: Discuss and recommend appropriate private wealth management approaches that maximize the human capital, financial capital, and economic net worth of complex family situations.

In modern-day wealth management we must consider family structures other than the traditional ones. These may include past marriages and cohabitation arrangements as an alternative to traditional marriage. Depending on the legal system involved, the outcome of wealth distribution may conflict with the owner's intentions.

## Legal Systems

Three general legal systems exist: (1) civil law, (2) common law, and (3) Shari'a law.

The civil law system is based on old Roman law and is frequently used throughout the world. In this system, laws are handed down (i.e., a "top-down" approach) by a legislative body. For cases brought to the courts, the courts must consider those laws when making judgments.

The common law system is based primarily on old English law and is more of a "bottom-up" approach. This system uses case law, which consists of past court decisions. Judges play important roles here by refining existing laws to meet particular situations. Once made by a judge, these decisions become precedents to be applied to future cases.

Shari'a law applies in Islamic countries and is similar to civil law in terms of how laws are enacted.

## Divorce

Some countries may recognize the rights of those involved in long-term domestic arrangements, while others only recognize rights when a formal marriage is in place.

Spouses can have marital property rights. Under a community property regime, each spouse is entitled to one-half of the estate earned during the marriage. Under a separate property regime, each spouse owns and controls their own property, separate from the other. Gifts and inheritances received before or after the marriage may be held separate from marital assets.

The division of financial assets if a marriage ends should be addressed in advance. This is often done through prenuptial agreements (before marriage). Depending on the country, and especially in a community property rights regime, such agreements may not be binding. Postnuptial agreements occur after the marriage is over and deal with financial matters after the marriage (e.g., alimony or child support).
When a divorce is contemplated, a full listing of assets is the usual starting point. This may become extremely complicated with ultra-high-net-worth families, which often disburse their assets in numerous jurisdictions and investment vehicles to minimize taxes and for legal and privacy reasons. The need to make payments and set up a second household often imposes liquidity needs. Therefore, setting aside sufficient assets or selling redundant assets may be required.

Assets to be distributed on a marriage dissolution include the following:

- Marital residence. Some countries may provide preferential rights to the marital home to the spouse who has greater custody of the children. In addition, factors such as the property rights regime in place and any prenuptial or postnuptial agreements may dictate the final outcome. The most practical solution may sometimes be to sell the home and divide the proceeds in amounts agreeable to all parties.
- Financial assets. The division of financial assets is usually based on whether the jurisdiction follows a community property or separate property regime.
- Pensions and retirement accounts. Division of such accounts has considerable variety depending on the jurisdiction. Ensuring the beneficiary designations are correct and up to date is crucial, because these often take priority over, for example, the provisions of a will.
- Inheritances. In community property regimes, many assets owned before marriage are considered community property that is subject to division. A key exception is premarital inheritances, which are often considered separate property that is not subject to division. Of course, inheritances received during a marriage are considered community property.

For separate property regimes, all assets owned, gifted, or inherited before marriage are considered separate property that is not subject to division. Inheritances received during marriage are also considered separate property, but care must be taken not to combine inheritance funds with other marital funds, or else the inheritance funds can become marital property. Income and gains on the inheritance funds might be subject to division in some jurisdictions. A separate asset such as a family business could become a marital asset if the non-owning spouse was a main cause of its value appreciation.

# Cohabitation

Cohabitation involves two individuals in a committed relationship who live together but are not married. Depending on the jurisdiction, this arrangement may result in significant limitations in terms of their rights and obligations to each other. Cohabitation does not include the same rights and obligations as marriage. A cohabitation agreement may be used to clarify the relationship and provide a basis for allocating assets and liabilities on dissolution of the relationship or the death of either individual. Careful documentation of asset ownership and continual updating of the agreement increases the likelihood that each individual will be protected.

In jurisdictions that do not place much weight on cohabitation agreements, more traditional measures such as wills and trusts may be necessary to achieve the desired

147
asset distribution. Cohabitation agreements may be more effective in separate property jurisdictions, where property distributions are open to more than a simple 50/50 split of assets.

## Same-Sex Couples

The key issue is whether the jurisdiction legally recognizes same-sex couples. If so (most likely in the Americas, Europe, and Australia), financial planning has few (if any) differences from opposite-sex couples. If not, significant hurdles may need to be overcome, given the greater complexity involved in wealth planning. Proper documentation of asset ownership and carefully worded cohabitation agreements become extremely important in overcoming such hurdles.

## Capital Transfers Within Families

The two primary means of transferring assets are through gifts and bequests. Gifts refer to lifetime gratuitous transfers or inter vivos (between living individuals) transfers and may be subject to gift taxes. Whether the gift is taxed and who pays the tax is determined by the authorities involved. Bequests refer to testamentary (after-death) gratuitous transfers and may be subject to estate taxes (paid by the grantor or transferor) or inheritance taxes (paid by the recipient).

Determining whether to gift or bequest an asset involves quantitative factors (e.g., gift taxes, estate taxes, exemptions to these taxes, the relative tax rates of the transferor and the recipient, and after-tax compounded returns) as well as qualitative factors such as loss of control of the funds and the financial maturity and responsibility of the recipient.

A client with excess capital can gift the capital before death or bequest it at death. Gifting likely gives up control and cannot be revoked if circumstances change. Tax issues can affect the ultimate value of a gift or bequest to the receiver. Annual tax-free gifts (up to specified limits) can be used for wealth transfers to maximize value and reduce estate taxes at death. The value of such gifts may be substantial for multiple givers and receivers within a family.

One approach is to calculate a ratio of a gift now versus a bequest at death, called a relative value (RV):


\mathrm{RV} = \frac{\mathrm{FV\ after-tax\ to\ the\ receiver\ if\ gifted\ now}}{\mathrm{FV\ after-tax\ to\ the\ receiver\ if\ bequeat\hed\ at\ death}}


An RV above or below 1 indicates that from a tax perspective, it is favorable or unfavorable to gift now.

Any FV after-tax calculations require assumptions, and the conclusions are only as good as these assumptions. There are three tax scenarios to consider:

1. The gift now is tax-free to both the receiver and the giver (donor).
2. The gift now is taxable, with the tax paid by the receiver.
3. The gift now is taxable, with the tax paid by the giver.

148
Relevant tax factors to consider include:

- $r_{\mathrm{g}}$ and $t_{\mathrm{ig}}$: the pretax return earned, and the applicable tax rate on those earnings for assets held by the gift receiver
- $r_{\mathrm{e}}$ and $t_{\mathrm{ie}}$: the pretax return earned, and the applicable tax rate on those earnings for assets held by the gift giver
- $T_{\mathrm{e}}$: the estate tax rate, and would be paid from the estate
- $T_{\mathrm{g}}$: the gift tax rate, paid by the giver (or by the receiver, if specified by the facts in a question)

The three potential RV ratios are as follows:

1. RV of a tax-free gift, $T_{\mathrm{g}} = 0$:


\mathrm{RV}_{\text{tax-free gift}} = \frac{\left[1 + \mathrm{r}_{\mathrm{g}} (1 - \mathrm{t}_{\mathrm{ig}}) \right]^{\alpha}}{\left[1 + \mathrm{r}_{\mathrm{e}} (1 - \mathrm{t}_{\mathrm{ie}}) \right]^{\alpha} (1 - \mathrm{T}_{\mathrm{e}})}


2. RV of a taxable gift, $T_{\mathrm{g}}$ paid by receiver:


\mathrm{RV}_{\text{taxable gift}} = \frac{\left[1 + \mathrm{r}_{\mathrm{g}} (1 - \mathrm{t}_{\mathrm{ig}}) \right]^{\alpha} (1 - \mathrm{T}_{\mathrm{g}})}{\left[1 + \mathrm{r}_{\mathrm{e}} (1 - \mathrm{t}_{\mathrm{ie}}) \right]^{\alpha} (1 - \mathrm{T}_{\mathrm{e}})}


3. RV of a taxable gift, $T_{\mathrm{g}}$ paid by giver:


\mathrm{RV}_{\text{taxable gift}} = \frac{\left[1 + \mathrm{r}_{\mathrm{g}} (1 - \mathrm{t}_{\mathrm{ig}}) \right]^{\alpha} (1 - \mathrm{T}_{\mathrm{g}} + \mathrm{T}_{\mathrm{g}} \mathrm{T}_{\mathrm{e}})}{\left[1 + \mathrm{r}_{\mathrm{e}} (1 - \mathrm{t}_{\mathrm{ie}}) \right]^{\alpha} (1 - \mathrm{T}_{\mathrm{e}})}


Notice that each formula is a cumulative variation of the previous formula:

- RV of a tax-free gift, $T_{\mathrm{g}} = 0$. The numerator projects the FV after tax of the investment if held by the receiver. The denominator projects the FV after tax if held by the giver, and then subject to estate taxes.
- RV of a taxable gift, $T_{\mathrm{g}}$ paid by receiver. This is a variation of the tax-free gift formula with $-T_{\mathrm{g}}$ in the numerator to reflect that the receiver of the gift must pay a tax and has less to invest. All else the same, this scenario makes gifting now less attractive.
- RV of a taxable gift, $T_{\mathrm{g}}$ paid by giver. This is a variation of the taxable gift if paid by receiver formula with $+T_{\mathrm{g}}T_{\mathrm{e}}$ in the numerator. All else the same, the giver's estate is reduced, so the future estate tax will be lower. One interpretation is that gifting now creates a partial gift tax credit against the estate tax bill. Also, with cross-border gifts, there is the risk of double taxation if both the giver and receiver are subject to taxes in their respective countries. If the giver is responsible for paying the gift tax but is unable to do so (e.g., the gift is an illiquid asset and the giver does not have cash), the tax authorities may deem the receiver to be responsible for payment.

## EXAMPLE: Gifting now vs. a bequest

Mary Paxton is considering making a gift now or a bequest at death. The assumptions are as follows:

149
- Life expectancy is 20 years.
- Paxton's and the receiver's pretax return and investment tax rate are both 8% and 35%, respectively.
- $\mathrm{T_g}$ and $\mathrm{T_e}$ are 25% and 40%.

Scenario 1: Assuming the gift is not subject to gift taxes, **calculate** the relative attractiveness of a gift or bequest and **recommend** the best approach.

Scenario 2: Assuming the gift is subject to gift taxes and the tax is paid by the receiver, **calculate** the relative attractiveness of a gift or bequest and **recommend** the best approach.

Scenario 3: Assuming the gift is subject to gift taxes and the tax is paid by the giver, **calculate** the relative attractiveness of a gift or bequest and **recommend** the best approach.

Answer:

Scenario 1


[1 + 0.08(1 - 0.35))]^{20} / [(1 + (0.08(1 - 0.35)))^{20} (1 - 0.40)] = 2.756 / ((2.756)(0.60)) = 2.756 / 1.654 = 1.67


Gifting now is more attractive. Also notice that because the giver and the receiver's after-tax investment return is assumed to be the same, the ratio is just $1 / (1 - \mathrm{T_e})$, a special case of the formula.

Scenario 2


[(1 + (0.08(1 - 0.35)))^{20}(1 - 0.25)] / [(1 + (0.08(1 - 0.35)))^{20} (1 - 0.40)] = ((0.75)(2.756)) / ((2.756)(0.60)) = 2.067 / 1.654 = 1.25


Gifting now is not as favorable, but it is still the best option. Also notice that because the giver and receiver's after-tax investment return is assumed to be the same, the ratio is just $(1 - \mathrm{T_g}) / (1 - \mathrm{T_e})$, a special case of the formula.

Scenario 3


[(1 + (0.08(1 - 0.35)))^{20}(1 - 0.25 + (0.25)(0.40))] / [(1 + (0.08(1 - 0.35)))^{20} (1 - 0.40)] = ((0.75 + 0.10)(2.756)) / ((2.756)(0.60)) = 2.343 / 1.654 = 1.42


Gifting now is still the best option, and it is more attractive than if the receiver pays the tax. Also notice that because the giver and receiver's after-tax investment return is assumed to be the same, the ratio is just $(1 - \mathrm{T_g} + \mathrm{T_eT_g}) / (1 - \mathrm{T_e})$, a special case of the formula.

150
151

# Spousal Exemptions

Many countries allow tax-free transfers of estates between spouses. Whether that is optimal from a tax perspective depends on other possible gift and inheritance exemptions. For example, assume tax laws permit tax-free transfers of estates less than $500,000. If the decedent leaves a large enough estate that the surviving spouse has sufficient funds for living expenses, $500,000 of the estate could be transferred immediately to the children tax-free. If the surviving spouse waited until later to transfer the assets, the children might be subject to gift or inheritance taxes.

# Deemed Dispositions

Some jurisdictions (e.g., Canada) do not have estate or inheritance taxes, but consider bequests (and possibly gifts) to be deemed dispositions at fair market value. This results in capital gains taxes payable by the transferor based on the cost basis of the assets subject to the bequest.

## MODULE QUIZ W6.2

1. Jan Jones has excess capital and is only concerned with maximizing the value of funds passed on to her heirs. She has an assumed after-tax return on investments of 8.5% with a tax rate on investments of 33%. Her heirs have an assumed pretax return on investments of 8% with a tax rate on investments of 25%. Gift and estate tax rates are 40% and 30%, respectively.

A. If the gift would fall within the gift tax exclusion amount, **discuss** whether it is better to gift now or make a bequest.

B. If the gift would exceed the gift tax exclusion amount, **discuss** whether it is better to gift now or make a bequest.

C. **Discuss** any nonmonetary considerations in deciding whether to make a gift versus a bequest.

# MODULE W6.3: ENTREPRENEURS AND BUSINESSOWNERS

**LOS W6.c: Discuss and recommend appropriate private wealth management approaches that maximize the human capital, financial capital, and economic net worth of entrepreneurs and business owners.**

Through work, inheritance, entrepreneurship, or other means, individuals may come to hold concentrated positions in a single asset. A concentrated position can lead to inefficient asset allocation. An investment advisor often works with the client to sell or otherwise monetize the asset. Such decisions often involve illiquidity, tax, legal, and emotional (behavioral finance) issues.

An entrepreneur’s human capital is similar to equity in the sense that the value of the business starts off low at the beginning, and with success over time, its value increases along with the entrepreneur’s human capital. This is different than the human capital of a typical wage earner, which is similar to a bond in its predictability and stability over time. Entrepreneurs must deal with greater nonsystematic (unique) risk in their human
capital because they usually focus in one specific sector or industry, and being an entrepreneur inherently involves uncertainty.

Concentration in specific assets is a key risk faced by many successful entrepreneurs. Those assets are usually one or more of the following: (1) private company shares, (2) publicly traded shares, or (3) real estate, usually related to the private company's operations. A concentrated position often has a low tax basis that would give rise to a significant capital gain on disposition.

As a guideline, any position that is 25% or more of total net worth is considered concentrated. Due to factors such as size, liquidity, operating history, level of business diversification, access to financing, and management talent, concentrated positions in private companies are significantly riskier than positions in public companies.

Concentrated positions involve both systematic (diversifiable) and nonsystematic (non-diversifiable) risk, with nonsystematic risk being either company-specific or property-specific. Nonsystematic risk is inefficient from a portfolio perspective because it increases the standard deviation of returns without additional expected return. This can be illustrated with the following example.

## EXAMPLE: Concentrated position risk management

Assume an investor holds an initial portfolio valued at $100 in a single stock with a zero-cost basis. The investor can hold the asset for 20 years or sell it and invest the after-tax proceeds in a diversified stock portfolio. In either case, at the end of 20 years, the portfolio is liquidated, and any additional taxes are paid.

| | Single Stock | Diversified Portfolio |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Annual expected return | 10.0% | 10.0% |
| Capital gains tax | 20.0% | 20.0% |
| Dividend | 0.0% | 1.2% |
| Standard deviation | 40.0% | 17.0% |

Compare the risk and return characteristics of holding the single stock or a diversified portfolio.

## Answer:

- Both portfolios have positive skew. The downside is limited to a 100% loss, while the probability of exceeding a 100% gain over a 20-year time horizon is significant.
- The median outcome is higher for the diversified portfolio, reflecting its lower probability of suffering a large or total loss.
- The single-asset portfolio has more extreme positive skew for two reasons:

1. A higher average ending value, reflecting the benefit of tax deferral (the full $100 can be compounded in the single-asset portfolio, while selling the stock and reinvesting in the diversified portfolio incurs an initial $20 capital gains tax, allowing only $80 to compound over time).
2. A much higher probability of a 100% loss due to bankruptcy of a single asset.

152
The single-asset portfolio appears superior when looking at only average ending value. However, when downside risk is considered, the diversified portfolio is superior for most investors.

Property-specific risk for real estate is the direct counterpart to company-specific risk for a company. This is the additional, diversifiable risk associated with owning a specific property. It may arise, for example, from the discovery of environmental pollution on the site, or the loss of a key tenant and rental income.

Illiquidity and high transaction costs are common issues with concentrated positions in private companies and real estate. The expense of finding a buyer can be substantial, and the intended use by the prospective buyer may affect the price.

Five common objectives when managing a concentrated position are:

1. Reduce both the systematic and nonsystematic risk caused by wealth concentration.
2. Overcome psychological resistance to reducing idiosyncratic risk.
3. Generate liquidity to meet diversification or spending needs.
4. Optimize tax efficiency to maximize after-tax ending value and manage cash outflows.
5. Transfer wealth within the family or to a charity.

# Goals-Based Planning With Concentrated Positions

A goals-based decision process modifies traditional mean-variance analysis to accommodate the insights of behavioral finance theory. This approach divides a portfolio into tiers of a pyramid, or risk buckets, with each tier or bucket designed to meet a different level of client goals. The risk buckets and sequence of priority are as follows:

1. Allocate funds to a personal risk bucket to protect the client from poverty or a drastic decline in lifestyle. Low-risk assets such as money market funds, as well as the personal residence, are held in this bucket. Safety is emphasized, but a below-market return is likely.
2. Next, allocate funds to a market risk bucket to maintain the client's existing standard of living. Portfolio assets in this bucket are allocated to stocks and bonds earning an expected market return.
3. Remaining portfolio funds are allocated to an aspirational risk bucket, holding positions such as a private business, concentrated stock holdings, real estate investments, and other riskier positions. Allocating these holdings to the aspirational risk bucket highlights their risky nature for the client. If successful, these high-risk investments can substantially improve the client's standard of living.

To implement a goals-based plan, the manager and client must determine the primary capital necessary to meet the goals of the first two risk buckets and the amount of any remaining surplus capital to meet aspirational goals. If a concentrated holding in the aspirational bucket leaves insufficient funds for the first two primary capital buckets,

153
the manager and client should discuss selling or monetizing the concentrated position. Some important questions to address are as follows:

- What are the client's lifetime spending needs and desires?
- What is the present value of those needs and desires?
- What is the value of the concentrated position? Do different approaches to sale or monetization produce different values?
- What other assets does the client have, and how liquid are they?
- Would selling or monetizing the concentrated position be sufficient to fund any shortfall in primary capital?

## EXAMPLE: Goals-based planning

Bill Cook is the founder, manager, and 100% owner of a private company. Through hard work, he has built his company to an estimated value of GBP 30 million. His tax basis is near zero, and he has been planning for his daughter to take over the business when he retires in three years. He was shocked when she recently announced that she is quitting the business to pursue different interests. Both of his grandchildren work at the company and are advancing rapidly in their business skills. Cook has a home (mortgage-free) and shorter-term government bonds valued at GBP 0.9 million. In addition, he has a stock and bond portfolio valued at GBP 1.7 million. He also owns the land and building where his business is located, which are valued at GBP 2 million and are fully mortgaged for that amount.

1. Determine the holdings and value of Cook's three risk buckets.
2. Recommend the next steps that Cook should take in structuring a financial plan.

## Answer:

1. Personal risk bucket of GBP 0.9 million, comprising his home and short-term bonds
- Market risk bucket of GBP 1.7 million, comprising the stocks and other bonds
- Aspirational risk bucket of GBP 30 million, comprising the private company plus the land and building less mortgage.

Working with an investment manager, Cook determines that he requires GBP 20 million today to fund the future value of his primary goals and objectives in retirement. Until retirement, his income from the business will fund his expenses.

2. Cook has only GBP 2.6 million in primary capital and GBP 30 million allocated to surplus capital. He has insufficient primary capital to fund the GBP 20 million to meet his primary needs. His allocation to higher-risk assets is too large. In addition, he will need a successor to manage the business when he retires. He must develop a sale or monetization strategy that will (1) increase his allocation to primary capital and (2) find other management for the company.

Giving up control of the business is no longer an issue as he will not be leaving the business to his daughter, but he should structure a plan so that he can continue to

154
work for the planned three years until retirement. As is common for an entrepreneur who builds a business over time, the tax basis of his holding is essentially zero. He currently has a GBP 30 million unrealized gain and a substantial tax liability if he sells.

Constraints of holding concentrated positions include the following:

1. There is illiquidity before full growth potential of the business; a few more years may be required before selling or giving up control.
2. The nature of the product (i.e., life cycle duration) and the owner's age may affect the time frame. More time may allow the concentration to be reduced gradually, which decreases the overall tax cost.
3. Owners may desire to maintain sole voting control or control within a family or key employees, particularly if the concentrated position consists of real estate.
4. Executives of public companies may receive stock as part of a compensation package, with company expectations or regulatory requirements that the executive will hold the stock for a certain length of time.
5. Psychological considerations (e.g., emotional attachments) may make an owner reluctant to reduce exposure to a concentrated position.

## Strategies for Managing Private Company Concentrated Positions

Private companies tend to be worth in the range of USD 10 million to USD 500 million. They may be extremely concentrated, as the owner might hold 100% of the company and it may be close to 100% of owner's net worth. This leads to high stand-alone and nonsystematic risk, together with the private company shares likely being illiquid. A relative lack of other liquid assets often gives rise to the phrase "asset rich, cash poor."

The owner may derive a large part of self-worth and income from the business, and the owner's business and personal life are often intermixed. This can make the owner resist selling the business or cause the owner to overestimate the company's value. If the company has been passed down through inheritance, the owner can have a strong sense of attachment to it.

Private company owners often seek liquidity for living needs or diversification. Achieving sufficient liquidity is often difficult or impossible while owning private company shares. Therefore, the owner may need to sell (partially or fully) to meet liquidity needs and reduce concentration risk, especially if no one in the family is willing or able to continue the business after the owner retires.

## Tax Implications of a Sale

Selling the entire position, which usually has a high market value and low tax cost basis, means incurring a significant tax liability, but then the after-tax funds may be used to invest in a more diversified portfolio. Alternatively, disposing of a concentrated position gradually spreads the tax liability over multiple years and is an example of tax deferral. A staged diversification strategy allows the owner to maintain some control

155
and gradually give it up over time, which may be more psychologically and financially appealing than selling the entire position in one transaction. This also means that the concentrated position is maintained for a longer time.

Some tax laws allow a tax-free step-up in basis if one holds an asset to death, which is a legal form of tax avoidance.

Assuming a progressive tax system, recognizing capital gains gradually over time may have significant financial benefits, as demonstrated in the following example.

## EXAMPLE: Impact of progressive tax system on sale

An investor in the country of Mytopia has a concentrated position with an unrealized capital gain of MYT 800,000. The relevant capital gains tax rates are as follows:

- 10% for gains up to MYT 100,000
- 20% for gains between MYT 100,001 and MYT 250,000
- 30% for gains over MYT 250,000

Ignoring the time value of money, **calculate** and **discuss** the differences between an immediate sale of the entire position and a staged sale (evenly over five years).

## Answer:

**Alternative 1: immediate sale of entire position**

The tax liability is calculated as (MYT 100,000 × 10%) + (MYT 150,000 × 20%) + (MYT 550,000 × 30%) = MYT 205,000 and is payable immediately, so there is no tax deferral opportunity.

**Alternative 2: gradual sale of entire position**

The MYT 800,000 unrealized capital gain is spread out over five years, resulting in an MYT 160,000 realized capital gain each year. The annual tax liability is calculated as (MYT 100,000 × 10%) + (MYT 60,000 × 20%) = MYT 22,000. The total payable over five years is MYT 22,000 × 5 = MYT 110,000, which is substantially less than the MYT 205,000. Also, the total of MYT 110,000 is payable over five years, so there is a tax deferral opportunity.

## Sale Strategies

A sale can occur in one or more of the following ways: (1) selling to an external party, (2) selling to an insider, (3) recapitalization, (4) divesting redundant assets, or (5) initial public offering. The company might also issue debt, but private company debt is less marketable to investors than public company debt.

Ultimately, the after-tax proceeds are what matter most. These depend on tax rates, the timing of cash receipts (e.g., up-front, deferred, contingent), and the likelihood of

156
receipt. Strategies to consider in managing a private business position include the following:

- Strategic buyers take a buy-and-hold perspective and generally offer the highest price to the seller. Strategic buyers seek to combine the business with an existing business.
- A financial buyer is often a private equity fund planning to restructure the business, add value, and resell the business (typically in a three-to-five-year period). They generally purchase more mature, established businesses and offer a lower price than a strategic buyer. Also, the fund is likely to require the seller to maintain an ownership percentage and continue managing the business during the fund's holding period.
- In a management buyout (MBO), the position is sold to existing key employees who are presumed to know the business. However, the drawbacks are as follows:
- Generally, the buyers will only purchase at a discounted price.
- The buyers may lack financial resources and expect the existing owner to finance a significant portion of the purchase with a loan or a promissory note, which is a promise that the buyers will pay in the future. The seller faces risks of delinquent payments and defaults, which may require extensions to the repayment terms.
- The promissory note is often contingent on future performance of the business, but current employees or managers might lack the skills or entrepreneurial spirit to run the business properly.
- Should an MBO be unsuccessful, the terms often involve the seller reassuming ownership and management of the company.
- In a leveraged recapitalization, the owner might retain 20% to 40% of the equity capital and sell 60% to 80% of his shares back to the company. The owner continues to manage the business with a significant financial stake. A private equity firm could arrange the financing for the company to purchase the owner's stock. In exchange, the private equity firm receives equity in the company. This could be part of a phased exit strategy for the owner—sell and receive cash for a portion of their equity in the initial transaction, then participate in and sell the remaining shares when the private equity firm resells their position in a few years. The owner owes taxes on the cash received in the initial recapitalization, but additional taxes are deferred until the owner sells the remaining stock. Recapitalization is generally used for established but less mature (middle-market) companies.
- With an employee stock ownership plan (ESOP), the owner sells stock to the ESOP, which in turn sells the shares to company employees. In the United States (subject to additional restrictions), for example, tax laws may encourage the sale to an ESOP because the owner's sale of shares might not trigger a capital gains tax. Overall, the ESOP is another method for the seller to achieve a phased exit, thereby extending control for a few more years. However, ESOPs entail incremental administrative costs.

Although these strategies may achieve their purpose of monetization and generating liquidity, they may not remove risk fully. The seller may be required to maintain a small equity position or accept a creditor position (e.g., promissory note), both of which involve risk.

157
An initial public offering (IPO) is often a desired outcome, assuming the company is large enough, and it may be extremely lucrative for the owner despite significant transaction costs. Generally, IPO purchasers expect the owner to retain a significant ownership stake and continue to manage the business, so this is not an immediate exit strategy. The owner faces the increased pressure (e.g., earnings expectations) and reduced privacy of running a public company.

A sale or gift to family members could be structured to take advantage of favorable tax laws (e.g., annual gift tax exemptions, if they exist). In the case of a sale, if the family members lack the financial resources to pay cash, the existing owner can structure the transaction like an MBO and accept a promissory note for the purchase price. Unfortunately, neither a gift nor an MBO-style sale provides the existing owner much immediate cash flow to satisfy their objectives.

158

## EXAMPLE: Strategic buyer, financial buyer, MBO, gift

We previously considered Bill Cook. He has taken no actions regarding his ownership of a private company. His health has seriously deteriorated and is not expected to improve, but the company has thrived. Cook believes that the company is worth "at least GBP 50 million." He wishes to retire next year and is now highly motivated to find the cash to fund a diversified portfolio of liquid stocks and bonds that will support his retirement and meet his medical expenses. He is no longer concerned about passing the company to family members.

He is considering several alternatives:

A. Sell 100% of the company to a strategic buyer for GBP 48 million.

B. Sell 100% of the company to a financial buyer for GBP 46 million.

C. Sell 100% of the company to existing employees in an MBO for GBP 50 million. Cook will receive GBP 4 million up front, accept a GBP 25 million promissory note from the employees (payable in equal installments over 10 years at 0% interest), and receive the balance in a note at 4% interest that will pay if the company meets defined future performance targets. Default on payments returns the company to Cook.

D. Gift the business to his grandchildren.

Recommend the best alternative for Cook and justify your response with two reasons specific to Cook's objectives. State one drawback to the recommended strategy. For each alternative not recommended, state one reason why it is not appropriate.

## Answer:

The strategic buyer (A.) is best:

- Cook receives the most cash to fund a diversified portfolio.
- He is completely out of the business and can concentrate on his health and retirement.

The primary disadvantage is that the sale will trigger a capital gains tax liability.

Here are the alternatives not selected:
(B.) The financial buyer from Cook's perspective is similar to the strategic buyer, but pays less—and there is the possibility he may have to keep an ownership percentage and continue managing the business.

(C.) The MBO price is higher but misleading. This alternative provides minimal upfront cash, and the future payments are not certain. In addition, Cook may end up having to go back to manage the company.

(D.) Gifting provides no money to Cook to meet his objectives.

The previous strategic and financial buyers withdraw their offers, and Cook negotiates to sell 85% of the company to a private equity fund for GBP 44 million. Cook will retire and no longer work for the company, or have any further authority. He will receive 90% of the GBP 44 million in cash and 10% in a promissory note payable in five annual installments (0% interest) starting in two years. The private equity firm's exit strategy is to sell the company to a strategic or financial buyer in three to five years. Cook's remaining shares will be included in that sale.

1. Assuming Cook's tax basis is zero and tax is due when cash is received at a 12% long-term capital gains tax rate, calculate Cook's first-year tax bill. Discuss any tax benefits of the transaction to Cook.
2. Also, discuss how the transaction changes Cook's allocation between primary and surplus capital. Discuss how the transaction changes his exposure to nonsystematic and total risk.

Answer:

1. Cook's cash received in the first year is 90% of GBP 44 million, which is GBP 39.6 million. With a zero cost basis, the full GBP 39.6 million is taxable at 12% for GBP 4.752 million in taxes due in the first year.

There is some deferral benefit because taxes owed on the remaining 10% of GBP 44 million will be due as the promissory note payments are received. The tax on the remaining 15% of the company owned by Cook will be due when the private equity firm makes a subsequent sale, anticipated in three to five years.

2. After tax, Cook has GBP 39.248 million (GBP 44 million less 4.752 million) more in primary capital that can be used to build a diversified portfolio to support his objectives.

Cook decreases his nonsystematic risk as he shifts funds from a concentrated private company to a diversified portfolio of marketable securities. His total risk, measured as portfolio standard deviation, should decline.

Completion Portfolio

Unlike the previous sales strategies designed to create liquidity and reduce risk, a completion portfolio will only reduce risk. This may be appropriate for individuals who have other sources of liquidity available.
Through quantitative optimization, a completion portfolio structures the owner's other portfolio assets to provide the greatest diversification benefit to complement (complete) the owner's concentrated position. The concentrated position is considered a distinct asset (e.g., private equity) for volatility and correlation. For example, if the concentrated position is in the pharmaceutical industry, the completion portfolio's assets will have low correlation to that industry, such that the resulting total portfolio better tracks the return of the chosen benchmark. The completion portfolio's assets are likely to be more conservative and more liquid than those of the concentrated position. Most positions in the completion portfolio are likely to be long, but it is possible to have some short positions.

A completion portfolio allows the owner to retain ownership of the concentrated position, but it may take time to implement and requires sufficient other assets and funds. This strategy provides a diversification benefit while deferring the gain from the concentrated position.

The completion portfolio may be invested to track a broad market index on a pretax basis and outperform the index on an after-tax basis. Quantitative techniques are used to determine how much rebalancing is required. Objectives include minimizing tracking error and maximizing after-tax return, but these objectives conflict because selling fewer shares at a time reduces taxes but increases tracking error. In general terms, after-tax returns are maximized by realizing more capital losses than capital gains, and using them to offset capital gains on the sale of the concentrated stock.

Used in the context of an extended diversification process, a completion portfolio can significantly reduce firm-specific risk, but it is not likely to have the same impact on systematic risk, given that the total portfolio seeks to retain market risk.

# Managing Concentrated Real Estate Positions

A single investment in a real estate asset may be large and constitute a significant portion of an investor's assets, bringing a high level of concentrated, property-specific risk. Real estate is generally illiquid and, if held for a long time, may have a significant unrealized taxable gain due to the low initial cost basis.

When selling or monetizing real property, the owner should consider its current value relative to its historical and expected value, taxes on any transaction, availability of credit, and interest rates. Given the nature of real estate, the transaction process may be complicated and continue for an extended time. If immediate liquidity is required and the real estate is considered a noncore business asset, the real estate alone can be sold as an alternative to selling private company shares.

Normally, selling an income-producing property results in immediate capital gains taxes, loss of control, and no ability to benefit from future appreciation. Mortgage financing (especially a nonrecourse loan) based on the strength of the accumulated equity in the property can be an attractive strategy to raise funds without suffering any of those three negative effects.

For example, if a rental property is worth $10 million and has $5 million of equity, it may be possible to obtain a mortgage for $4 million, which would be a loan-to-value

160
(LTV) ratio of 40%. Rental income presumably offsets some or all of the mortgage payments. If an interest-only loan can be obtained (with the full principal amount repaid at the end), more of the rental income can be used for diversification. Investing these funds in a diversified portfolio reduces concentration (nonsystematic) risk, but the increased leverage from the mortgage increases systematic risk.

The mortgaging strategy works best in stable and growing economic environments with low fixed rates, in that interest costs are lower and capital appreciation is higher. In contrast, a combination of an unstable economic environment and floating rates is undesirable because of higher interest rates and lower (or even negative) capital appreciation.

## Psychological Considerations

Emotional or cognitive biases may pose barriers to reducing a concentrated position and often focus on loyalty and family succession issues. Such biases may lead to irrational decision-making.

## Emotional Biases

Typical emotional biases include overconfidence, familiarity, and illusion of knowledge, leading the investor to overestimate the probability that the investment will produce favorable returns; status quo bias and a failure to consider making changes; naïve extrapolation of past results; endowment effect in expecting to be able to sell an asset for more than the investor would pay for it; and loyalty bias in retaining employer stock or feeling an obligation to retain an inherited position.

Managers may be able to help investors with emotional biases by asking questions such as:

- "What would you do if the position were cash rather than a single asset?"
- "When your grandmother left this stock to you, did she think it made sense for you to hold those shares for the long term based on their risk and return characteristics, or did she want to provide you with a source of funds to help you achieve your life goals?"

Reviewing past performance and current risk may help the investor gain perspective and think rationally.

## Cognitive Biases

Typical cognitive biases include conservatism in maintaining existing beliefs; confirmation in seeking support for what is already believed; illusion of control when the investor believes they can control what will happen to the investment; anchoring and adjustment in making decisions in reference to the current position held; and the availability heuristic in making decisions based on the ease of recalling information.

Relative to emotional biases, it is usually easier to correct errors stemming from cognitive biases with education because investors are more receptive when not hindered by their emotions.

161
162

# Family Governance

Family disputes regarding investment and philanthropic objectives may arise. A family governance system may reduce or eliminate such disputes.

Family enterprises often go through the following cycle: the first generation generates wealth, the second generation holds on to the wealth, and the third generation spends the wealth. The reduction in wealth is caused by one or more of wealth dilution over many family members, lack of interest in continuing the family business, and poor financial literacy and planning. A robust system of family governance can reduce negative outcomes.

Family governance can be thought of as the mechanism for families to maintain and increase their wealth over the long term. The process should take place early, and the family must determine its values, mission, and vision as a group.

## Passing the Business On to the Next Generation

Succession planning for a family business focuses on passing it to the next generation, or simply selling the business. The founder may do a direct transfer or use a trust to pass on the shares to the next generation. This can be done before death or at death. If done before death, the founder might elect to keep the voting shares to maintain control, and only transfer nonvoting shares. Another possibility is to transfer voting shares only to the active members of the business and transfer nonvoting shares to the nonactive family members.

## Disposing of the Business

Selling the business may be necessary if no one in the next generation is appropriate to assume it. Endowment bias is likely to be an issue that could overinflate the seller's expectations. The seller may need to be counseled as to what is realistic. The transition must address key issues such as taxation, liquidity, and philanthropy, as they will likely have a significant impact on the proceeds from the sale.

## Timing the Disposition

An estate freeze, establishing a limited partnership, gifting, or transferring to a trust may reduce taxes on future appreciation. The transfer value of a non-public family business should reflect a discount for its lack of liquidity and marketability, as well as a minority interest discount if the ownership percentage sold does not provide control. This value reduction could result in lower gift and estate taxes.

## After the Disposition

The sale proceeds will produce a sudden liquidity inflow. These funds can be used to finance the next business venture or be donated to charitable causes. The family should be able to agree on the next steps in terms of charitable goals and maximizing its social capital through philanthropy (e.g., family foundation). In some cases, the family may desire to be less broad with its philanthropy and specify how much to donate to which charities.
MODULE QUIZ W6.3

1. For which of the following individuals would reducing specific risk be most appropriate?

C. A spouse who inherited a real estate investment, and the position is 10% of the spouse's total assets.

A. The owner of a rental apartment property.
B. The owner of a warehouse who leases the building to a private company he also owns.
C. Young children who inherit a concentrated position in private shares of the company their father founded.

The present values of the investor's primary and secondary objectives are $6 million and $4 million, respectively. Based on the information provided, the investor's advisor should:

A. suggest monetizing the residence.
