# MODULE W6.4: PROFESSIONALS, EXECUTIVES, AND OTHERS

Video covering this content is available online.

LOS W6.d: Discuss and recommend appropriate private wealth management approaches that maximize the human capital, financial capital, and economic net worth of professionals, executives, and others.

# Public Company Executives

## Total Compensation

Public company executives often earn a base salary that is much higher than typical employees. The salary and annual performance-based bonus for a public company executive usually comprises a greater percentage of total compensation than equity
remuneration; the opposite is usually true for private company executives. Bonuses are often significant but are subject to wide variation from year to year, which adds significant uncertainty to that source of income.

In some instances, stock options and equity awards may be a substantial portion of, or most of, the total compensation for the public company executive. The rationale is to incentivize the executive to act in the best interests of the shareholders, which is more likely to occur if the executive also has a significant ownership stake in the company.

Deferred compensation plans may be offered that will pay out the earned income in later years (e.g., retirement) when the executive is in a lower tax bracket (assuming a progressive tax system). Pension income may also provide deferred compensation.

Total compensation may include noncash benefits such as use of an automobile and club memberships.

## Spending and Savings Patterns

When total compensation largely comprises bonuses and stock options, and when the year-to-year variability of those amounts is wide, spending by executives tends to increase or decrease with the size of the bonuses and equity awards. Those items often comprise much of executives' long-term savings. The lifestyle of an executive often features high discretionary spending (e.g., travel, secondary home), and such a lifestyle is potentially jeopardized during weaker economic periods.

## Managing Concentration Risk for Public Company Executives

In managing concentration risk, higher liquidity of the underlying stock is the main advantage public company executives have over private company executives. Greater liquidity may result in a higher selling price. Liquid derivatives on the underlying public equity provide more reliable pricing and make it easier and less costly to establish and adjust hedges.

However, public company executives may be subject to securities laws and regulations that classify them as "insiders" who may be presumed to have material nonpublic information, and impose restrictions, regulations, and reporting requirements on their share holdings. Contracts and employee mandates may impose additional restrictions, such as minimum holding periods or blackout periods when sales may not be made. For private company shares, the equity holder may be required contractually to first allow the company to buy back the shares being offered for sale, or to offer them for sale to other equity holders before offering them to outsiders.

Monetization strategies commonly require over-the-counter (OTC) derivative trades with a dealer. To offer such trades, dealers must be able to hedge the risks they assume. That may be impossible, for example, if the asset is an IPO or trades infrequently, as the dealer will not have a price history on which to base a hedge. For hedging strategies to be feasible, the shares must be readily available in the marketplace for short-selling so the dealer is able to hedge the risk. However, this is prohibited in some markets. Alternatively, a hedge using the investor's long position in shares is possible, but some

164
tax jurisdictions may treat the hedge as a constructive sale, creating a taxable event to the investor.

## OTC Derivatives

Compared to exchange-traded derivatives that are guaranteed by a clearinghouse, OTC derivatives bear much greater credit risk. Closing out positions with OTC derivatives is also more challenging, and attempting to terminate a contract early usually requires some form of payment to the counterparty. As an alternative, it is possible to perform an offsetting transaction with another participant, although fewer participants are available compared to exchange-traded derivatives.

Pricing of OTC derivatives is relatively less transparent, given that the transaction is executed with only one dealer. To ensure that the price is reasonable, it is advisable to obtain quotes from other dealers and use pricing models.

Hedging techniques often use OTC or exchange-traded derivatives. OTC instruments create counterparty credit risk but provide flexibility in setting the specific terms of the instrument. Exchange-traded instruments facilitate closing the transaction early by entering an offsetting transaction, provide price discovery because transaction prices are publicly reported, and provide more transparent fees and transaction costs (it is easier for a dealer to embed transaction costs in complex OTC derivatives).

A prepaid variable forward (PVF) provides cash without triggering a sale for tax purposes. A PVF is like a collar (hedge) and a loan combined into one. For example, an investor who owns 100,000 shares of a publicly traded company (Pubco), currently priced at $50 per share, can enter a PVF with a dealer that will pay the investor $45 per share now, which is equivalent to the investor borrowing $4.5 million. The loan will be repaid by delivering shares of Pubco at a future date. The PVF could specify delivery of all 100,000 shares if the price of Pubco is below $50 per share on the repayment date in two years (i.e., minimum sale price of $45), or a smaller number of shares if the price of Pubco is above $50. If all 100,000 shares are delivered, the implied annual interest rate on the loan is based on the difference between $50 and $45, or approximately 5% [= 0.5 × ($50 - $45) / $50].

A PVF is a good example of how OTC derivatives can be highly customized to fit the specific needs of an investor. In contrast, exchange-traded instruments are much more standardized. The minimum contract size for an OTC derivative is approximately USD 3 million, which is generally larger than exchange-traded derivatives.

## Hedging Concentrated Exposures in Publicly Traded Companies

Hedging strategies for public companies with liquid derivative contracts include:

1. Short sale against the box
2. Protective put
3. Zero-cost collar
4. Covered call

165
166

# Short Sale Against the Box

A short sale against the box involves the investor being long and short the stock at the same time. Because any stock price change will have identical offsetting positions from the long and short positions, this is a "riskless position" that is expected to earn the risk-free rate of return. Using the Pubco shares example, the investor borrows 100,000 shares of Pubco (currently valued at $50 per share), sells them short, and uses the proceeds to invest and meet portfolio objectives. The more effective the hedge, the higher the LTV ratio for the loan can be. Therefore, the borrowing amount is often as high as 99% of the stock price (in this example, loan proceeds of up to $4.95 million), and the loan expense can be reduced if the investments earn interest income.

Unfortunately, because of the riskless nature of a short sale against the box, many tax authorities consider it a constructive sale. To minimize taxes, this should be done on a stock with a higher cost basis, thereby resulting in a lower capital gain.

# Protective Put

A protective put can be thought of as paying a premium for portfolio insurance. In this example it would involve buying puts on the 100,000 shares of Pubco currently held. The exercise price of the put establishes the floor price of the shares while maintaining the upside. An at-the-money (ATM) or slightly out-of-the-money (OTM) put is frequently used.

A potential drawback of using a protective put is negative public perception. A key company owner needing to protect a personal position in the company stock could depress its price. Even if the real reason is for estate planning, the general public is unlikely to know that. Another potential drawback is rollover risk if the investor maintains the hedge for a longer time after the first options expire.

Put premiums can be significant, but the investor can limit the cost of the protection in several ways:

- Buy an OTM put. An ATM put with an exercise price of $50 might cost $4.57 per share and protect against any decline if the price falls below $50. An OTM put with an exercise price of $48 might cost only $2.98 per share. By using the OTM put, the investor is self-insuring the first $2 of stock price decline, but the put will cost less initially.
- Buy a put with a shorter time to expiration. The protection lasts for less time and will cost less.
- Use a pair of puts, buying a put with a higher exercise price of $X_{\mathrm{H}}$ and selling a put with a lower exercise price of $X_{\mathrm{L}}$. Buying a put with an exercise price of $48 protects against price declines below $48, but selling a put with an exercise price of $40 removes any protection below that price. The investor is protected between $X_{\mathrm{H}}$ and $X_{\mathrm{L}}$. Selling puts offsets some of the initial cost.
- Add exotic features not found in standard options. For example, a knockout put expires before its stated expiration date if the stock price rises above a specified level. This may reduce the protection, but the option will cost less.
167

# Zero-Cost Collar

A zero-cost collar (or zero-premium collar) is frequently used to provide downside protection at a lower cost. However, this strategy involves giving up some stock upside. The investor buys a put and sells a call, with different exercise prices such that the premiums are equal. For Pubco, the investor might be able to purchase a 48 put for $2.98 and sell a 53 call for $2.98. The investor has a no net expenditure and is protected if the stock price falls below $48, but the stock will be called if its price rises to $53.

From a tax perspective, given that the price exposure is relatively low (given the narrow range between the exercise prices of the call and the put), the constructive sale rules may apply in some jurisdictions and treat the collar as a taxable transaction.

General tax treatment of the option premium paid for the put is as follows:

- If the put expires worthless, it creates a capital loss at the short-term or long-term (depending on the jurisdiction) capital gains rate.
- If the put is exercised, this increases the cost basis of the stock, which ultimately reduces the capital gain realized on sale of the stock.
- If the put is closed before expiration (e.g., with an offsetting position), the option premium is deducted from the proceeds from closing. The net amount may be considered regular income or a short-term capital gain, which is potentially tax inefficient if the stock is taxed at the long-term capital gains rate.

General tax treatment of the option premium received for the call is as follows:

- If the call expires worthless, the premium is regular income or a short-term capital gain. This is potentially tax inefficient, especially if taxed as regular income.
- If the call is exercised, it decreases the cost basis of the stock, which ultimately increases the capital gain realized on sale of the stock.
- If the call is closed before expiration, the option premium is deducted from the cost of closing. The net amount may be considered regular income or a short-term capital gain, which is potentially tax inefficient if the stock is taxed at the long-term capital gains rate.
- Some jurisdictions treat dividends as regular income (instead of taxing them at a lower qualified dividend rate) when ITM or short-term (less than 30 days) call options are involved.

For collars, the premiums paid on the puts and received on the calls may be offset in some jurisdictions, resulting in a tax-neutral treatment. In other instances, there is a mismatch in character, whereby the put premium paid might only reduce the long-term capital gain (assume taxed at about 20%) but the call premium received is treated as ordinary income (assume taxed at about 35%). The mismatch results in tax inefficiency.

The reduction in upside potential is the key disadvantage of a collar. Methods to increase upside potential include the following:

- Sell a call with a higher exercise price. To maintain the initial zero cost, a put with a lower exercise price must be purchased. The upside potential is increased, but the downside protection is reduced.
- Sell a call with a higher exercise price, but without maintaining a zero cost. The investor does not change the exercise price of the put, but bears the difference between the put premiums of the regular and lower exercise prices. This effectively transforms a zero-cost collar to a debit collar.
- Use a pair of puts, buying a put with a higher exercise price of $X_{\mathrm{H}}$ and selling a put with a lower exercise price of $X_{\mathrm{L}}$. Doing so means the corresponding call premium received does not need to be as high, so a call with a higher exercise price can be sold.

## Covered Call

The investor with 100,000 shares of Pubco currently valued at $50 per share could sell call options with an exercise price of $53 for $2.98 if the investor is unwilling to sell below $53 per share. Because the stock will be called away if the price rises above $53, the investor is predetermining a liquidation value of $53. This may help to psychologically prepare the investor to gradually reduce the concentrated position. The $2.98 of premium income received can be viewed as either enhancing the income yield of the stock or protecting against the first $2.98 of stock price decline.

## EXAMPLE: Prepaid variable forward vs. zero-cost collar

An investor holds 50,000 shares valued at $100 per share. The investor simultaneously buys an OTC European style 6-month put with a strike price of 97 and sells a 1-year exchange-traded American style call with a strike price of 105. Both options cover the 50,000 shares, and the option premiums are equal. The relevant tax code specifies that option premiums received are taxed as ordinary income (t = 30%). Option premiums paid increase the tax basis of the underlying holding. In this case, the underlying position is a long-term holding, and any gain will be taxed at 10% when realized.

1. Comment on whether this structure is more like a prepaid variable forward (PVF) or a zero-cost collar (ZCC). Also, comment on how the risks in this transaction differ from a typical PVF or ZCC.
2. Are there any tax inefficiencies in the transaction?

## Answer:

1. This structure is most like a zero-cost collar because a put is purchased and a call is sold on the 50,000 shares owned. The two premiums are equal, so there is a zero initial cost. This is unlike a PVF because no funds are received up front, and there is no variation in the number of shares to be delivered in the future.

This is not a standard ZCC because the options have different expiration dates and features. The put protection only lasts for six months. The OTC European-style put option can only be exercised at expiration and will have limited liquidity until that point in time. The American-style call option can be exercised at any time up to expiration in 12 months and is marketable.

168
2. The tax treatment is not optimal. The premiums received for selling the calls will be taxed now at 30%. The cost of the put increases the tax basis of the underlying stock. Any resulting tax savings are realized only when the shares are sold to reduce gains that would have been taxed at 10%.

# Equity Monetization Strategies Without a Sale

Monetization strategies to avoid tax, deal with selling restrictions, maintain control, or provide liquidity include the following:

1. Personal line of credit, secured with the underlying stock
2. Total return swap
3. Equity forward
4. Synthetic equity forward
5. Tax-free exchange

Monetization generally involves receiving cash for a position without triggering a tax event. The following two-step process applies to the second, third, and fourth strategies:

Step 1: Hedge a large part of the risk in the position. This is often complicated by tax regulations. If a perfect hedge removes all risk, it may be taxed as a sale. The hedge must be structured to eliminate as much risk as possible without the tax liability coming due.

Step 2: Borrow using the hedged position as collateral. As stated before, the more effective the hedge, the higher the LTV ratio.

# Personal Line of Credit

A line of credit from a third-party lender, with the shares pledged as security, avoids a sale of shares and a taxable event and allows the owner to maintain control and access liquidity. Private company shares are less likely to be accepted as security. Because the shares are not hedged, and therefore remain risky, the LTV ratio will be lower, possibly around 50%. Interest paid is often a tax-deductible business expense.

The company can offer the lender a put option, allowing the lender to transfer the loan to the company for cash. This increases the lender's assurance of repayment in exchange for more favorable loan terms to the owner. Alternatively, the loan might set up as a nonrecourse loan with the security offered initially having a higher value than the loan amount. Should the security value fall below the loan balance outstanding at maturity, the borrower may choose not to repay the loan balance and simply forfeit the security.

# Total Return Swap

The investor enters a swap to pay the total return on the $5 million of Pubco and receives the market reference rate (MRR) from the counterparty, which is usually a bank. The investor is fully hedged. If the return of Pubco exceeds the MRR (gain on underlying), the investor pays the difference (loss on swap), which eliminates any gains

169
on Pubco. If the return of Pubco is less than the MRR (loss on underlying), the investor receives the difference (gain on swap), thereby locking in the MRR.

Compared to a short sale against the box, the total return swap probably has a lower return, resulting from a higher dealer spread. Because the Pubco shares are completely hedged, a high LTV ratio is possible.

## Equity Forward

The investor enters a forward contract to sell the 100,000 shares of Pubco to the counterparty on a future date. The investor has a known sale price and does not share in any upside or downside price movement of Pubco from that contract price. Given the risk-free (hedged) nature of the total position, the risk-free rate is implied in setting the forward price, and a high LTV ratio is possible.

## Synthetic Equity Forward

A synthetic equity forward involves buying a put and selling a call on the same stock to create an identical payoff to a short forward. The investor would buy Pubco puts and sell Pubco calls, both with an exercise price of $50 and identical expiration dates on 100,000 Pubco shares. If the stock price rises above $50 it will be called, and if it falls below $50 it can be put. A hedged (and therefore, risk-free) ending value of $50 is established. Again, given the risk-free and fully hedged position, a high LTV ratio for monetization is possible. Additionally, the loan cost will be reduced by income earned from the diversified portfolio.

## Tax-Free Exchange

Exchange funds are possible in some tax jurisdictions. Consider 10 investors, each of whom has a concentrated position in a single stock with a low cost basis. Each investor's position is in a different stock. The investors contribute their holdings to a newly formed exchange fund. Each investor then owns a pro rata share of the new fund. If the investor contributes Pubco shares worth $5 million (with a low cost basis of $0.5 million) and the total value of the new fund is $100 million, the investor owns 5% of the new fund at a cost basis of $0.5 million. The investor now participates in a diversified portfolio, and is eventually issued shares of that portfolio after a minimum holding period. The exchange fund strategy defers any tax event until shares of the fund are sold.

Compared to a regular plan of selling the concentrated stock and then reinvesting in a diversified portfolio, an exchange fund has a distinct financial advantage through tax deferral, although some of that advantage is reduced by the management fees paid to establish and manage the fund.

## Choosing the Best Hedging Strategy

The taxation of shares is a significant driver in selecting the best strategy. The goal is to select the strategy that will not trigger an existing tax liability. Critical questions include what happens when a derivative is executed or closed, how gains or losses are taxed, what expenses are involved, and the effect on the concentrated position. For example, remuneration in the form of restricted company shares or employee stock

170
options is usually taxed as regular employment income (higher tax rate) and not as capital gains (lower tax rate).

## Athletes and Actors

Professional athletes often have a compressed period of high earnings (e.g., average of 8 to 11 years), resulting in a long retirement period (e.g., 50 years). Ensuring sufficient funds to meet living needs for such a long period requires careful financial management.

In contrast, actors usually have a "start-up" stage with low earnings, and if they are fortunate enough, they will reach the "success" stage that consists of much higher earnings. In general, female actors have a shorter start-up stage than male actors, but male actors often have a longer success stage than female actors.

Professional athletes should set aside 50% to 75% of annual earnings for retirement to ensure a stable financial position for the extended retirement period. Doing so will provide greater financial flexibility to deal with unforeseen events such as serious injuries or nonrenewed contracts that could curtail earning power indefinitely. Frequently, high incomes result in high spending, and as a result, few athletes end up saving a sufficient amount of their earnings.

Monte Carlo simulations or retirement spending models may be used to determine how much needs to be saved in total to maintain a desired lifestyle during retirement. Alternatively, they may be able to determine the maximum annual amounts that may be spent during the income-earning years to allow for a desired lifestyle during retirement. Compared to regular employees with more stable salaries over a longer period, athletes and actors with more erratic salaries over a shorter period will experience a wider distribution of financial outcomes when performing Monte Carlo simulations.

Unfortunately, many athletes and actors are financially uninformed, neither understanding finance nor appreciating the importance of saving and setting aside funds to pay taxes. As a result, they often make unwise investments, spend and borrow excessively, fail to stick to a reasonable budget, and suffer financial losses due to unscrupulous friends and family members.

## MODULE QUIZ W6.4

1. A prepaid variable forward is economically most similar to a:
A. collar.
B. covered call.
C. protective put.

2. An investor seeking to hedge a stock position executes appropriate at-the-money call and put options. Premiums paid are $100,000 and premiums received are $70,000. Option premiums received are taxed as income at a 40% tax rate, while option premiums paid increase the cost basis of the underlying. Realized capital gains are taxed at 10%. What is the effect on the investor's current period tax payable, based solely on the information provided and assuming the asset is not sold in the current period?

A. $12,000.
B. $28,000.
C. $40,000.

3. Patricia and Steve Peters, both age 54, meet with their advisor to discuss financial goals in retirement. Steve is the president and sole owner of a private company he founded 15 years ago. They provide the following information:
- Steve plans to retire in one year. He wants to make a clean break from the business with no further responsibility. Instead, he will focus on charity work and travel with Patricia.
- Within the next several years, the Peters want to gift 10% of their wealth to a charity for disadvantaged children.
- Steve estimates that his company is worth at least $45 million and has no doubt it will grow in the future, just as it has in the past. His cost basis of the company shares is $4 million, and the relevant capital gains tax rate is 20%. The company does not pay dividends.
- Their only other investment asset is a $5 million diversified stock portfolio.
- Their primary goal is to maintain their standard of living for the remaining lives, and their advisor estimates that it will require $25 million of assets today.

A. Discuss two potential problems in meeting their goals relating to the business owned by Steve.

B. The Peters ask their advisor to consider several ways to monetize the business. Specifically, they are interested in (1) an employee buyout, 70% funded by Steve, with the company's middle management team; (2) a leveraged recapitalization with a private equity firm to purchase 60% of Steve's equity; or (3) a sale to a financial buyer. Assuming each transaction has the same value for the entire company, identify the strategy that is most suitable, given their objectives. Justify your response.

C. The Peters ask the advisor to explain a short sale against the box and an exchange fund. Explain each and determine which is most suitable for the Peters.

## KEY CONCEPTS

### LOS W6.a

Obtaining citizenship in a country may occur by birth, extended descent, naturalization, marriage, or investment.

Potential advantages of dual citizenship include asset diversification, global mobility, tax optimization, education and health care, and a political safety net. Potential disadvantages of dual citizenship include legal and administrative burden, double taxation, cultural challenges, and political and economic risks.

Tax domicile is the country of a person's residency for tax purposes. Countries use many different tests to determine residency. One example includes subjective standards
such as personal or economic ties to the country. Another example includes objective measures such as the number of days residing within a country's borders.

Residence jurisdiction is the most prevalent type of jurisdiction whereby a country taxes the income of its residents, whether generated inside or outside of the country. Under source jurisdiction, a country levies taxes on all income generated within its borders, whether by citizens or noncitizens.

In a residence-residence conflict, two countries claim residence for the same individual. In a source-source conflict, two countries claim authority over the same income. In a residence-source conflict, an individual is subject to residence jurisdiction and receives income on assets in a foreign country with source jurisdiction.

Tax treaties may partially or fully resolve the double taxation of residence-source conflicts. The income is taxed by the source country and then taxed by the residence country differently using one of the following methods:

- Exemption method. This means not taxed by the residence country.
- Credit method. The tax owed to the residence country is computed, and a credit for taxes paid to the source country is applied:
- If more is owed to the residence country, the difference is paid.
- If less is owed to the residence country, the bill is zero.
- Deduction method. Taxes owed to the source country reduce the taxable income in (and thereby, partially reduce the tax owed to) the residence country.

The OECD Model Treaty is the basis of all DTTs and recommends either the credit or the exemption methods for resolving double taxation between the source country and the residence country.

Ceasing tax residency in one country often involves setting up permanent residency in and transferring day-to-day functions to a new country.

To avoid residence taxation, individuals may renounce their citizenship and move to a less strict jurisdiction. In response, some residence jurisdictions impose an exit tax.

Investment visas (e.g., investor visas, golden visas) are an easier way to stay in a country and be considered a legal resident compared to full citizenship.

## LOS W6.b

Spouses can have marital property rights according to the type of marriage. Under a community property regime, each spouse is entitled to one-half of the estate earned during the marriage. Under a separate property regime, each spouse owns and controls their own property, separate from the other spouse. Gifts and inheritances received before or after the marriage may be held separate from marital assets.

The division of financial assets in the event of a marriage breakdown can be addressed through prenuptial agreements or postnuptial agreements. Assets to be distributed on marriage dissolution include the marital residence, financial assets, pensions and retirement accounts, and inheritances.

173
174
Cohabitation involves two individuals in a committed relationship who live together but are not married. A cohabitation agreement can be used to clarify the relationship and provide a basis for the allocation of assets and liabilities upon dissolution of the relationship or death of either individual.

The key issue with same-sex couples is whether the jurisdiction legally recognizes these unions. If not, then significant hurdles may need to be overcome, given the greater complexity involved in wealth planning.

A client with excess capital can gift the capital now or bequest it at death. The three potential relative value (RV) ratios to compare a gift to a bequest are as follows:

RV of a tax-free gift, $T_{\mathrm{g}} = 0$:


\mathrm{RV}_{\text{tax-free gift}} = \frac{\left[1 + \tau_{\mathrm{g}} (1 - t_{\mathrm{ig}}) \right]^{\mathrm{a}}}{\left[1 + \tau_{\mathrm{e}} (1 - t_{\mathrm{ie}}) \right]^{\mathrm{a}} (1 - T_{\mathrm{e}})}


RV of a taxable gift, $T_{\mathrm{g}}$, paid by receiver:


\mathrm{RV}_{\text{taxable gift}} = \frac{\left[1 + \tau_{\mathrm{g}} (1 - t_{\mathrm{ig}}) \right]^{\mathrm{a}} (1 - T_{\mathrm{g}})}{\left[1 + \tau_{\mathrm{e}} (1 - t_{\mathrm{ie}}) \right]^{\mathrm{a}} (1 - T_{\mathrm{e}})}


RV of a taxable gift, $T_{\mathrm{g}}$, paid by giver:


\mathrm{RV}_{\text{taxable gift}} = \frac{\left[1 + \tau_{\mathrm{g}} (1 - t_{\mathrm{ig}}) \right]^{\mathrm{a}} (1 - T_{\mathrm{g}} + T_{\mathrm{g}} T_{\mathrm{e}})}{\left[1 + \tau_{\mathrm{e}} (1 - t_{\mathrm{ie}}) \right]^{\mathrm{a}} (1 - T_{\mathrm{e}})}


## LOS W6.c

Concentration risk in specific assets is a key risk faced by many successful entrepreneurs. Those assets are usually one or more of the following: (1) private company shares, (2) publicly traded shares, and (3) real estate.

A concentrated position often has a low tax basis that would give rise to a significant capital gain upon disposition. Additionally, the concentrated position may reflect the businessowner's cumulative success over the years. In either case, the current position is inefficient and lacks sufficient diversification.

A goal-based decision process modifies traditional mean-variance analysis to accommodate the insights of behavioral finance theory. The portfolio is divided into risk buckets, with each designed to meet progressive levels of client goals. The risk buckets and sequence of priority are as follows: (1) a personal risk bucket to protect the client from poverty or a drastic decline in lifestyle, (2) a market risk bucket to maintain the client's existing standard of living, and (3) an aspirational risk bucket with risky assets that could substantially improve the client's standard of living.

Selling an entire concentrated position often means incurring a significant tax liability, but then the after-tax funds may be used to invest in a more diversified portfolio. Alternatively, a gradual disposition of a concentrated position spreads the tax liability out over multiple years. A staged diversification strategy allows the owner to maintain some control and gradually give up the control over time, which may be more
psychologically and financially appealing than selling the entire position in one transaction.

A sale of a concentrated position may occur in one or more of the following ways:

- Selling to an external party (strategic or financial buyer):
- Strategic buyers take a buy-and-hold perspective and generally offer the highest price.
- A financial buyer is often a private equity fund planning to restructure, add value to, and resell the business (typically in a three-to-five-year period)

- Selling to an insider:
- With an management buyout, the owner sells the position to existing key employees of a company who are presumed to know the business well. Drawbacks are a discounted price and the need for the seller to finance much of the purchase.
- An employee stock ownership plan allows for phased exit and extends control for a few more years, but adds administrative costs.

- There is recapitalization, whereby the owner retains an equity stake and continues to manage the business as part of a phased exit strategy.
- There is the divestiture of redundant assets that are not used in regular business operations.
- There is the IPO, whereby the owner continues to retain a significant ownership stake and manage the business, so this is not an immediate exit strategy.

A completion portfolio structures other portfolio assets for the greatest diversification benefit to complement a retained concentrated position.

Emotional and cognitive biases may pose barriers to reducing a concentrated position. Typical emotional biases include overconfidence, familiarity, illusion of knowledge, status quo bias, naïve extrapolation of past results, endowment effect, and loyalty bias. Typical cognitive biases include conservatism, confirmation, illusion of control, anchoring and adjustment, and the availability heuristic.

Family enterprises often go through the following cycle: the first generation generates wealth, the next generation holds on to the wealth, and the last generation spends the wealth. The reduction in wealth is caused by wealth dilution over many family members, no interest in continuing the family business, and poor financial literacy and planning. Robust family governance may reduce negative outcomes.

## LOS W6.d

Three broad techniques can be used to manage concentrated positions:

1. Sell the asset. This will trigger a tax liability and loss of control.
2. Monetize the asset. Borrow against its value and use the loan proceeds to accomplish client objectives.
3. Hedge the asset value. This is often done using derivatives to limit downside risk.

Monetization strategies commonly require OTC derivative trades with a dealer to hedge the security's risk and increase the LTV ratio. Compared to exchange-traded

175
derivatives (which are guaranteed by a clearinghouse), OTC derivatives bear much greater credit risk.

A PVF provides cash without triggering a sale for tax purposes. In that regard, it is like a collar (hedge) and a loan combined into one. A PVF is a good example to illustrate how OTC derivatives can be highly customized to fit the specific needs of an investor. In contrast, exchange-traded instruments are much more standardized.

Hedging strategies for public companies with liquid derivative contracts include the following:

- A short sale against the box involves the investor being long and short the stock at the same time; it is a "riskless position" that is expected to earn the risk-free rate of return because any stock price change will have identical offsetting positions from the long and short positions.
- A protective put involves purchasing puts on shares currently held; it can also be thought of as paying a premium for portfolio insurance. The strike price of the put establishes the floor price of the shares while maintaining the upside. ATM or OTM puts are frequently used. To lower put premium costs, consider strategies such as buying an OTM put, buying a put with a shorter time to expiration, or engaging in a put spread (subsidize cost by selling put).
- A zero-cost (zero-premium) collar is frequently used to provide downside protection at a lower cost. However, this strategy involves giving up some stock upside. Besides owning the underlying stock, a put is purchased, and a call is sold with different strike prices selected so that the premiums are equal.
- A covered call position could be established by selling call options with a strike price higher than the current price of the stock. Because the stock will be called away if the price rises above the strike price, the investor is predetermining a liquidation value that is the strike price. In that sense, a covered call could be used to psychologically prepare the investor to gradually reduce the concentrated position.

Monetization strategies to avoid tax, deal with selling restrictions, maintain control, or provide liquidity include the following:

- A personal line of credit from a third-party lender avoids a sale of shares and a taxable event, but it allows the owner to maintain control and access liquidity. The shares would be pledged as security.
- A total return swap involves the investor entering a swap to pay the total return on the stock and receives the market reference rate (MRR) from the counterparty. If the return on the stock exceeds the MRR, the investor pays the difference, which eliminates any gains on the stock. If the return on the stock is less than the MRR, the investor receives the difference, thereby locking in the MRR.
- An equity forward involves the investor entering a forward contract to sell the shares to the counterparty on a future date. The investor has a known sale price and does not share in any upside or downside price movement of the shares from that contract price.
- A synthetic equity forward involves buying a put and selling a call on the same stock with an identical payoff to a short forward. A short forward would directly offset the

176
long position of shares.

- An exchange fund involves numerous investors, each of whom has a concentrated position in a single stock with a low cost basis. Each investor's position is in a different stock. The investors contribute their holdings into a newly formed exchange fund, and each owns a pro rata share of the new fund. The exchange fund strategy defers any tax event until shares of the fund are sold.

Professional athletes should aim for 50% to 75% of annual earnings to be set aside for retirement to ensure a stable financial position for the extended retirement period. Monte Carlo simulations or retirement spending models may be used to determine how much needs to be saved in total to maintain a desired lifestyle during retirement.

## Module Quiz W6.2

1. A The giver can earn a higher after-tax return of 8.5% versus 6% for the receiver [8%(1 - 0.25) = 6%]. That favors waiting to make a bequest at death if the investment is held in the estate long enough and compounding at the higher 8.5%. But gifting now avoids any estate tax that could decrease the return to less than 6%, making gifting now more beneficial assuming the gift tax is avoided. The relevant equation is as follows:


\mathrm{RV}_{\text{tax-free gift}} = \frac{[1 + \tau_{\text{g}}(1 - t_{\text{ig}})]^{\alpha}}{[1 + \tau_{\text{s}}(1 - t_{\text{ia}})]^{\alpha}(1 - T_{\text{s}})}


Until the time period (n) is known, the optimal solution of gifting now versus making a bequest is unknown.

B The giver can earn a higher after-tax return, and waiting to make a bequest will be subject to a lower transfer tax of 30%. Thus, it is better to wait and make a bequest.

C Gifting now gives up control, and the assets cannot be recovered if the giver changes their mind or needs the funds. That may create a reason to wait and make a bequest.

(LOS W6.b)

## Module Quiz W6.4

1. A A prepaid variable forward is equivalent to hedging the risky asset with a collar plus borrowing the value of the hedged position. The other two choices are less appropriate comparisons. A protective put consists of owning the asset and buying a put. A covered call consists of owing the asset and selling calls. (LOS W6.d)

2. B The $70,000 received this period is taxed at 40% for a tax due of $28,000. The $100,000 paid increases the cost basis of the underlying asset. When the asset is eventually sold, that reduces any gain taxed at 10% by $100,000, a potential tax savings of $10,000. This is describing a mismatch in tax character. (LOS W6.d)

3. A Any two of the following are sufficient to answer this question:

- The portfolio lacks diversification with $45 million (90%) in Steve's business, and his business is private, which would have a substantial idiosyncratic risk.

Based on a business value of $45 million, after taxes, they should net $36.8 million, as tax is calculated as ($45 million - $4 million) × 20% = $8.2 million. Including their $5 million stock portfolio, the total is $41.8 million. Even

if the business value is overestimated, there is a considerable cushion of $16.8 million.

The employee buyout is partially funded by Steve and leaves him at risk of having to go back and manage the company. The private equity firm would most likely expect Steve to retain a stake and role in the business.

C A short sale against the box is a hedging technique that provides a loan equal to most of the value of the company and requires borrowing and shorting the company stock. This strategy is not suitable for Steve's company because his company is private; it is more suited to a company that trades publicly.

An exchange fund would involve Steve joining with other individuals who have concentrated positions in other companies. With each investor contributing their shares to the fund, all investors own a portion of the resulting diversified portfolio. No tax liabilities are realized on the initial contribution of shares.

Neither option makes sense for the Peters because both options leave Steve responsible for managing the business. Both options are appropriate for public company shares.

(LOS W6.d)
