READING W7

TRANSFERRING THE WEALTH

READING

EXAM FOCUS

This reading focuses on the ways individuals transfer their wealth through gifting and at death. Estate planning accounts for how an individual wishes to transfer wealth and who they intend to benefit. Transfer taxes play a critical role in the overall strategy of wealth transfer. Candidates must understand the different strategies that individuals can use to minimize transfer taxes. For gifts, understand the different parties, mechanisms for gifting, and tax implications for the donor and donee. For transfers upon death, distinguish between community and separate property rules, what is allowable in common law versus civil law jurisdictions, and how marital deductions and joint property structures can minimize legal complications. Candidates must be familiar with the different types of trusts and what advantages and disadvantages each form provides. Finally, be prepared to differentiate among the different mechanisms available for gifting to charity.

MODULE W7.1: TRANSFERRING WEALTH VIA GIFTS

Video covering this content is available online.

LOS W7.a: Discuss and recommend appropriate wealth management planning approaches for transferring wealth during the lifetime of the giver through gifts.

One of the primary ways in which wealth is transferred is through gifting. A gift is made between a donor (the individual making the gift) and a donee (the individual receiving the gift). A lifetime gift is also known as an inter vivos transfer (or lifetime gratuitous transfer) and is often made with donative intent, which means having no expectation of anything in return.

Gifts may be assessed a transfer tax, which is a tax on any form of wealth transfer (whether through a gift or as part of a will covering how assets are bequeathed upon death). The tax may be applied to the donor or the donee. A gift tax is generally applied to the donor, as a means of ensuring that transfer taxes are not avoided upon death. A gift tax can be applied using either a progressive rate schedule or a flat tax rate.

180
Although gifting during a donor's lifetime provides certain tax benefits, donors must be careful not to gift so much that their portfolio is fully depleted while they are still alive. Factors such as asset growth, inflation, spending rates, and taxes must be accounted for to ensure that funds are not fully depleted. Interests across generations as well as determining where and how wealth will be transferred also play important roles.

## Transfer Taxes

In terms of transfer taxes, the distinction between an **estate tax** and an **inheritance tax** is that the former is paid by the transferor's estate, while the latter is paid by the recipient. Gifts can serve to decrease the impact of both of these taxes by decreasing the value of any future transfers of wealth. Transfer taxes may be avoided if a gift is less than an allowance (either a periodic or lifetime allowance), but in some countries, gifts may be added back to the estate when calculating these taxes. Alternatively, some countries apply exclusions based on the age of the donor. If a donor implements a gifting program early enough, the tax savings can be substantial. Even though asset appreciation is still taxed regardless of whether it is gifted or left in the estate, the estate or inheritance tax is typically much higher (as a rate) than the capital gains or dividend tax rates.

## EXAMPLE: The impact of gifting (capital gains taxed annually)

A donor living in the United States who wishes to reduce the impact of transfer taxes on her estate implements a plan to gift $17,000 (equal to the amount of the annual exclusion) to her son every year for the next 20 years, beginning today. The annual nominal return is 5%, the expected annual inflation rate is 2%, and the capital gains tax rate applied each year (at year-end) is 15%. Determine the real, after-tax value of the gifts at the end of the 20-year horizon.

### Answer:

The real after-tax annual investment return is approximately [5.0% × (1 - 0.15)] - 2.0% = 2.25%. BGN mode for an annuity due:


N = 20; I/Y = 2.25; PV = 0; PMT = -17,000; CPT FV = 433,024


## EXAMPLE: The impact of gifting (capital gains taxed at end of horizon)

Using the same facts as the previous example, **determine** the real, after-tax value of the gifts at the end of the 20-year horizon, assuming capital gains taxes are deferred until the end of the horizon.

### Answer:

Because taxes are not due each year, the real annual return is approximately 5% - 2% = 3%.

BGN mode for an annuity due:


N = 20; I/Y = 3; PV = 0; PMT = -17,000; CPT FV = 470,500

The taxable capital gain is $470,500 – 20($17,000) = $130,500. After taxes (15%), the real after-tax capital gain is $130,500(1 – 0.15) = $110,925. The real after-tax value of the gifts after 20 years is equal to 20($17,000) + $110,925 = $450,925.

## Valuation Discounts

When cash or marketable securities are gifted, the tax is assessed on the fair market value of the asset transferred. One way to potentially reduce transfer taxes is to gift assets that are eligible for valuation discounts.

An asset such as shares of a privately held business will likely require estimates and assumptions to determine fair market value. Because these shares tend to lack liquidity, an illiquidity discount (typically 20%–25%) may be applied. A higher discount may be needed for smaller companies with lower profit margins.

An additional discount (typically 25%–40%) may be needed if the shares represent a minority interest in the business, because a position with a lack of control is less marketable than a controlling interest.

If discounts are taken for both illiquidity and lack of control, they are applied multiplicatively, not additively, as in the following example.

### EXAMPLE: Illiquidity and lack of control discounts

James is considering a gift (before discounts) of $2 million in privately held shares in which he has a minority interest. Determine the fair market value of the gift if a 20% illiquidity discount and 35% lack of control discount are applied.

#### Answer:

The combined discount is equal to [(1 – 0.20) × (1 – 0.35)] – 1 = 0.48.

The combined 48% discount is less than the sum of 20% and 35%.

The fair market value is $2,000,000 × (1 – 0.48) = $1,040,000.

To take advantage of discounts for lack of control and illiquidity, donors may put their assets in a family limited partnership (FLP). Individuals may then be gifted minority interests in the FLP (which is an illiquid asset), which could allow for valuation discounts.

Another benefit could come from pooling family member assets to reach minimum investment levels for certain types of higher-risk investments like venture capital and hedge funds. Gains and losses on these investments could then be distributed pro rata.

## Trusts

A trust stems from common law and is an arrangement often used in estate planning. The settlor (or grantor) provides the assets (corpus or principal) used to create the trust. A trustee has the fiduciary responsibility of managing the assets for the eventual benefit of beneficiaries (who are the beneficial, but not legal, owners of the assets in

182
the trust). The trust agreement is the document that specifies the principals, relationships, and distributions to be made to the beneficiaries.

A revocable trust allows the settlor to rescind the trust and regain title to the assets. As the owner, the settlor is responsible for reporting on investment returns and making tax payments. Creditors can come after trust assets in a revocable structure.

In an irrevocable trust, the settlor does not have the ability to revoke the trust. Reporting and tax payments are often the responsibility of the trustee (who has title to ownership), and assets in the trust have greater protection from creditors.

A fixed trust has specific beneficiary distributions (in terms of amounts and times) described in the trust document. Beneficiaries have legal rights to assets and income, which means a beneficiary's creditors can reach those assets.

A discretionary trust gives the trustee the flexibility to determine the amount and timing of distributions based on the best interests of the beneficiaries. In this structure, beneficiaries do not have legal rights to assets or income; therefore, their creditors cannot reach those assets.

Reasons for using a trust include the following:

- Asset protection. It is more difficult for creditors to reach assets held in a trust. Also, trusts can be used to avoid probate, a lengthy legal process used to verify the will of the deceased. A trust can also be used to reduce the negative impact on a family business from community property laws, under which property obtained during marriage is owned jointly by both spouses and equally divided upon divorce or death.
- Control. Assets in a trust can be made available to beneficiaries without providing them with full control.
- Taxes. Assets and income held in a trust or provided to beneficiaries may be taxed at lower rates than those applicable to the wealthy individual who contributed assets to the trust.

Trusts can be set to last for a period of years or forever (perpetuity). Common law had established a rule against perpetuities that prohibited trusts from extending beyond a specific period. This rule, which capped the length of a trust at 21 years beyond the death of the last specified individual alive at initiation of the trust, has been relaxed in many jurisdictions. A dynasty trust is one that is set to last much longer, or into perpetuity.

Some wealthy individuals may try to reduce transfer taxes by gifting assets directly (or into a trust) for the benefit of grandchildren (considered the "third generation") or beyond. Generation skipping is designed to gift assets to the third generation and beyond while minimizing the taxation inherent in the transfer to each generation.

## EXAMPLE: Generation skipping—benefits

Blake and Jennifer Samuels have an estate currently worth $65 million. They wish to gift a total of $30 million to their children and grandchildren during their lifetimes.

183
Their plan is to initially transfer $10 million to their children in 5 years that will subsequently be transferred to their grandchildren 20 years afterward.

1. Assuming a 4.5% real rate of return and a 40% transfer tax rate, what is the real after-tax value of the transfer from the Samuels' children to their grandchildren?
2. Using the same assumptions, but assuming that the Samuels gift the $10 million directly to their grandchildren, what is the real after-tax value?

Answer:

1. The real after-tax value is equal to:

\$10,000,000 \times \left[(1.045)^5(1 - 0.40)(1.045)^{20}(1 - 0.40)\right] = \$10,819,561


2. The real after-tax value is equal to:

\$10,000,000 \times \left[(1.045)^25(1 - 0.40)\right] = \$18,032,607


The value of transferring capital and skipping generations is 1 / (1 - T1), with T1 serving as the tax rate on the transfer from the first generation (the Samuels) to the second generation (their children). A generation-skipping transfer tax has been implemented by the United States to prevent the strategy of reducing transfer taxes by skipping generations. Tax exemptions may still help families get around this tax.

Another important distinction is between inter vivos trusts and testamentary trusts. An inter vivos trust is an irrevocable trust created and funded by the grantor when he or she is alive. A testamentary trust is created from the estate plan of someone who is deceased.

Tax rules on trusts may be more stringent than on individuals because trusts may be seen as ways to minimize or avoid transfer taxes. An intentionally defective grantor trust is an irrevocable trust (in the United States) that removes the gifted assets from the grantor's estate, but it still requires the grantor to pay income taxes on the assets. The grantor's taxable estate is, therefore, reduced by the original contribution, investment returns, and taxes paid, while allowing the assets themselves to grow without a tax impact.

An offshore trust is created in a jurisdiction outside an individual's home jurisdiction to gain tax benefits, greater investment opportunities, and more asset protection. To set up an offshore trust, an individual (or representatives) must select the jurisdiction, involve tax and legal experts, draft the agreement, appoint trustees, transfer the assets, and manage the trust on an ongoing basis.

The distinction between common and civil law comes into play with trusts. Common law (known as case law) is based on legal consistency, judicial decisions, adaptability, and precedent. Civil law is based on statutes and legal codes, providing definitive guidelines on obligations and rights. As a general rule, trusts are not recognized by civil law jurisdictions. However, the Hague Trust Convention has been adopted by many countries and recognizes the validity of trusts created, regardless of individual country rules.

184
Trusts may be created to avoid forced inheritance rules, laws that otherwise require children or others to receive some of the decedent's assets. Anstalts (company/foundation hybrids) and stiftungs (like a noncommercial, personal institution) are two civil law hybrid structures used as alternatives to trusts in civil law jurisdictions.

The prudent investor rule dictates how trustees must invest trust assets. When selecting trust assets, trustees must consider the terms, purposes, and distribution requirements, along with many other issues such as the economy, tax and inflation impacts, the expected total return, beneficiary resources, liquidity needs, and the role of each investment within the portfolio.

## Gift and Estate Planning Objectives

In thinking about what an individual wishes to accomplish with gifting and their estate overall, there are many objectives (and appropriate investment strategies) to consider:

- Control over assets. Fixed or discretionary trusts can be used to ensure distributions are in line with the grantor's wishes.
- Income and liquidity. Investments such as fixed income, public equities, REITs, private equity, or even private debt could provide long-term ongoing income.
- Charitable goals. Gifts outright to charity or through a private foundation (which may give the donor and his or her family more involvement and decision-making authority) may be beneficial.
- Wealth transfer with tax awareness. Gifts to spouses or to relatives may provide tax advantages, as can generation-skipping gifts. Trusts may also be beneficial.
- Asset protection. Irrevocable, discretionary trusts can protect assets from creditors of both the grantor and beneficiaries.
- Business succession. Ownership/management of a business could stay within the family, external management could be used, or the business could be partially or fully sold to an outside party or parties.
- Preservation of family wealth. A family governance system can help to align the goals of all family members and make the transfer process as smooth and conflict free as possible.

## MODULE QUIZ W7.1

1. Which of the following statements is most accurate regarding an intentionally defective grantor trust structure?

A. Income taxes are avoided using this structure.
B. The client's estate will be reduced by the assets gifted to this trust.
C. The trust will be revocable, which will give the client flexibility to change the terms.

2. An investor wishes to gift funds in privately held shares. Her advisor suggests an illiquidity discount of 15% and a lack of control discount of 20%. The fair market value of the gift will be reduced by:

A. 5%.
B. 32%.

185
C. 35%.

Video covering this content is available online.

# MODULE W7.2: TRANSFERRING WEALTH: BEQUESTS AND INHERITANCES

## LOS W7.b: Discuss and recommend appropriate wealth management planning approaches for transferring wealth at death through bequests and inheritance.

With proper planning, an individual’s assets should transfer upon death as intended. A will can be used to give or leave property to a specific person or persons through a bequest. The bequest provides the directive for what will be done with the assets of the testator (the will creator) when they die. Having no valid will is called intestacy, in which case the courts decide how the assets will be disposed. What a beneficiary receives upon death is called an inheritance.

The formal structure for how an individual’s wealth will be transferred upon death is called a testamentary plan. Differences between common law and civil law jurisdictions affect the structure of a testamentary plan. While civil law dominates globally, countries such as the United States and United Kingdom are common law jurisdictions that give the testator the freedom to dispose of their assets as they wish through a will. Civil law jurisdictions often have forced heirship or inheritance rules that present a legal obligation for at least some portion of the estate of the deceased to be distributed to specific family members (like a spouse or children) regardless of the relationship preceding death, for the benefit of spouses and children. Shari’a law under Islam is similar in this respect to civil law. Forced heirship rules trump whatever is established in the decedent’s will. Housing assets in a common law country through a mechanism like an offshore trust is one way to avoid these rules, but some jurisdictions may “claw back” amounts that force lifetime gifts back into the estate.

In addition, the surviving spouse (or children) may, by law, receive the greater of the amount dictated by community property laws or forced heirship rules. A community property regime gives each spouse a one-half interest in specific assets and income earned during a marriage. Anything received before or after the marriage may be considered separate property.

A separate property regime (seen often in civil law jurisdictions) gives ownership and control to individuals within the marriage, which means each can dispose of his or her assets per their wishes.

## Inheritances

Even under community property rules, an inheritance received by one spouse before the marriage is typically considered separate property, and it would not be divided if the marriage should dissolve. If the inheritance occurs during marriage, community property rules would apply in those jurisdictions, and the inheritance would be considered jointly owned—and, therefore, divided evenly upon divorce.

186
Exceptions may be made if an inheritance received by one spouse is not commingled with community property funds. In other words, keeping it in a separate bank account in the name of the recipient spouse allows it to be considered separate.

In separate property jurisdictions, inheritances during the marriage generally remain separate as long as the funds are not commingled or used for joint expenses, the inheritance is not used to acquire more assets or invested such that its value increases, and the non-owning spouse did not contribute significant effort toward asset growth (transmutation). The specific laws may vary from one jurisdiction to another.

## Components of Estate Planning

Estate planning is the work involved during an individual's lifetime in preparing to dispose of their property when they die. Legal, tax, and financial professionals are often needed for consultation.

When an individual dies, their estate (all assets controlled and owned by a person before their death) will be distributed in some form. In a common law jurisdiction, they will have full freedom of disposal, and their testamentary documents (such as a will or trust) will take effect on their death. Dying without a will (intestate) means their assets will be distributed based on the laws where they live.

Individuals often try to avoid probate, which can be expensive and time-consuming. Owning assets in forms such as retirement plans, living trusts, or joint ownership can reduce or eliminate the need for probate.

In a joint tenants with right of survivorship (JTWROS) asset holding, multiple individuals own property together in equal shares. Each owner's interest is considered undivided, and on the death of one owner, the deceased's interest transfers automatically to the other owner or owners without going through probate. If, before death, one of the owners transfers or sells their interest (permission is not needed), the JTWROS status ends.

A testamentary gratuitous transfer is any bequest or asset transfer made on one's death. Drivers of taxation on these transfers include the type of asset, the location, and where the donor and donee reside. The jurisdiction drives who is responsible for any taxes owed. An estate tax is one owed by the donor or their estate, while an inheritance tax is one owed by the donee. The amount of wealth transferred, the impact of any allowance deductions, and the relationship between the donor and donee all drive the tax status. Some countries, instead of levying estate or inheritance taxes, consider a bequest to be a deemed disposition and apply a capital gains tax only to the increase in principal value, treating a bequest like the sale of a property.

## EXAMPLE: Estate taxes payable, flat tax rate

Liam Campbell and his wife, Maria, have an estate worth $36 million. In the country where the Campbells live, the estate tax threshold is $13.6 million per individual, and a flat 40% tax rate applies. What is the estate tax payable for the Campbells' estate?

187
# Answer:

The excess of the estate's value over the threshold is equal to $36 million - ($ 13.6 million × 2) = $ 8.8 million. $8.8 million × 40% = $ 3.52 million owed.

# EXAMPLE: Estate taxes payable, progressive tax rates

Liam Campbell and his wife, Maria, have an estate worth $36 million. In the country where the Campbells live, the estate tax threshold is $13.6 million per individual, and progressive tax rates apply with the following thresholds:

| Taxable Amount | Rate | Payable |
| --- | --- | --- |
| $500,000–$749,999 | 37% | $155,800 base + 37% taxable amount |
| $750,000–$999,999 | 39% | $248,300 base + 39% on taxable amount |
| $1,000,000 + | 40% | $345,800 base + 40% on taxable amount |

What is the estate tax payable for the Campbells' estate?

# Answer:

The excess of the estate's value over the threshold is equal to $36 million - ($ 13.6 million × 2) = $ 8.8 million. $345,800 + [($ 8,800,000 - $1,000,000) × 40%] = $ 3,465,800 (approximately).

# The Structure of an Estate Plan

The goals of an estate plan should be to meet the decedent's wealth transfer wishes while minimizing conflict and taxes. Three primary tools used to accomplish these goals are lifetime estate tax exclusions or exemptions, marital deductions or exemptions, and charitable gratuitous transfers:

- Lifetime estate tax exclusions or exemptions. In the previous examples, the estate tax threshold was given as $13.6 million per individual. If one's estate is below this threshold, no estate taxes or inheritance taxes will be owed. Trusts may be established if an estate's value is above this threshold. Portability is a concept that allows the surviving spouse to use any unused exemption amount from the deceased.
- Marital deductions or exemptions. This is where an unlimited marital deduction exists for any bequests made to a spouse. Tax payments are not eliminated; they are simply postponed until the death of the second spouse. To maximize this advantage, an estate should be divided into one trust that uses the marital exemption (called the exemption trust) and another trust that protects the exempt amount from taxation when the second spouse dies (called the marital deduction trust).

- The amount of assets that are placed into the exemption trust upon the death of the first spouse will vary depending on the actual exemption amount set by regulation. These assets could be used for the benefit of the surviving spouse and children. Upon the death of the surviving spouse, the remaining assets will remain in further trust for any beneficiaries without being subject to estate tax. The marital deduction trust would include the amount of assets in the decedent's estate that exceed the exemption amount. By creating these two trusts, the future appreciation on the exempt
amount is removed from the surviving spouse's estate. The net benefit of this mechanism is the tax that would have been paid on the appreciation amount. For this to be beneficial, the assets must appreciate (rather than depreciate) in value.

- Charitable gratuitous transfers. Two common forms of tax relief offered for transfers to charitable organizations and not-for-profits include exemptions from gift or estate tax for charitable donations and income tax deductions for charitable gifts. Although they sound similar, there are differences between bequests and charitable gifts. The following equation is used to determine the relative after-tax value (after a period of $n$ years) of a charitable gift versus a taxable bequest:


\mathrm{RV}_{\text{charitable gift}} = \frac{\mathrm{FV}_{\text{charitable gift}}}{\mathrm{FV}_{\text{bequest}}} = \frac{(1 + r_{\mathrm{g}})^n + T_{\mathrm{oi}}[1 + r_{\mathrm{e}}(1 - t_{\mathrm{ie}})]^n(1 - T_{\mathrm{e}})}{[1 + r_{\mathrm{e}}(1 - t_{\mathrm{ie}})]^n(1 - T_{\mathrm{e}})}


where:

- $r_{\mathrm{g}}$ = pretax returns to the recipient of the charitable gift
- $r_{\mathrm{e}}$ = pretax returns to the estate making the gift
- $t_{\mathrm{ie}}$ = effective tax rate on investment returns of the estate
- $T_{\mathrm{oi}}$ = current income tax benefit (ordinary rates) of the charitable transfer
- $T_{\mathrm{e}}$ = estate tax applicable if assets are bequeathed upon death
- $n$ = expected time until donor death

## EXAMPLE: Charitable donations vs. taxable bequests

A family lives in a country with estate taxes at $40\%$. The country does not apply inheritance taxes to charitable donations. An income tax rate of $34\%$ is applicable to investment income, but any charitable donations are eligible as tax deductions using the same $34\%$ rate. The family wishes to donate assets to a charity that is expected to earn $5\%$ (real return) annually over the next 15 years. Calculate the relative after-tax value of the charitable donation compared to the taxable bequest.

Answer:


\begin{array}{l}
\mathrm{RV}_{\text{charitable gift}} = \frac{\mathrm{FV}_{\text{charitable gift}}}{\mathrm{FV}_{\text{bequest}}} \\
= \frac{(1.05)^{15} + 0.34[1 + 0.05(1 - 0.34)]^{15}(1 - 0.40)}{[1 + 0.05(1 - 0.34)]^{15}(1 - 0.40)} \\
= 2.41093 / 0.97646 = 2.47 \\
\end{array}


The reason the charitable gift has almost 2.5 times the value of the bequest is because of the income tax deduction to the donor, the absence of the estate tax, and the investment returns growing tax free within a tax-exempt structure (the charity).

## Strategies for Wealth Transfers

With the goals of maximizing wealth transfers and minimizing taxes and conflicts, an overall strategy for wealth transfers should combine lifetime gifting and transfers at death. Decisions include how much to leave to descendants while the donor is alive, retaining sufficient assets for the needs of the donor, and then at death, ensuring that
the gifts are fully funded and leaving assets to the spouse to qualify for the marital deduction. A charitable entity can be chosen to receive the assets on the death of the second spouse to avoid transfer taxes.

Maximizing estate tax exemption amounts is critical. Valuation discounts and specialized trusts may also reduce overall transfer taxes. One such specialized trust is the grantor retained annuity trust (GRAT), where the donor gifting to the trust must retain an annual annuity, which reduces the taxable value of the gift. A unique benefit is if the assets in the trust appreciate faster than the assumed rate, the excess value can be transferred without any gift taxes.

Conflicts often arise among beneficiaries of an estate. While communication among all parties is critical, sometimes no-contest clauses may be needed to prevent conflict by disinheriting beneficiaries who may challenge the estate plan. Beneficiaries (the rising generation) must understand the wishes and reasons for the decisions made by the individual with the plan (the elder generation), as well as to provide input as needed. One area of concern is blended families, where stepparents and stepchildren may become potential beneficiaries. They should be clear on whether they will receive a share of a deceased individual's estate.

## MODULE QUIZ W7.2

1. A married couple has an estate currently worth $16 million. They live in a country with a flat estate tax rate of 35%, and the estate tax threshold per individual is $12 million. Given the value of the estate today, the tax payable for the estate is closest to:
A. $0.
B. $1.4 million.
C. $5.6 million.

2. Two individuals have bank accounts worth $2 million set up in both of their names, with JTWROS noted in the title. Which of the following statements is most accurate regarding this structure?
A. Probate will be needed to distribute the funds upon one owner's death.
B. Permission is needed if one individual wishes to transfer his or her interest.
C. The funds in the bank account are owned by each individual in equal shares.

## MODULE W7.3: PHILANTHROPY AND CHARITIES

Video covering this content is available online.

LOS W7.c: Discuss and recommend appropriate wealth management planning approaches for the preservation of wealth across multiple generations through charitable giving and philanthropy.

For many high-net-worth individuals, philanthropy represents a significant portion of their wealth transferred during their lifetimes and on death. The Giving Pledge is a collective movement where the wealthy commit most of their wealth to charitable organizations and efforts. Advisors must understand what charitable activities a client is involved in as well as how and where their funds are currently being donated.
Each individual client will have their own goals and reasons for gifting to charity. Mission-related investing, for example, is where clients gift to charities with the goal of bringing about positive social or environmental changes.

Risk assessments are critical because some charities present more risk than others that are more established. Determining the client's focus area comes from understanding their values, motivations, legacy desires, and risk tolerance. Advisors should ask clients what motivates them, what their short-term and long-term goals are, what they wish to achieve, what changes they would like to make, and what things would they like to preserve. Values such as education, justice, or social responsibility should also be identified.

Much like with a company, a mission statement or focus statement can be developed to combine a client's values and motivations and to clarify how giving can help address these areas.

## Working With Others

Many high-net-worth donors like to collaborate with other individuals who share a similar focus and mission. Ways to collaborate with others may include the following:

- Knowledge exchange. This is learning from others through affinity groups.
- Foundations. These are not-for-profit organizations that grant funds to other individuals and organizations that pursue educational, social, and charitable activities. Collaboration can occur with three primary types of foundations:

1. Private grant-making (non-operating) foundation. Individual donors or donor families establish these to support specific types of charities. The goal is to maintain purchasing power so grants can continue in perpetuity.
2. Private operating foundation. These exist to operate a not-for-profit business benefitting a charitable cause. They support and fund related activities and programs, rather than giving grants.
3. Community foundation. This is a public charity pooling donor funds and focused on a specific community. These are often associated with churches, schools, or hospitals, and their grants are often educational or social.

- Coordinating strategies. This is coordinating funding to back other donors supporting similar causes.
- Aggregating funds. This is pooling donated funds with other donors to have a greater impact on the recipient charity or cause.
- Community collaborations. This is working with the community that the donor intends to support and fund.

## Working With Family

Involving family in philanthropic efforts can help to educate them on investment and financial matters. Working together as a family can also provide valuable bonding opportunities and positive feelings over collaborating to benefit good causes. Family members can serve as co-philanthropists who help set priorities, establish mission or focus statements, and maintain involvement throughout the process. A family member
may also function as an advisor to provide feedback to the donor, or they can lead or implement the goals.

# Methods for Gifting

Figure W7.1 summarizes several ways of gifting.

Figure W7.1: Charitable Giving Vehicles

| Element | Donor-Advised Fund (DAF) | Private Grant-Making Foundation | Limited Liability Company (LLC) |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Distributions | No required annual distributions | Must pay out ≥ 5% of asset value annually | No distribution requirements |
| Taxes | Immediate deduction and no capital gains taxes | Contribution deductions and excise tax on net investment income | Entity receives no tax benefits, but donors may receive tax advantages |
| Asset and investment decisions | Donors can advise on investment strategies | Full discretion on investment strategies and decisions | Full control of investments and assets |
| Grantmaking | Donors have advisory rights, but sponsoring organization has legal control | Significant ability to direct program operations and control granting | Donors/owners have freedom to make charitable decisions |
| Costs of administration | Relatively low | Relatively high | Can be costly |
| Flexibility | Significant flexibility to support many charities | Significant operational flexibility to fund outside programs or support own operations | Highest level of operational flexibility |
| Control and involvement | Donor serves as an advisor, but does not have legal control | Donors/families have active role in governance and management | Members of the LLC have direct operational and management control |
| Privacy | Anonymous gifts allowed | Public disclosure requirements mean privacy is limited | Significant privacy |
| Legacy | Family members can serve as advisors for generations | Family members can serve as advisors/ managers for generations | Not specifically designed for generational management |

A direct gift to charity (often in cash) is a popular method of gifting that may provide income tax deductions. Gifting appreciated securities such as stocks at fair market value is also popular. A qualified charitable distribution (in the United States) allows an individual to gift up to $100,000 through a required minimum distribution from a retirement plan.

Donor-advised funds (DAFs) are philanthropic funds managed and administered by a not-for-profit institution that allows donors to make contributions (cash, securities, or
other assets), receive tax deductions for the donation (either in a specific year or allocated over many years), and recommend grants to other charities such as publicly supported charitable organizations (which receive at least one-third of their support from the public). While legal control of donated funds rests with the sponsoring organization that manages the assets, they typically abide by donor recommendations. Successor advisors within a family may be established to continue the work after the original donor's death.

A private grantmaking foundation is a legal entity that exists for a charitable purpose. A donor creates the foundation by funding it with assets and benefits from a tax deduction for the contributions (but not for grants made subsequently). Certain requirements may need to be met to remain tax exempt, such as in the United States, where at least 5% of annual assets must be distributed to charitable aims. A board of directors (with at least three people) must be established to manage all operations, including awarding grants and managing assets, and it exercises autonomy over philanthropic activities and investment strategies. Limited-life foundations exist for a fixed period, while other foundations may exist into perpetuity. Some donors may use both private grantmaking foundations and DAFs because the latter may not have stringent distribution requirements and may allow for anonymous gifting.

Limited liability companies (LLCs) can be used to limit owners' liability against lawsuits. These have no public filing or annual distribution requirements. While no charitable tax deductions are available to the donor on funding, charitable contributions made from the LLC are deductible. The owners manage the LLC, and direct contributions and donations.

Planned giving represents gifting to charities via an estate plan. A charitable remainder trust (CRT), available in the United States, is an irrevocable tax-exempt trust consisting of two parts: (1) an income stream to the donor or other beneficiaries, and (2) a commitment of the remainder to a future gift to charity. The investment and distribution of trust assets is managed by a trustee, who may be the original donor, another individual, or a financial institution. The annual income payouts come from dividends and interest and must be at least 5% of trust assets. A charitable remainder annuity trust (CRAT) will pay out a fixed amount, while a charitable remainder unitrust (CRUT) can be used to pay out variable amounts based on fixed trust value percentages. No gift tax is generated from the creation of a CRT if the donor or spouse is the initial beneficiary and is a U.S. citizen. An income tax deduction equivalent to the remainder value (to be distributed to the charity or charities, DAF, or private foundation) is available in the year of establishment.

Advantages of a CRT include the tax benefits, regular income for beneficiaries, removal of the assets from the estate of the donor, and the impact to charities. Disadvantages include its irrevocability, inflation risk, investment risk, and administrative costs.

A non-grantor charitable lead trust (CLT) is an irrevocable trust paying out in the opposite way to a CRT. The current beneficiary is a charity or charities, while the remainder beneficiaries are the donor's heirs or other non-charity beneficiaries. The charities receiving benefits will do so over a fixed period, with no minimum required distributions. At the end of the trust term, the remainder is distributed to the donor's

193
heirs or non-charitable beneficiaries. A zeroed-out charitable lead annuity trust (CLAT) produces an annuity large enough to reduce the remainder value to zero based on actuarial calculations. Any amounts that do remain will pass tax free.

Advantages of a CLT include estate tax only being payable on the remainder, income tax deductions based on charitable distributions, predictable benefits to the charity or charities, tax deductions for the trust for annual payments made to the charity, and wealth transfers across generations. Disadvantages include no tax benefit for initial contributions by the donor, the cost and complication of establishing the trust, market risk for the heirs to the CLT, and the long-term commitment of funds.

## Cross-Border Philanthropy

When a donor is considering gifting outside their home country, the following points must be considered in addition to the issues raised earlier:

- Accountability. The donor expects the recipient of their funds to be operationally and fiscally accountable for those funds. Understanding the type of entity (domestic-based organization, intermediary organization, local not-for-profit) responsible for stewardship of the funds is critical. Some funds may be provided during emergencies, but the donor and donee must communicate about funding after the crisis.
- Risk mitigation. Funds used for illegitimate activities, terrorist activities, or for laundering money are important risks to monitor. The PATRIOT Act (2001) was put in place to prevent funding terrorism.
- Cross-cultural competency. Because of differences in cultures, principles, and ethics, donors wishing to gift outside their country may find it better to do so through another party, like an international aid organization.

There are rules and regulations regarding grants made by foundations. Two key rules relate to the U.S. Internal Revenue Code and charitable contributions:

- Equivalency determination. This refers to whether the tax authorities consider a donee outside the United States to be equivalent to a public charity in the United States.
- Expenditure responsibility. This describes whether a non-U.S. charity will use the grant funds appropriately. Requirements here include (1) an analysis done before the grant to review how the money is intended to be spent; (2) a grant agreement in writing that specifies how the funds will be spent; (3) reports from the donee as to how the money is being spent; and (4) filings to the U.S. tax authorities.

## Non-U.S. Charitable Giving in Common Law and Civil Law Jurisdictions

Common law jurisdictions like the United Kingdom, Singapore, and Hong Kong are similar to the United States in terms of charitable giving. Standard structures include donor-transferred assets, a trustee, and income and capital gains distributed as gifts. Tax exemptions are usually available for income and capital gains, while donations are often tax deductible for the donor. Foundations are also common in these jurisdictions.

194
Similar to charitable trusts, foundations may offer tax exemptions on income and tax deductions for donors.

In civil law jurisdictions, trusts are often not recognized. The Hague Trust Convention includes 14 countries that formally recognize trusts. For charitable gifting vehicles in civil law jurisdictions, the following options may also be used:

- **Fondation** (used in Belgium, France, and Luxembourg). This is an independent entity without owners or shareholders, and it is dedicated to philanthropy. One or more founders provide the capital, which can give them tax deductions. The Fondation itself is also typically tax exempt. While the benefits include the tax advantages, control over fund usage, and positive public recognition, they can also be heavily scrutinized, be complex and costly, and may lack flexibility.

- **Stiftung** (used in Austria, Switzerland, Germany, and Liechtenstein). This is a foundation used to manage assets for a specific educational, scientific, cultural, or charitable purpose. The founder (donor) provides the assets to be managed by the foundation for income generation. These are typically tax-exempt organizations where tax benefits are received for the initial and subsequent contributions. While these foundations are designed to be long lived and provide autonomy and efficiency, they are heavily scrutinized, inflexible, and often require significant up-front capital.

- **Fideicomiso** (used in Latin America). This is an entity functioning much like a trust where assets are held and managed for specific purposes. Assets are transferred from a settlor to a fiduciary tasked with managing them for the benefit of a third party. These assets are usually excluded from the settlor's estate, and income can be tax exempt. The estate planning benefit can be significant, as can the targeted purpose of the entity. However, these structures are often not used outside of Latin America and can be legally challenging to establish.

- **Charitable incorporated organizations** (used in Japan). These are incorporated entities set up to achieve nonprofit goals such as education, environmental protection, and social welfare. The government approves their creation, and a board of trustees or directors is set up to manage the entity. Tax exemptions and tax deductions for the donor are beneficial, as is the support from the government. However, these may not allow changes to be made from their original purpose, and maintaining their legal status can be challenging.

For wealthy individuals in Asia and Europe, the common themes that come into play when choosing vehicles for philanthropy are tax considerations, cultural factors, the regulatory environment, and control versus flexibility.

2. A client wishes to contribute funds to a trust from which she will receive fixed distributions each year until her death, when the remainder will revert to her favorite charity. Which of the following structures should her advisor most

```markdown
appropriately recommend?

B. A charitable remainder annuity trust (CRAT).

# KEY CONCEPTS

## LOS W7.a

A gift is made between a donor (the individual making the gift) and a donee (the individual receiving the gift). A lifetime gift is also known as an inter vivos transfer. Gifts may be assessed a transfer tax.

An estate tax is paid by the transferor, and an inheritance tax is paid by the recipient. Gifts can reduce the impact of both taxes by decreasing the value of future wealth transfers. Another way to reduce transfer taxes is to gift assets that are eligible for valuation discounts.

A trust stems from common law and is an arrangement often used in estate planning. A trust may be revocable or irrevocable. A fixed trust has specific beneficiary distributions. A discretionary trust allows the trustee to determine the amount and timing of distributions. Trusts can be set to last for a period of years, or forever (perpetuity).

Generation skipping is designed to gift assets to the third generation and beyond while minimizing the taxation.

An inter vivos trust is an irrevocable trust created and funded by the grantor when he or she is alive. A testamentary trust is created from the estate plan of someone who is deceased. An intentionally defective grantor trust is an irrevocable trust (in the United States) that removes the gifted assets from the grantor's estate, but it still requires the grantor to pay income taxes on the assets. An offshore trust is created outside an individual's home jurisdiction.

Trusts may be created to avoid forced inheritance rules, where children or others are legally obligated to receive some of the decedent's assets.

The prudent investor rule dictates how trustees must invest trust assets.

Objectives to consider in gifting and estate planning include control over assets, income and liquidity, charitable goals, tax awareness, asset protection, business succession, and preservation of family wealth.

## LOS W7.b

A bequest directs what will be done with the assets of the testator (the will creator) when they die. Having no valid will is called intestacy, in which case courts decide how the assets will be disposed.

A testamentary plan is the formal structure for how an individual's wealth will be transferred on death. Common law and civil law differences affect the structure of the plan. Shari'a law is similar to civil law.
Forced heirship rules require at least some portion of an estate to be distributed to specific family members.

A community property regime gives each spouse a one-half interest in specific assets and income earned during a marriage. Anything received before or after the marriage may be considered separate property. A separate property regime gives ownership and control to individuals within the marriage.

Estate planning is the work involved during an individual’s lifetime in preparing to dispose of their property when they die. In a common law jurisdiction, they have full freedom of disposal, and their testamentary documents (such as a will or trust) take effect at their death. Owning assets in forms such as retirement plans, living trusts, and joint ownership can reduce or eliminate the need for probate, which can be expensive and time-consuming.

Joint tenants with right of survivorship (JTWROS) is a vehicle in which multiple individuals own assets together in equal shares. On the death of one owner, the deceased’s interest automatically transfers to the other owner(s).

A testamentary gratuitous transfer is any bequest or asset transfer made on one’s death. Drivers of taxation on these transfers include the type of asset, the location, and where the donor and donee live.

Goals of an estate plan should be to meet the decedent’s wealth transfer wishes with minimal conflict and taxes. Tools to accomplish these goals include lifetime estate tax exclusions or exemptions, marital deductions or exemptions, and charitable gratuitous transfers.

With a grantor retained annuity trust (GRAT), the donor gifting to the trust must retain an annual annuity. This reduces the value of the gift for tax purposes.

## LOS W7.c

Advisors must understand what charitable activities a client is involved with and how and where their funds are being donated. Determining the client’s focus area comes from understanding their values, motivations, legacy desires, and risk tolerance.

Mission-related investing is where clients gift to charities wishing to effect positive social or environmental changes.

Much like with a company, a mission statement (or focus statement) can be developed to combine a client’s values and motivations and how giving can help address these areas.

High-net-worth individuals may collaborate with others who share a similar focus and mission through knowledge exchange, coordinating strategies, aggregating funds, community collaborations, and foundations. Foundations can be private grantmaking (non-operating), private operating, and community foundations.

Ways of gifting include outright gifts to charity, donor-advised funds (DAFs), private grantmaking foundations, limited liability companies (LLCs), and planned giving.

197
Charitable remainder trusts (CRTs) are tax-exempt trusts consisting of an income stream to the donor or other beneficiaries and a commitment of the remainder to a future gift to charity. A charitable remainder annuity trust (CRAT) will pay out a fixed amount, while a charitable remainder unitrust (CRUT) can pay out variable amounts. Advantages of a CRT include tax benefits, regular income for beneficiaries, removal of the assets from the estate, and the impact to charities. Disadvantages include irrevocability, inflation risk, investment risk, and administrative costs.

Non-grantor charitable lead trusts (CLTs) are irrevocable trusts paying out in the opposite way to a CRT. The current beneficiary is a charity, while the remainder beneficiaries are the donor's heirs or other non-charity beneficiaries. A zeroed-out CLAT produces an annuity large enough to reduce the remainder value to zero. Advantages of a CLT include the estate tax only being payable on the remainder, income tax deductions based on charitable distributions, predictable benefits to the charity, tax deductions for the trust, and wealth transfers across generations. Disadvantages include no tax benefit for initial contributions by the donor, the cost to establish, market risk for the heirs, and long-term commitment of funds.

When donors are considering gifting outside of their home country, they must consider accountability, risk mitigation, and cross-cultural competency. Equivalency determination is the tax authorities' view of whether a donee outside of the United States is equivalent to a public charity in the United States. Expenditure responsibility is whether a non-U.S. charity will use the grant funds appropriately.

In civil law jurisdictions, trusts are often not recognized. The Hague Trust Convention includes 14 countries that recognize trusts.

## ANSWER KEY FOR MODULE QUIZZES

### Module Quiz W7.1

1. B The assets gifted to an intentionally defective grantor trust are removed from the client's estate. While the assets themselves will grow in the trust without a tax impact, the grantor still has to pay the income taxes on the appreciation of the assets. The trust is irrevocable. (LOS W7.a)
2. B The combination of the illiquidity discount and the lack of control discount will equate to a reduction in fair market value of $[(1 - 0.15) \times (1 - 0.20)] - 1 = 0.68 - 1 = 0.32$. (LOS W7.a)

### Module Quiz W7.2

1. A As a married couple, each spouse has an exemption of $12 million. The total estate value of $16 million falls below the combined exemption of $24 million. As such, there will be no estate tax owed based on the value of the estate (and the exemption threshold) today. (LOS W7.b)
2. C JTWROS stands for joint tenants with rights of survivorship. This structure allows two or more individuals to own assets together in equal shares, and it allows the

198
assets to transfer to one individual after the death of the other without going through probate. No permission is needed from other parties if one party wishes to transfer or sell their interest. (LOS W7.b)

## Module Quiz W7.3

1. C While DAFs and LLCs do not have annual distribution requirements, a private grantmaking foundation must pay out at least 5% of its asset value annually. If the donor wants to minimize these requirements, they should avoid using this vehicle. (LOS W7.c)

2. B CRATs provide an income stream to the donor or other beneficiaries first, and the charity later. In addition, a CRAT pays out a fixed distribution each year. Both CLTs and CLATs benefit the charity first (with distributions) and beneficiaries later. (LOS W7.c)

# TOPIC QUIZ: PRIVATE WEALTH

You have now finished the Private Wealth topic section. On your Schweser online dashboard, you can find a Topic Quiz that will provide immediate feedback on how effective your study of this material has been. The test is best taken timed; allow three minutes per question. Topic Quizzes are more exam-like than typical QBank questions or module quiz questions. A score less than 70% suggests that additional review of the topics is needed.
FORMULAS

expected annual return of HY bonds = $[(1 - \mathrm{PD}) \times \mathrm{YTM}] - [\mathrm{PD} \times \mathrm{LGD}]$

where:

$\mathrm{PD} =$ annual probability of default of the HY bonds in the portfolio

$\mathrm{YTM} =$ yield to maturity of the HY bonds

$\mathrm{LGD} =$ loss given default (expressed as a positive number)

expected return = forward dividend yield + growth of dividends

## Portfolio Utility

utility = portfolio expected return $-\frac{\gamma}{2} \times$ portfolio variance

where:

$\gamma =$ client's coefficient of risk aversion (a number typically between 1 and 10, where 10 is high risk aversion)

## Portfolio Utility for Taxable Investors


\text{utility} = \text{pretax expected return} \times (1 - \mathrm{tx}) - \frac{\gamma}{2} \times \text{pretax variance} \times (1 - \mathrm{tx})^2

= (1 - \mathrm{tx}) \left[ \text{pretax expected return} - \frac{\gamma}{2} \times \text{pretax variance} \times (1 - \mathrm{tx}) \right]


funding ratio = $\frac{\text{assets}}{\text{liabilities}}$

funding ratio return = $\frac{\text{funding ratio}_{\text{FND}}}{\text{funding ratio}_{\text{START}}} - 1$

funding ratio return = $\frac{\text{asset return} - \text{liability return}}{1 + \text{liability return}}$

## Annuity Payment


\text{initial payment} = \frac{\text{lump sum} \times (E[r] - g)}{1 - \left(\frac{1 + g}{1 + E[r]}\right)^N}


where:

initial payment = first annuity payment to the investor at the end of the first year

lump sum = price of the annuity, paid at the start of the first year by the investor

$E[r] =$ expected return on the invested lump sum (effectively a discount rate applied to annuity payments)

When growth rate $g$ is equal to the required return $E[r]$
\text{initial payment} = \frac{\text{lump sum} \times (1 + E[r])}{N}


Performance measures based on return
Arithmetic active return for the period $R_p - R_E$

Excess return $R_P - R_f$

Logarithmic active return $ln(1 + R_P) - \ln(1 + R_E)$

Logarithmic excess return $ln(1 + R_P) - \ln(1 + R_f)$

logarithmic Sharpe ratio $=\frac{\text{mean}(\text{excess logarithmic returns})}{\text{standard deviation}(\text{excess logarithmic return})}$

logarithmic information ratio $=\frac{\text{mean}(\text{active logarithmic returns})}{\text{standard deviation}(\text{active logarithmic return})}$

Sortino ratio $=\frac{\text{mean}(\text{return}) - \text{MAR}}{\text{semideviation}(\text{return} - \text{MAR})}$

logarithmic Sortino ratio $=\frac{\text{mean}(\text{logarithmic return}) - (\text{logarithmic MAR})}{\text{semideviation}(\text{logarithmic return} - \text{logarithmic MAR})}$

Brinson-Hood-Beebower (or accounting-based) attribution model:

allocation effect $= \sum$ (active sector overweight or underweight $\times$ benchmark sector or asset class return)

selection effect $= \sum$ (weight of benchmark $\times$ active sector or asset class return)

interaction effect $= \sum$ (active sector overweight or underweight $\times$ active sector or asset class return)

$t = \frac{\text{sample mean}}{\text{sample standard deviation}} \times \sqrt{N}t = \frac{\text{sample mean} - 0}{s / \sqrt{N}}$

Survival probability

$p(\text{survival}) = p(\text{husband survives}) + p(\text{wife survives}) - [p(\text{husband survives}) \times p(\text{wife survives})]$

Present value of the spending need

PV(spending need) $= \sum_{j=1}^{N} \frac{p(\text{survival}_j) \times \text{spending}_j}{(1 + r)^j}$

Human life value $= \sum_{j=1}^{B} \frac{\text{income}(1 - t_{\text{emp}}) + \text{benefits} - \text{expense}}{(1 - t_{\text{ins}})} (1 + g)^j$

Nominal growth of an investment goal

$R_{\text{goal},t} = \alpha_0 + \beta_{\text{inf,goal}} \pi_t + \varepsilon_t$

where:

$R =$ return (nominal growth) of the investment goal

$t =$ investment period

$\beta_{\text{inf,goal}} =$ inflation factor beta of investment goal

$\pi =$ inflation (expected and unexpected)

$\varepsilon =$ error term

Inflation factor beta

202

\beta_{\inf, \text{goal}} = \rho \frac{\sigma_{\text{goal}}}{\sigma_{\inf}}


where:
$\rho$ = correlation coefficient between inflation and the investment goal return
$\sigma$ = relative volatilities for inflation and the goal, respectively

## Inflation hedge ratio


H_{\inf} = \left( \frac{\beta_{\text{target}} - \beta_{\inf, \text{goal}}}{\beta_{\inf, \text{hedge}}} \right) \left( \frac{\text{goal}}{\text{hedge}} \right)

H_{\inf} = - \left( \frac{\beta_{\inf, \text{goal}}}{\beta_{\inf, \text{hedge}}} \right) \left( \frac{\text{goal}}{\text{hedge}} \right)


where:
$\beta_{\text{target}}$ = desired amount of inflation exposure
$\text{goal}$ = amount (nominal) of investment goal to be hedged
$\text{hedge}$ = amount of the hedging asset

## After-tax future value for investments taxed using the capital gains rate


FVI_{cgb} = (1 + r)^n (1 - t_{cg}) + t_{cg} B


where:
$FVI_{cgb}$ = future value interest factor
$r$ = nominal pretax return
$n$ = number of periods
$t_{cg}$ = capital gains tax rate
$B$ = cost basis as a proportion of current market value

## Net asset decline = return$_{\text{base currency}}$ (asset$_{\text{base currency}}$ - liabilities$_{\text{base currency}}$)

where:
$\text{return}_{\text{base currency}}$ = percentage change in the exchange rate of the base currency
$\text{expressed in terms of the price currency, } \left( \frac{S_{t, \text{price/base}}}{S_{t-1, \text{price/base}}} - 1 \right)\text{asset}_{\text{base currency}}$ = amount of assets denominated in the base currency in terms of the price currency
$\text{liabilities}_{\text{base currency}}$ = amount of liabilities in the base currency, expressed in terms of the price currency

## Relative value


RV = \frac{FV \text{ after-tax to the receiver if gifted now}}{FV \text{ after-tax to the receiver if bequeathed at death}}


## Relative value ratios

1. RV of a tax-free gift, $T_g = 0$:

RV_{\text{tax-free gift}} = \frac{ \left[ 1 + r_g (1 - t_{ig}) \right]^n }{ \left[ 1 + r_e (1 - t_{ie}) \right]^n (1 - T_e)}


2. RV of a taxable gift, $T_g$ paid by receiver:

RV_{\text{taxable gift}} = \frac{ \left[ 1 + r_g (1 - t_{ig}) \right]^n (1 - T_g) }{ \left[ 1 + r_e (1 - t_{ie}) \right]^n (1 - T_e)}


3. RV of a taxable gift, $T_g$ paid by giver:

RV_{\text{taxable gift}} = \frac{ \left[ 1 + r_g (1 - t_{ig}) \right]^n (1 - T_g + T_g T_e) }{ \left[ 1 + r_e (1 - t_{ie}) \right]^n (1 - T_e)}

Relative after-tax value (after a period of $n$ years) of a charitable gift versus a taxable bequest


\mathrm{RV}_{\text{charitable gift}} = \frac{\mathrm{FV}_{\text{charitable gift}}}{\mathrm{FV}_{\text{bequest}}} = \frac{(1 + \mathrm{r_g})^{\mathrm{n}} + \mathrm{T_{oi}}[1 + \mathrm{r_e}(1 - \mathrm{t_{ie}})]^{\mathrm{n}}(1 - \mathrm{T_e})}{[1 + \mathrm{r_e}(1 - \mathrm{t_{ie}})]^{\mathrm{n}}(1 - \mathrm{T_e})}


where:

- $\mathrm{r_g} =$ pretax returns to the recipient of the charitable gift
- $\mathrm{r_e} =$ pretax returns to the estate making the gift
- $\mathrm{t_{ie}} =$ effective tax rate on investment returns of the estate
- $\mathrm{T_{oi}} =$ current income tax benefit (ordinary rates) of the charitable transfer
- $\mathrm{T_e} =$ estate tax applicable if assets are bequeathed upon death
- $\mathrm{n} =$ expected time until donor death
